Part II — Section 4

BigQuery Storage

Capacitor format, partitioning, clustering, nested/repeated fields, and storage pricing — the highest-leverage optimization surface in BigQuery.

Section 4 — BigQuery Storage — Partitioning, Clustering, Nested/Repeated Fields

4.1 Why Storage Optimization Is the #1 Priority

BigQuery storage optimization is the highest-leverage skill for controlling both cost and performance. Proper partitioning, clustering, and data modeling can reduce query costs by 100× or more — transforming a $62.50 full-table scan into a $0.06 partition-and-cluster-pruned query. No other optimization (slot tuning, materialized views, BI Engine) comes close to this impact.

This section covers every storage mechanism in depth: partitioning (the first line of defense), clustering (the second line), nested/repeated fields (the BigQuery-native data model), and storage pricing (active, long-term, physical, time travel). Each subsection includes production-ready SQL examples, cost calculations, and common pitfalls.

OptimizationMechanismTypical ImpactEffort
PartitioningSkip entire date ranges (partition pruning)10–365× less scanDDL change (table creation)
ClusteringSkip Capacitor files via min/max stats2–100× less scanDDL change (table creation)
Column selectionColumnar format reads only referenced columns5–50× less I/OQuery change (drop SELECT *)
DenormalizationSTRUCT/ARRAY eliminates JOINs → no shuffle2–10× faster + cheaperSchema redesign
Storage billing modelPhysical billing for well-compressed data30–70% storage savingsALTER SCHEMA one-time

4.2 Partitioning — The First Line of Cost Defense

Partitioning divides a table into segments based on a column's value. When a query filters on the partition key, BigQuery skips entire partitions — reading zero bytes from irrelevant date ranges. This is called partition pruning and it's the most impactful single optimization available.

Partition Types

TypeSyntaxBest ForMax Partitions
Time-unit (DATE)PARTITION BY sale_dateEvent data, time-series, logs — most common4,000
Time-unit (MONTH)PARTITION BY DATE_TRUNC(ts, MONTH)Long-lived tables (>10 years → daily exceeds 4K limit)4,000
Time-unit (HOUR)PARTITION BY TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(ts, HOUR)High-volume streaming with hourly query patterns4,000
Integer rangePARTITION BY RANGE_BUCKET(age, ...)Numeric dimensions (age buckets, score ranges)10,000
Ingestion timePARTITION BY _PARTITIONDATEWhen data lacks a natural date column4,000
SQLpartitioning_examples.sql
-- ═══ TIME-UNIT PARTITIONING (most common — daily) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.sales`
(
    sale_id     INT64,
    sale_date   DATE,
    customer_id STRING,
    product_id  STRING,
    revenue     NUMERIC,
    country     STRING,
    category    STRING
)
PARTITION BY sale_date
CLUSTER BY country, category
OPTIONS (
    partition_expiration_days = 365,      -- Auto-delete old partitions
    require_partition_filter  = TRUE      -- CRITICAL: reject queries without date filter
);

-- ═══ MONTHLY PARTITIONING (very long-lived data) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.historical_events`
(
    event_id   INT64,
    event_ts   TIMESTAMP,
    event_type STRING,
    payload    JSON
)
PARTITION BY TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(event_ts, MONTH)
CLUSTER BY event_type;
-- 20 years of data = 240 monthly partitions (well under 4K limit)

-- ═══ INTEGER RANGE PARTITIONING ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.user_segments`
(
    user_id STRING,
    age     INT64,
    segment STRING,
    ltv     NUMERIC
)
PARTITION BY RANGE_BUCKET(age, GENERATE_ARRAY(0, 100, 10));
-- Creates partitions: [0,10), [10,20), [20,30), ... [90,100)

-- ═══ INGESTION-TIME PARTITIONING ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.raw_events`
(
    event_id STRING,
    data     JSON
)
PARTITION BY _PARTITIONDATE;
-- BigQuery auto-assigns partition based on ingestion timestamp
💰 Cost Tip — require_partition_filter Saves Fortunes Setting require_partition_filter = TRUE is the single most important cost control in BigQuery. Without it, a developer can accidentally run SELECT COUNT(*) FROM sales and scan the entire table — potentially terabytes. With it, BigQuery rejects any query missing a partition filter. On a 10 TB table at $6.25/TB on-demand, one accidental full scan = $62.50. Over a year with 100 analysts = thousands of dollars wasted. This setting costs zero and prevents the most common BigQuery cost mistake.

Partition Pruning — How It Works

Figure 4.1 — Partition pruning: a query filtering on sale_date reads only 1 of 365 partitions, reducing scan from 10 TB to 27 GB — a 365× cost reduction on on-demand pricing.

4.3 Clustering — Sorting Within Partitions

Clustering sorts and co-locates data within each partition by up to 4 specified columns. When queries filter on clustering columns, BigQuery uses min/max statistics per Capacitor file to skip entire files that cannot contain matching rows. This is called file-level pruning and it stacks on top of partition pruning for compound savings.

Clustering Rules and Best Practices

  • Up to 4 columns: Order matters — first column has strongest effect, second is clustered within blocks of first, etc.
  • Choose by filter frequency: Pick columns most frequently used in WHERE clauses
  • Higher cardinality first: customer_id (millions of values) gives more pruning than country (200 values) — BUT only if you filter on it frequently
  • Auto-reclustering: BigQuery automatically reclusters data in the background as new data is ingested — zero manual maintenance
  • Works on unpartitioned tables too: Clustering without partitioning is valid and still provides file-level pruning
Figure 4.2 — Clustering sorts data within partitions. BigQuery uses per-file min/max statistics to skip files that cannot contain matching rows — often reducing scan by 50–99%.
SQLclustering_strategies.sql
-- ═══ E-COMMERCE: partition by date, cluster by top-filtered columns ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.orders`
(
    order_id    INT64,
    order_date  DATE,
    customer_id STRING,
    country     STRING,
    category    STRING,
    revenue     NUMERIC
)
PARTITION BY order_date
CLUSTER BY country, category, customer_id;
-- Order: country (most filtered) → category → customer_id
-- WHERE country='US' AND category='electronics' → prunes heavily

-- ═══ LOG ANALYTICS: partition by day, cluster by severity + service ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.app_logs`
(
    log_id   STRING,
    log_time TIMESTAMP,
    severity STRING,     -- ERROR, WARN, INFO, DEBUG
    service  STRING,     -- payment-api, user-api
    message  STRING,
    trace_id STRING
)
PARTITION BY TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(log_time, DAY)
CLUSTER BY severity, service;
-- WHERE severity='ERROR' AND service='payment-api' → very efficient

-- ═══ VERIFY CLUSTERING BENEFIT: dry run ═══
-- In Console → "More" → "Dry run":
SELECT country, SUM(revenue)
FROM `project.dataset.orders`
WHERE order_date = '2024-03-15' AND country = 'US'
GROUP BY country;
-- Dry run shows estimated bytes → should be MUCH less than full partition

-- ═══ CHECK CLUSTERING EFFECTIVENESS via INFORMATION_SCHEMA ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    clustering_ordinal_position,
    column_name
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.COLUMNS`
WHERE clustering_ordinal_position IS NOT NULL
ORDER BY table_name, clustering_ordinal_position;
💰 Cost Tip — Partitioning + Clustering = 99.9% Reduction Consider a 10 TB orders table: 365 daily partitions (~27 GB/day), clustered by country. Query: WHERE order_date = '2024-03-15' AND country = 'US'. Step 1: partition prune → 27 GB (1 day). Step 2: cluster prune → ~5 GB (US only). Instead of scanning 10 TB ($62.50), scan 5 GB ($0.03). That's a 99.95% cost reduction. Always partition by date. Always cluster by your most-filtered columns. This is non-negotiable for production tables.

4.4 Nested and Repeated Fields — STRUCT and ARRAY

BigQuery's native support for STRUCT (nested fields) and ARRAY (repeated fields) enables a denormalized data model that eliminates JOINs — the "BigQuery way" of modeling data. This is fundamentally different from traditional star-schema design.

Why Denormalization Beats Star Schema in BigQuery

Figure 4.3 — BigQuery data modeling: denormalized tables with STRUCT (nested) and ARRAY (repeated) eliminate JOINs, avoiding expensive shuffle operations.
SQLstruct_array_patterns.sql
-- ═══ CREATE denormalized table with STRUCT + ARRAY ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
(
    order_id   INT64,
    order_date DATE,
    customer   STRUCT<
        id      STRING,
        name    STRING,
        address STRUCT<city STRING, state STRING, zip STRING>
    >,
    items ARRAY<STRUCT<
        product_id   STRING,
        product_name STRING,
        quantity     INT64,
        unit_price   NUMERIC
    >>,
    tags ARRAY<STRING>
)
PARTITION BY order_date
CLUSTER BY customer.address.state;

-- ═══ QUERY nested STRUCT fields (dot notation) ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    customer.name          AS customer_name,
    customer.address.city  AS city,
    customer.address.state AS state
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE order_date = '2024-03-15'
  AND customer.address.state = 'CA';

-- ═══ UNNEST arrays to access repeated elements ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    customer.name,
    item.product_name,
    item.quantity * item.unit_price AS line_total
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`,
UNNEST(items) AS item
WHERE order_date = '2024-03-15'
  AND item.quantity > 5;

-- ═══ BUILD denormalized table FROM normalized tables ═══
SELECT
    o.order_id,
    o.order_date,
    STRUCT(c.customer_id AS id, c.name, c.email) AS customer,
    ARRAY_AGG(
        STRUCT(i.product_name, i.quantity, i.unit_price)
    ) AS items
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id
JOIN order_items i ON o.order_id = i.order_id
GROUP BY o.order_id, o.order_date, c.customer_id, c.name, c.email;
-- Run this ONCE to denormalize, then query the denorm table going forward

-- ═══ ARRAY contains check ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE 'premium' IN UNNEST(tags);

-- ═══ Aggregate within array (correlated subquery) ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    (SELECT SUM(item.quantity * item.unit_price)
     FROM UNNEST(items) AS item) AS order_total
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`;

-- ═══ ARRAY_LENGTH — count items per order ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    ARRAY_LENGTH(items) AS num_items
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE ARRAY_LENGTH(items) > 10;
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — The BigQuery Modeling Rule In BigQuery, prefer denormalized tables with STRUCT and ARRAY over normalized star schemas. BigQuery's columnar storage handles wide tables efficiently — 50+ columns incur no penalty if unused columns aren't queried. Avoiding JOINs eliminates shuffle, saving both cost and latency. The rule: if two entities have a 1:1 or 1:many relationship and are always queried together, embed the child as a STRUCT (1:1) or ARRAY of STRUCTs (1:many). Only normalize when the entity is independently queried or updated separately with different frequency.
⚠️ Common Pitfall — When NOT to Denormalize Denormalization isn't always the answer: (1) If the nested data changes independently — e.g., customer address updates shouldn't require rewriting all their orders. (2) If the ARRAY is very large — a user with 10,000 order items creates a huge row that's expensive to scan. (3) If the nested entity is queried independently — e.g., "show me all products sold" doesn't need order context. In these cases, keep separate tables and JOIN when needed — but be aware of the shuffle cost.

4.5 Storage Pricing — Active, Long-Term, Physical, Time Travel

Storage TypePrice (US multi-region)TriggerNotes
Active logical$0.02 / GB / monthDefault — table modified in last 90 daysCharged on uncompressed logical size
Long-term logical$0.01 / GB / monthAutomatic — unmodified 90+ consecutive days50% discount, no perf change, zero action needed
Active physical$0.04 / GB / monthOpt-in per datasetCharged on compressed physical bytes (typically 3–10× smaller)
Long-term physical$0.02 / GB / monthOpt-in + unmodified 90+ daysBest rate for well-compressed cold data
Time travelSame rate as active/long-termDefault 7 days (configurable 2–7)Previous versions for recovery — counts toward storage
Fail-safeSame rate (not configurable)7 days after time travel expiresGoogle-managed DR — cannot be queried or disabled

Physical vs Logical Billing — Which Is Cheaper?

BigQuery offers two billing models for storage: logical (default, charged on uncompressed size) and physical (opt-in, charged on compressed size). For well-compressed analytical data, physical billing is often 50–70% cheaper despite the higher per-GB rate, because compression reduces the billable bytes by 3–10×.

SQLstorage_optimization.sql
-- ═══ Check logical vs physical size (determine which billing is cheaper) ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    ROUND(total_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS logical_gb,
    ROUND(total_physical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS physical_gb,
    ROUND(total_logical_bytes / NULLIF(total_physical_bytes, 0), 1) AS compression_ratio,
    -- If ratio > 2.0, physical billing is cheaper
    CASE
      WHEN total_logical_bytes / NULLIF(total_physical_bytes, 0) > 2.0
      THEN 'PHYSICAL cheaper'
      ELSE 'LOGICAL cheaper'
    END AS recommendation
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.TABLE_STORAGE`
ORDER BY total_logical_bytes DESC;

-- ═══ Switch dataset to physical billing ═══
ALTER SCHEMA `project.dataset`
SET OPTIONS (storage_billing_model = 'PHYSICAL');
-- Takes effect immediately. Can switch back to 'LOGICAL' anytime.

-- ═══ Reduce time travel window (save storage) ═══
ALTER TABLE `project.dataset.large_table`
SET OPTIONS (max_time_travel_hours = 48);
-- 2 days instead of default 7 — saves 71% of time travel storage

-- ═══ Set partition expiration ═══
ALTER TABLE `project.dataset.logs`
SET OPTIONS (partition_expiration_days = 90);
-- Auto-delete partitions older than 90 days

-- ═══ Total storage cost breakdown ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    ROUND(active_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS active_gb,
    ROUND(long_term_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS longterm_gb,
    ROUND(time_travel_physical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS timetravel_gb,
    ROUND(fail_safe_physical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS failsafe_gb,
    -- Monthly cost estimate (logical billing)
    ROUND(active_logical_bytes / POW(2,30) * 0.02
        + long_term_logical_bytes / POW(2,30) * 0.01, 2) AS monthly_cost_usd
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.TABLE_STORAGE`
ORDER BY (active_logical_bytes + long_term_logical_bytes) DESC;
💰 Cost Tip — Physical Billing Decision Formula The math is simple: Physical billing is cheaper when compression ratio > 2.0. Logical rate = $0.02/GB. Physical rate = $0.04/GB. If your data compresses 4× (typical for analytical data), then: Logical: 100 GB × $0.02 = $2.00/mo. Physical: 25 GB × $0.04 = $1.00/mo. 50% savings. At 10× compression: $0.40/mo vs $2.00 = 80% savings. Check with the INFORMATION_SCHEMA query above and switch all datasets where ratio > 2.

4.6 Advanced Storage Topics

Auto-Reclustering

BigQuery automatically reclusters data in the background when new data is added to a clustered table. Unlike traditional databases (which require manual VACUUM or OPTIMIZE TABLE), BigQuery manages this entirely — zero user intervention, zero scheduled maintenance jobs. Reclustering is free (no compute charges) and happens asynchronously without affecting query performance.

Partition vs Clustering — When to Use Which

Use Partitioning WhenUse Clustering WhenUse Both When
You always filter by date/timestampYou filter by multiple columnsDate-filtered AND other column filters (most common)
You need partition expiration (auto-delete old data)You need flexible filter combinationsE-commerce: partition by order_date, cluster by country, category
You need to enforce partition filters (require_partition_filter)The filter column has many distinct valuesLogs: partition by log_date, cluster by severity, service
Data is naturally time-seriesYou can't predict which filter comes firstAnalytics: partition by event_date, cluster by user_id, event_type

Table Snapshots and Clones

SQLsnapshots_and_clones.sql
-- ═══ TABLE SNAPSHOT — Point-in-time copy (metadata-only, fast) ═══
CREATE SNAPSHOT TABLE `project.dataset.sales_snapshot_20240315`
CLONE `project.dataset.sales`
FOR SYSTEM_TIME AS OF TIMESTAMP('2024-03-15 00:00:00 UTC');
-- Zero-copy snapshot — only stores delta from base table
-- Cost: only bytes that differ from base (often pennies)

-- ═══ TABLE CLONE — Independent writable copy (metadata-only, fast) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.sales_dev`
CLONE `project.dataset.sales`;
-- Zero-copy clone — shares storage with base until modified
-- Ideal for development/testing environments
-- Writes to the clone create new delta storage (only changed data)

-- ═══ TIME TRAVEL — Query data as it was in the past ═══
SELECT *
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
FOR SYSTEM_TIME AS OF TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 3 DAY);
-- Query the table as it was 3 days ago
-- Useful for: debugging data issues, auditing, recovery
-- Window: configurable 2–7 days (default 7)

-- ═══ RECOVER deleted table (within time travel window) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.sales_recovered` AS
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.sales`
FOR SYSTEM_TIME AS OF TIMESTAMP('2024-03-14 23:59:59 UTC');
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Table Clones for Zero-Cost Dev Environments Table clones are one of BigQuery's most underutilized features. A clone of a 10 TB production table is created in seconds and costs $0 initially (shared storage via copy-on-write). Only modifications to the clone incur storage costs. This means you can give every developer a complete copy of production data for testing without the cost of full data duplication. Clone → test → drop → repeat. The clone shares the same Colossus files until modified.

4.7 Terraform — Partitioned and Clustered Tables

HCLbigquery_tables.tf
# ═══ Production partitioned + clustered table ═══
resource "google_bigquery_table" "sales" {
  dataset_id          = google_bigquery_dataset.warehouse.dataset_id
  table_id            = "sales"
  deletion_protection = true

  time_partitioning {
    type                     = "DAY"
    field                    = "sale_date"
    expiration_ms            = 31536000000   # 365 days
    require_partition_filter = true          # CRITICAL
  }

  clustering = ["country", "category", "customer_id"]

  schema = file("schemas/sales.json")

  labels = {
    layer      = "marts"
    managed_by = "terraform"
  }
}

# ═══ Switch dataset to physical billing ═══
resource "google_bigquery_dataset" "warehouse" {
  dataset_id                 = "warehouse"
  location                   = "US"
  storage_billing_model      = "PHYSICAL"    # Physical billing
  max_time_travel_hours      = 48            # 2 days (save storage)
  default_table_expiration_ms = null         # No auto-expiry
}

4.8 Production Storage Optimization Checklist

#ActionSQL / ConfigImpact
1Partition by datePARTITION BY sale_date10–365× less scan
2Require partition filterrequire_partition_filter = TRUEPrevents accidental full scans
3Cluster by filtered columnsCLUSTER BY country, category2–100× less scan within partition
4Select only needed columnsNever SELECT *5–50× less I/O
5Denormalize with STRUCT/ARRAYEmbed child entities as nested fieldsEliminate JOINs → no shuffle
6Set partition expirationpartition_expiration_days = 365Auto-delete old data
7Evaluate physical billingCheck compression ratio > 2 → switch30–70% storage savings
8Reduce time travelmax_time_travel_hours = 48~71% time travel storage savings
9Use table clones for devCREATE TABLE ... CLONE ...Zero-cost dev environments
10Monitor with INFORMATION_SCHEMATABLE_STORAGE viewFind oversized/un-optimized tables
🎯 Interview Tip — "How would you optimize a BigQuery table that costs too much?" Walk through the checklist in order: (1) Is it partitioned? If not, partition by date. (2) Does it require partition filters? Enable it. (3) Is it clustered by the most-filtered columns? Add clustering. (4) Are queries using SELECT *? Fix them. (5) Can tables be denormalized to avoid JOINs? Embed with STRUCT/ARRAY. (6) Is physical billing cheaper? Check compression ratio. This systematic approach shows structured thinking — exactly what interviewers want.

Section 4 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
PartitioningSkip entire date ranges (partition pruning)PARTITION BY date_col + require_partition_filter = TRUE on every production table
ClusteringSort within partitions → file-level pruning via min/maxCLUSTER BY most-filtered columns (up to 4). Auto-reclustered by BQ.
Combined effectPartition + cluster can reduce scan 99.9%10 TB → 5 GB = $62.50 → $0.03. Non-negotiable for production.
DenormalizationSTRUCT (1:1) + ARRAY (1:many) eliminate JOINsEmbed related entities. Use UNNEST() to query arrays. Avoid star schema.
Storage pricingActive $0.02 / Long-term $0.01 (auto after 90d)Physical billing when compression > 2×. Reduce time travel to 2 days.
Table clonesZero-copy, instant, writable copiesUse for dev/test environments — $0 initial cost via copy-on-write.
Column selectionColumnar format reads only referenced columnsNever SELECT *. Specify exact columns needed.
Optimization priorityPartition → Cluster → Columns → Denormalize → BillingFollow the 10-point checklist (4.8) for every production table.
Part II — Section 5

BigQuery Compute

Slots, reservations, on-demand vs editions, autoscaling, capacity planning — controlling performance and cost on the compute side.

Section 5 — BigQuery Compute — Slots, Reservations, On-Demand vs Editions

5.1 What Is a BigQuery Slot?

A BigQuery slot is a unit of computational capacity — roughly equivalent to one virtual CPU with associated memory and shuffle bandwidth. When BigQuery executes a query, the Dremel engine breaks the query plan into stages, and each stage is distributed across multiple slots running in parallel on Borg-managed machines.

Understanding slots is essential because they are the currency of BigQuery compute. On on-demand pricing, Google auto-allocates slots and charges per-TB-scanned. On Editions pricing, you purchase slot capacity and pay per-slot-hour. Every performance tuning and cost optimization decision on the compute side ultimately reduces to: how many slots are used, for how long, doing what work?

Slot Anatomy

PropertyDetails
What it is~1 vCPU + proportional RAM + shuffle bandwidth, running as a Borg task
GranularitySlots are assigned per-stage, not per-query. Stage 1 may use 500 slots while Stage 2 uses 30.
ParallelismMore slots = more parallel workers = faster scan/aggregate (up to the query's parallelism limit)
Shared resourceOn-demand: slots from shared pool (~2000 max/project). Editions: slots from your reserved pool.
Measurementtotal_slot_ms in INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS = total compute consumed
Average concurrencytotal_slot_ms / elapsed_ms = average concurrent slots during query
Figure 5.1 — Stages use different numbers of slots. Stage 1 (scan) needs the most; later stages need fewer. Idle slots serve concurrent queries.

Key Slot Metrics

  • total_slot_ms: Total compute consumed across all stages. A query using 200 slots for 5 seconds = 1,000,000 slot-ms. This is the number that determines cost on Editions.
  • elapsed_ms: Wall-clock time from query start to completion. Includes Borg allocation, scan, shuffle, merge, and result return.
  • avg_concurrent_slots = total_slot_ms / elapsed_ms: How many slots were active on average. High slot_ms but low elapsed_ms = good parallelism. High slot_ms AND high elapsed_ms = data skew (some slots doing disproportionate work). Low slot_ms but high elapsed_ms = slot contention (queueing for available slots).

5.2 Pricing Models — On-Demand vs Editions

ModelHow It WorksCost (US)Best ForKey Constraint
On-DemandPay per TB of data scanned by queries$6.25 / TBAd-hoc, exploration, small teams, unpredictableShared pool ~2000 slots max. Free 1 TB/mo.
Standard EditionReserve slot capacity. Autoscale 0 → max.~$0.04 / slot-hourPredictable workloads, cost controlNo commitment required. No CMEK.
Enterprise EditionReserve slots + advanced features~$0.06 / slot-hourLarge orgs, compliance, multi-team1yr (~25% off) / 3yr (~50% off) commitments.
Enterprise PlusReserve slots + all features~$0.10 / slot-hourLargest enterprise deploymentsBI Engine included. Omni. Highest SLA.

Feature Comparison Across Editions

AutoscalingAuto (~2000 shared)0 → max (per-second)0 → max (per-second)0 → max (per-second)
