5.3 Break-Even Calculation — When to Switch to Editions

The critical question: when does flat-rate (Editions) become cheaper than on-demand? The calculation depends on your monthly scanning volume relative to reserved slot capacity.

FORMULAbreak_even_calculation.txt
-- ═══ BREAK-EVEN FORMULA ═══

-- Monthly cost of N Standard Edition slots:
editions_cost = N_slots × $0.04/slot-hr × 730 hrs/month

-- Monthly cost of on-demand scanning:
ondemand_cost = monthly_TB_scanned × $6.25/TB

-- Break-even: editions_cost = ondemand_cost
-- N × $0.04 × 730 = TB × $6.25
-- TB = N × $0.04 × 730 / $6.25
-- TB = N × 4.672

-- ═══ EXAMPLES ═══

-- 100 Standard slots:
--   Monthly cost: 100 × $0.04 × 730 = $2,920
--   Break-even at: $2,920 / $6.25 = 467 TB/month
--   → If you scan >467 TB/mo, Editions is cheaper

-- 500 Standard slots:
--   Monthly cost: 500 × $0.04 × 730 = $14,600
--   Break-even at: $14,600 / $6.25 = 2,336 TB/month

-- 100 Enterprise slots (1yr commitment = ~$0.048 effective):
--   Monthly cost: 100 × $0.048 × 730 = $3,504
--   Break-even at: $3,504 / $6.25 = 561 TB/month
--   → Higher rate but more features (CMEK, higher autoscale)

-- ═══ RULE OF THUMB ═══
-- 100 Standard slots ≈ 467 TB/month on-demand equivalent
-- 100 Enterprise slots ≈ 561 TB/month on-demand equivalent
-- Most production environments with scheduled dbt/Dataform jobs,
-- dashboards, and analyst queries cross this threshold quickly.
⚙️ Engineering Insight — The Real Decision Factors Beyond Break-Even Break-even math isn't the only factor. Editions also provide: (1) Performance predictability — on-demand shares slots with other customers; during peak hours your queries may get fewer slots and slow down. Editions guarantees your reserved capacity. (2) Cost predictability — on-demand bills vary month-to-month; Editions is fixed (plus autoscale). (3) Multi-team isolation — reservations let you give ETL 500 slots and analytics 300 slots, preventing one team from starving the other. (4) CMEK requirement — Enterprise Edition is mandatory for customer-managed encryption keys.

5.4 Decision Framework: On-Demand vs Editions

Figure 5.2 — Most orgs start on-demand and migrate to Editions as workloads mature and monthly scan exceeds ~500 TB.

5.5 Reservations and Assignments — Multi-Team Capacity Management

In Editions mode, you organize capacity using two concepts: reservations (pools of slots) and assignments (bindings of projects/folders/orgs to reservations). This enables fine-grained capacity management across teams.

Figure 5.3 — Reservations isolate teams while idle slot sharing maximizes utilization across pools.
SQLreservations_management.sql
-- ═══ CREATE reservation (Enterprise Edition) ═══
CREATE RESERVATION `admin-project.region-us.etl_pool`
OPTIONS (
    slot_capacity       = 500,            -- baseline: 500 slots
    edition             = 'ENTERPRISE',
    autoscale_max_slots = 2000           -- burst up to 2000
);

-- ═══ CREATE assignment (bind project to reservation) ═══
CREATE ASSIGNMENT `admin-project.region-us.etl_pool.etl_assign`
OPTIONS (
    assignee = 'projects/etl-prod',
    job_type = 'QUERY'                   -- QUERY, PIPELINE, or ML_EXTERNAL
);

-- ═══ CREATE second assignment (staging) ═══
CREATE ASSIGNMENT `admin-project.region-us.etl_pool.staging_assign`
OPTIONS (
    assignee = 'projects/etl-staging',
    job_type = 'QUERY'
);
-- Both projects now share the 500-slot ETL pool
-- Can burst to 2000 during peaks (billed per-second for autoscale)

-- ═══ MODIFY reservation (increase baseline) ═══
ALTER RESERVATION `admin-project.region-us.etl_pool`
SET OPTIONS (slot_capacity = 800);

-- ═══ PURCHASE commitment (annual discount) ═══
CREATE CAPACITY COMMITMENT `admin-project.region-us.annual`
OPTIONS (
    slot_count = 500,
    plan       = 'ANNUAL',              -- 1yr → ~25% discount
    edition    = 'ENTERPRISE'
);

-- ═══ VIEW current reservations and utilization ═══
SELECT *
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.RESERVATIONS;

SELECT *
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.ASSIGNMENTS;

5.6 Autoscaling Behavior in Editions

Editions support autoscaling: slot count can dynamically increase from the baseline up to a configured maximum, and scale back down when demand drops. Autoscale slots are billed per-second — you only pay for what you use above baseline.

PropertyStandardEnterpriseEnterprise Plus
Baseline can be 0✅ Scale-to-zero✅ Scale-to-zero✅ Scale-to-zero
Max autoscaleUp to 4× baselineUp to 4× or 8× baselineHighest burst
Scale-up speed~seconds~seconds~seconds
Scale-down speed~minutes after query completes~minutes~minutes
Billing granularityPer-second for autoscalePer-secondPer-second
Idle slot sharing✅ Default ON✅ Default ON✅ Default ON
💰 Cost Tip — Optimal Editions Reservation Strategy The optimal strategy for most organizations: (1) Set baseline to your P50 (median) slot usage — covers typical steady-state load. (2) Set autoscale max to your P95 usage × 1.2 — handles burst peaks with 20% headroom. (3) Purchase 1-year commitment for the baseline — 25% discount on Enterprise. (4) Enable idle slot sharing across reservations — unused ETL slots serve analytics during off-peak. (5) Monitor and adjust quarterly using INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS_TIMELINE_BY_RESERVATION.

5.7 Slot Monitoring and Capacity Planning

SQLslot_monitoring_queries.sql
-- ═══ HOURLY SLOT USAGE (last 7 days) — capacity planning ═══
SELECT
    TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(period_start, HOUR) AS hour,
    ROUND(AVG(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60), 1) AS avg_slots,
    ROUND(MAX(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60), 1) AS peak_slots,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60, 100)[OFFSET(50)] AS p50_slots,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60, 100)[OFFSET(95)] AS p95_slots
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS_TIMELINE
WHERE period_start > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 7 DAY)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY'
GROUP BY hour
ORDER BY hour;
-- P50 → set as baseline. P95 × 1.2 → set as autoscale max.

-- ═══ PER-QUERY SLOT ANALYSIS — find slot-hungry queries ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    user_email,
    SUBSTR(query, 1, 100) AS query_preview,
    total_slot_ms,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, MILLISECOND) AS elapsed_ms,
    SAFE_DIVIDE(total_slot_ms,
        TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, MILLISECOND)) AS avg_slots,
    ROUND(total_bytes_processed / POW(2,30), 2) AS gb_scanned
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 DAY)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY' AND state = 'DONE'
ORDER BY total_slot_ms DESC
LIMIT 20;

-- ═══ RESERVATION UTILIZATION (Editions only) ═══
SELECT
    reservation_id,
    TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(period_start, HOUR) AS hour,
    ROUND(SUM(period_slot_ms) / 3600000, 1) AS slot_hours_used,
    MAX(slot_capacity) AS slot_capacity,
    ROUND(SAFE_DIVIDE(SUM(period_slot_ms),
        MAX(slot_capacity) * 3600000) * 100, 1) AS utilization_pct
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS_TIMELINE_BY_RESERVATION
WHERE period_start > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 7 DAY)
GROUP BY reservation_id, hour
ORDER BY hour;
-- utilization_pct < 30% → over-provisioned, reduce baseline
-- utilization_pct > 80% → under-provisioned, increase baseline or autoscale

-- ═══ DETECT SLOT CONTENTION (queueing) ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(start_time, creation_time, SECOND) AS queue_seconds,
    total_slot_ms,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, SECOND) AS execution_seconds
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 DAY)
  AND TIMESTAMP_DIFF(start_time, creation_time, SECOND) > 5
ORDER BY queue_seconds DESC;
-- queue_seconds > 5 = queries waiting for slots → increase capacity
⚠️ Common Pitfall — Under-Provisioned Reservations Cause Queueing When switching from on-demand to Editions, the most common mistake: under-provisioning. On on-demand, BigQuery quietly gives up to ~2000 shared slots. With Editions, queries are limited to your reservation + autoscale headroom. If baseline is 200 slots and a heavy ETL job needs 500, the job runs slowly or queues. Symptoms: start_time - creation_time > 5 seconds (queries waiting). Fix: increase baseline or autoscale_max_slots. Use the P50/P95 monitoring queries above to right-size.

5.8 Slot Estimation Rules of Thumb

Formula / RuleWhat It Tells You
avg_slots = total_slot_ms / elapsed_msAverage concurrent slots used. Good for comparing query efficiency.
slots ≈ bytes_scanned / elapsed_sec / 500 MB/sRough estimate for scan-heavy queries. ~500 MB/sec per slot typical throughput.
100 GB scanned in 10 sec ≈ 200 slots100 GB / 10s / 0.5 GB/s = 200 concurrent slots
High avg_slots + high elapsed = data skewSome slots doing much more work than others. Fix: repartition, avoid hotkey JOINs.
Low avg_slots + high elapsed = contentionQueries waiting for slots. Fix: increase reservation baseline or autoscale max.
Baseline = P50 slot usageCovers typical steady-state. Commit annually for discount.
Autoscale max = P95 × 1.2Handles burst peaks with 20% headroom. Billed per-second.

5.9 Terraform — Reservations and Commitments

HCLbigquery_reservations.tf
# ═══ Capacity commitment (annual = 25% discount) ═══
resource "google_bigquery_reservation" "etl" {
  name                = "etl-reservation"
  location            = "US"
  slot_capacity       = 500
  edition             = "ENTERPRISE"
  autoscale {
    max_slots = 2000
  }
}

resource "google_bigquery_reservation_assignment" "etl_prod" {
  assignee    = "projects/etl-prod"
  job_type    = "QUERY"
  reservation = google_bigquery_reservation.etl.id
}

resource "google_bigquery_capacity_commitment" "annual" {
  location   = "US"
  slot_count = 500
  plan       = "ANNUAL"    # ~25% discount vs pay-as-you-go
  edition    = "ENTERPRISE"
}

5.10 Migration Path: On-Demand → Editions

Most organizations start on on-demand and migrate to Editions as workloads mature. The migration path:

  1. Month 1–3: Run on-demand. Instrument with INFORMATION_SCHEMA queries. Track monthly TB scanned and slot usage patterns.
  2. Month 3: Run the P50/P95 slot analysis. Calculate break-even. If monthly scan exceeds the threshold, start planning migration.
  3. Month 4: Create Standard Edition reservation in a test project. Assign non-critical workloads. Compare performance vs on-demand.
  4. Month 5: If Standard works, consider Enterprise for CMEK/commitment discounts. Create reservations per team (ETL, Analytics, ML).
  5. Month 6: Migrate all production workloads to Editions. Purchase annual commitment for baseline slots. Set autoscale for peaks.
  6. Quarterly: Review utilization. Adjust baselines. Re-evaluate commitment levels.
🎯 Interview Tip — "How would you size BigQuery reservations for a new platform?" The ideal answer: "I'd start on on-demand to establish a usage baseline. After 2–3 months, I'd query INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS_TIMELINE for P50 and P95 slot usage per hour. P50 becomes the reservation baseline — I'd purchase an annual commitment for 25% savings. P95 × 1.2 becomes the autoscale maximum — billed per-second for burst capacity. I'd create separate reservations per team (ETL, analytics, ML) with idle slot sharing enabled for maximum utilization. Then review quarterly and adjust."

5.11 Worked Example — Cost Comparison

A mid-sized analytics team with 20 analysts + scheduled dbt jobs scanning ~2,000 TB/month:

ScenarioCalculationMonthly Cost
On-Demand2,000 TB × $6.25/TB$12,500
Standard Edition (300 slots)300 × $0.04/hr × 730 hrs$8,760
Enterprise (300 slots, annual)300 × $0.048/hr × 730 × 0.75$7,884
Enterprise (300 slots, 3yr)300 × $0.048/hr × 730 × 0.50$5,256

Result: Switching from on-demand to Enterprise 3-year commitment saves $7,244/month (58%). Over 3 years: $261K saved. The 300 slots handle the team's P50 load; autoscale to 1200 covers peak scheduled job windows. This is a common production scenario where Editions dramatically reduces costs.

⚙️ Engineering Insight — Slot Efficiency: Reduce Scan, Not Just Add Slots Before increasing slots, always optimize queries first. A query scanning 10 TB because it lacks a partition filter costs 200 slots × 20 seconds. The same query with proper partitioning scans 27 GB and uses 50 slots × 2 seconds. Storage optimization (Section 4) reduces compute demand by 10–100× — far more impactful than adding more slots. Always optimize partitioning + clustering + column selection BEFORE increasing reservation size.

Section 5 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Slot = compute unit~1 vCPU + memory + shuffle bandwidthMonitor total_slot_ms and avg_concurrent_slots per query
On-Demand$6.25/TB scanned, shared pool, no commitmentBest for ad-hoc, <500 TB/mo, small teams, exploration
EditionsReserved slots per-hour, autoscaling, commitmentsBest for production >500 TB/mo, multi-team, cost predictability
Break-even100 Standard slots ≈ 467 TB/month on-demandCalculate using your actual monthly scan volume
ReservationsSlot pools with project assignmentsSeparate pools per team: ETL, Analytics, ML. Enable idle sharing.
Autoscaling0 → max, per-second billingBaseline = P50 usage. Max = P95 × 1.2. Annual commit for baseline.
Contention detectionstart_time - creation_time > 5s = queueingMonitor INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS. Increase baseline if queueing.
Optimize query firstStorage optimization > adding more slotsPartition + cluster + column selection before increasing reservation
Part II — Section 6

BigQuery Ingestion

Streaming Inserts, Storage Write API, Batch Load, Federated Queries, Datastream CDC, and BQ Data Transfer — every path for getting data into BigQuery.

Section 6 — BigQuery Ingestion — Streaming, Batch, Storage Write API, Federated

6.1 Ingestion Methods Overview

BigQuery provides six distinct ingestion paths, each optimized for a different latency, throughput, and cost profile. Choosing the right ingestion method is critical — the wrong choice can cost 10× more or add hours of unnecessary latency. The following table summarizes all methods, and subsequent subsections cover each in depth with production-ready code.

MethodLatencyThroughputCostBest For
Batch Load (LOAD DATA)MinutesTB/hourFree (no query charges)Daily/hourly batch from GCS. Default choice for batch.
Storage Write API (committed)SecondsMillions rows/secFree (committed mode)High-throughput streaming. Replaces legacy inserts.
Storage Write API (buffered)SecondsMillions rows/sec$0.025/GB (buffered)Exactly-once streaming with flush control.
Legacy Streaming InsertsSeconds100K rows/sec/table$0.01/200 MBLegacy — use Storage Write API instead.
Federated QuerySecondsVaries (GCS throughput)Per-TB scannedQuery GCS/Drive/Bigtable without loading.
Datastream CDCSeconds–MinutesVaries (DB WAL rate)Per-GB processedReal-time CDC from MySQL/PG/Oracle.
BQ Data Transfer ServiceScheduledVariesFree (transfer) + storageSaaS sources (GA, Ads, YouTube, S3).
Figure 6.1 — All BigQuery ingestion paths: sources (left) → ingestion methods (middle) → BigQuery storage layers (right). Choose method based on latency requirement and source type.

6.2 Batch Load — The Default for GCS Files

Batch load is the default ingestion method for getting data from Cloud Storage into BigQuery. It's free (no query compute charges — only storage costs), supports all major formats (Parquet, Avro, ORC, JSON, CSV), and can load terabytes per hour. For most data platforms, batch load from GCS is the primary ingestion path, typically run on a daily or hourly schedule via Cloud Composer.

BASHbatch_load_bq_cli.sh
# ═══ LOAD Parquet from GCS (most common) ═══
bq load \
  --source_format=PARQUET \
  --autodetect \
  --time_partitioning_field=sale_date \
  --clustering_fields=country,product_id \
  --replace \
  project:dataset.sales \
  'gs://bucket/sales/2024/03/15/*.parquet'

# ═══ LOAD Avro (schema included in file) ═══
bq load \
  --source_format=AVRO \
  --use_avro_logical_types \
  project:dataset.events \
  'gs://bucket/events/2024-03-15/*.avro'

# ═══ LOAD JSON (newline-delimited) ═══
bq load \
  --source_format=NEWLINE_DELIMITED_JSON \
  --autodetect \
  --max_bad_records=100 \
  project:dataset.raw_events \
  'gs://bucket/raw/events_20240315.json'

# ═══ LOAD CSV (with explicit schema) ═══
bq load \
  --source_format=CSV \
  --skip_leading_rows=1 \
  --schema='name:STRING,age:INT64,email:STRING,revenue:NUMERIC' \
  project:dataset.users \
  'gs://bucket/users/export.csv'
SQLbatch_load_sql.sql
-- ═══ SQL-based LOAD DATA (preferred — more control) ═══
LOAD DATA OVERWRITE `project.dataset.sales`
PARTITION BY sale_date
CLUSTER BY country, product_id
FROM FILES (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://bucket/sales/2024/03/*.parquet']
);

-- ═══ LOAD DATA with partition decorator (append to specific partition) ═══
LOAD DATA INTO `project.dataset.sales`
OVERWRITE PARTITION (sale_date = '2024-03-15')
FROM FILES (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://bucket/sales/2024/03/15/*.parquet']
);

-- ═══ LOAD from multiple URIs with wildcards ═══
LOAD DATA INTO `project.dataset.all_events`
FROM FILES (
    format        = 'PARQUET',
    uris          = ['gs://bucket/events/year=2024/month=03/day=*/data.parquet'],
    hive_partition_uri_prefix = 'gs://bucket/events/'
);
-- Hive-style partitioning: auto-extracts year/month/day from path

Batch Load Best Practices

PracticeWhyHow
Use Parquet formatColumnar, compressed, typed — best performanceConvert CSV/JSON to Parquet before loading
Target file size 128–512 MBToo small = metadata overhead. Too large = slow retries.Coalesce in Spark/Dataflow before writing to GCS
Use OVERWRITE per partitionIdempotent — re-run safely without duplicatesLOAD DATA OVERWRITE PARTITION (...)
Set autodetect for Parquet/AvroSchema embedded in file — no manual schema needed--autodetect or let SQL infer
Avoid CSV for productionNo types, no compression, no schema — error-proneUse Parquet or Avro. CSV only for legacy/one-off.
💰 Cost Tip — Batch Load Is Free Batch loading from GCS into BigQuery incurs zero compute charges. You pay only for the storage of the loaded data ($0.02/GB/month active). This makes batch load the most cost-effective ingestion method by far. Compare: streaming via legacy inserts costs $0.01/200 MB (~$50/TB). Storage Write API committed mode is also free. The general rule: if data can wait minutes, use batch load. If data needs sub-second freshness, use Storage Write API committed mode.

6.3 Storage Write API — High-Throughput Streaming

The Storage Write API is BigQuery's modern streaming ingestion interface, replacing the legacy streaming inserts API. It provides higher throughput (millions of rows/sec vs 100K), lower cost (free in committed mode), and better exactly-once semantics. All new streaming implementations should use the Storage Write API.

Storage Write API Modes

ModeSemanticsCostLatencyUse Case
Default stream (committed)At-least-once, immediately visibleFree<1 secHigh-volume streaming, can tolerate rare duplicates
Committed (explicit stream)Exactly-once within stream, visible on commitFreeSeconds (on commit)Exactly-once from Dataflow, controlled visibility
BufferedExactly-once, visible after flush$0.025/GBSeconds (on flush)Transaction-like writes with rollback capability
PendingExactly-once, visible after commit all$0.025/GBMinutes (batch commit)Atomic multi-stream commits (batch-style with streaming API)
PYTHONstorage_write_api.py
# ═══ Storage Write API — Committed Mode (free, exactly-once) ═══
from google.cloud import bigquery_storage_v1
from google.cloud.bigquery_storage_v1 import types
from google.protobuf import descriptor_pb2
import json

# Create write client
client = bigquery_storage_v1.BigQueryWriteClient()
parent = client.table_path("my-project", "my_dataset", "events")

# Create write stream (COMMITTED = immediately visible, free)
write_stream = types.WriteStream(type_=types.WriteStream.Type.COMMITTED)
stream = client.create_write_stream(
    parent=parent, write_stream=write_stream
)
stream_name = stream.name

# Append rows using protobuf-serialized data
# (In production, use protobuf schema matching your BQ table)
request = types.AppendRowsRequest(
    write_stream=stream_name,
    proto_rows=types.AppendRowsRequest.ProtoData(
        writer_schema=proto_schema,   # protobuf descriptor
        rows=proto_rows               # serialized row data
    )
)
response = client.append_rows(iter([request]))
# Data immediately visible in BigQuery after response

# ═══ SIMPLIFIED: Using BigQuery client library (easier API) ═══
from google.cloud import bigquery

client = bigquery.Client()
table_ref = client.dataset("my_dataset").table("events")

# Streaming insert using Storage Write API under the hood
errors = client.insert_rows_json(
    table_ref,
    [
        {"event_id": "e1", "event_type": "click", "ts": "2024-03-15T10:30:00Z"},
        {"event_id": "e2", "event_type": "purchase", "ts": "2024-03-15T10:30:01Z"},
    ]
)
if errors:
    print(f"Errors: {errors}")
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Storage Write API vs Legacy Streaming Inserts The Storage Write API is superior in every dimension: (1) 10× higher throughput — millions vs 100K rows/sec. (2) Free in committed mode — legacy costs $0.01/200 MB. (3) Exactly-once via committed streams — legacy is at-least-once only. (4) Protobuf wire format — more efficient than JSON. (5) Better error handling — per-row error reporting. All new streaming implementations should use Storage Write API. Legacy streaming inserts are maintained for backward compatibility but are effectively deprecated.

6.4 Pub/Sub → BigQuery Subscription — Zero-Code Streaming

The simplest streaming path to BigQuery: create a BigQuery subscription on a Pub/Sub topic. Messages are automatically written to a BigQuery table with zero code, zero infrastructure. This is ideal for simple event logging where no transformation is needed.

BASHpubsub_bq_subscription.sh
# ═══ Create BigQuery subscription (zero code streaming) ═══
gcloud pubsub subscriptions create events-to-bq \
    --topic=app-events \
    --bigquery-table=project:dataset.events \
    --use-topic-schema \
    --write-metadata \
    --drop-unknown-fields

# That's it. Messages from app-events topic now stream to BigQuery.
# --use-topic-schema: use Avro/Proto schema defined on the topic
# --write-metadata: include publish_time, message_id, attributes
# --drop-unknown-fields: ignore fields not in BQ schema

# ═══ Verify it's working ═══
gcloud pubsub subscriptions describe events-to-bq
# Look for bigqueryConfig.state = ACTIVE
HCLpubsub_bq_subscription.tf
# ═══ Terraform: Pub/Sub BigQuery subscription ═══
resource "google_pubsub_subscription" "events_bq" {
  name  = "events-to-bigquery"
  topic = google_pubsub_topic.events.id

  bigquery_config {
    table               = "${var.project}.raw.app_events"
    use_topic_schema    = true
    write_metadata      = true
    drop_unknown_fields = true
  }
}
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — When to Use Each Streaming Path Three streaming paths to BigQuery, each for a different complexity level: (1) Pub/Sub → BQ Subscription: zero code, zero infra — for simple event logging without transformation. (2) Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BQ: full Apache Beam — for complex enrichment, windowing, aggregation, exactly-once. (3) Direct Storage Write API: application writes directly — for custom streaming from non-Pub/Sub sources or when you need maximum control. Start with #1, upgrade to #2 only when you need transformation, and use #3 for custom integrations.

6.5 Federated Queries — Query Without Loading

Federated queries let you query data in place — in GCS, Bigtable, Cloud SQL, Cloud Spanner, or even Google Sheets — without loading it into BigQuery native tables. You create an external table pointing to the data source, then query it with standard SQL.

SQLfederated_queries.sql
-- ═══ EXTERNAL TABLE on GCS Parquet ═══
CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE `project.dataset.external_sales`
WITH CONNECTION `project.us.my-connection`
OPTIONS (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://bucket/sales/2024/*.parquet']
);

-- Query as normal — data stays in GCS
SELECT country, SUM(revenue)
FROM `project.dataset.external_sales`
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
GROUP BY country;

-- ═══ EXTERNAL TABLE on Cloud SQL (PostgreSQL) ═══
CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE `project.dataset.pg_customers`
WITH CONNECTION `project.us.cloudsql-connection`
OPTIONS (
    type = 'CLOUD_SQL',
    database = 'mydb',
    table_name = 'customers'
);

-- Join BigQuery native data with Cloud SQL in real-time
SELECT s.order_id, c.name, s.revenue
FROM `project.dataset.native_sales` s
JOIN `project.dataset.pg_customers` c ON s.customer_id = c.id
WHERE s.sale_date = '2024-03-15';

-- ═══ EXTERNAL TABLE on Bigtable ═══
CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE `project.dataset.bt_metrics`
OPTIONS (
    format = 'CLOUD_BIGTABLE',
    uris   = ['https://googleapis.com/bigtable/projects/project/instances/instance/tables/metrics'],
    bigtable_options = '{"readRowkeyAsString": true, "columnFamilies": [{"familyId": "cf1", "columns": [{"qualifierString": "value", "type": "FLOAT"}]}]}'
);

-- ═══ BIGLAKE TABLE (external with native security) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
WITH CONNECTION `project.us.biglake-conn`
OPTIONS (
    table_format = 'ICEBERG',
    file_format  = 'PARQUET',
    storage_uri  = 'gs://bucket/iceberg/orders'
);
-- Full DML on Iceberg: INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE, MERGE
-- Row/column security applied same as native tables
⚠️ Common Pitfall — Federated Query Performance Limitations External tables on GCS do NOT benefit from BigQuery's native optimizations: no Capacitor columnar format (uses Parquet/Avro as-is), no automatic clustering/reclustering, limited partition pruning (Hive-style only), and performance bounded by GCS read throughput. A query on a native BQ table scanning 1 TB might take 2 seconds; the same query on an external Parquet table might take 30+ seconds. Rule: if data is queried frequently, load it into native BigQuery tables. Use federated queries only for ad-hoc exploration, rare queries, or data that must stay in GCS for compliance reasons.

6.6 Datastream CDC — Real-Time Database Replication

Datastream reads database transaction logs (MySQL binlog, PostgreSQL WAL, Oracle LogMiner) and streams changes to BigQuery or GCS in near-real-time. It handles both the initial snapshot (backfill) and ongoing incremental CDC. When targeting BigQuery directly, Datastream automatically applies MERGE operations — INSERT new rows, UPDATE changed rows, DELETE removed rows. Zero code required.

BASHdatastream_setup.sh
# ═══ Create connection profile (source: PostgreSQL) ═══
gcloud datastream connection-profiles create pg-source \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --display-name="Production PostgreSQL" \
    --type=postgresql \
    --postgresql-hostname=10.0.0.5 \
    --postgresql-port=5432 \
    --postgresql-username=replication_user \
    --postgresql-password=secret \
    --postgresql-database=production

# ═══ Create connection profile (destination: BigQuery) ═══
gcloud datastream connection-profiles create bq-dest \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --display-name="BigQuery Destination" \
    --type=bigquery

# ═══ Create stream (PostgreSQL → BigQuery with auto MERGE) ═══
gcloud datastream streams create pg-to-bq \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --display-name="PG to BQ CDC" \
    --source=pg-source \
    --postgresql-source-config=source-config.json \
    --destination=bq-dest \
    --bigquery-destination-config=dest-config.json \
    --backfill-all

# Datastream now:
# 1. Performs initial full snapshot (backfill) of all selected tables
# 2. Reads PostgreSQL WAL for ongoing changes
# 3. Applies MERGE to BigQuery (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE automatically)
# 4. Handles schema drift (new columns added to source)

Manual MERGE Pattern (from GCS-staged CDC)

If Datastream writes CDC events to GCS (instead of directly to BigQuery), or if you use Debezium/Kafka for CDC, you need to apply changes manually using a BigQuery MERGE statement. This is also useful for understanding what Datastream's direct-to-BQ mode does automatically.

SQLcdc_merge_pattern.sql
-- ═══ CDC MERGE — Apply changes from staging to target ═══
MERGE INTO `project.dataset.orders` AS target
USING `project.dataset.orders_cdc_staging` AS source
ON target.order_id = source.order_id

-- DELETE: source indicates row was deleted in the source DB
WHEN MATCHED AND source._metadata_deleted = TRUE THEN DELETE

-- UPDATE: source has newer version of existing row
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET
    target.status     = source.status,
    target.amount     = source.amount,
    target.updated_at = source.updated_at

-- INSERT: new row not yet in target
WHEN NOT MATCHED AND source._metadata_deleted = FALSE THEN INSERT
    (order_id, customer_id, status, amount, created_at, updated_at)
    VALUES (source.order_id, source.customer_id, source.status,
            source.amount, source.created_at, source.updated_at);

-- Schedule this MERGE via Cloud Composer every 5 minutes
-- Or use Datastream direct-to-BQ to avoid manual MERGE entirely

6.7 File Format Selection for GCS → BigQuery Loading

FormatLoad SpeedCompressionSchemaBest ForAvoid When
ParquetFastestExcellent (Snappy/Zstd)Embedded (typed)Default for all analytics loadingNever — always prefer Parquet
AvroFastGood (Deflate/Snappy)Embedded (typed)Streaming writes, schema evolutionAnalytics queries (columnar is better)
ORCFastExcellentEmbedded (typed)Hive/Dataproc migration workloadsNew projects (use Parquet)
JSON (NDJSON)ModerateNone (unless gzipped)Inferred or explicitQuick debugging, simple ingestionProduction at scale (no compression)
CSVSlowestNoneNo typesLegacy imports, one-off loadsEverything else — CSV is fragile
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Parquet File Size Matters When loading Parquet from GCS, file size significantly affects performance. Ideal: 128–512 MB per file. Too small (<10 MB): excessive metadata overhead, slow listing, many parallel reads for little data. Too large (>1 GB): slow retries on failure, poor parallelism for small loads. If your upstream produces many small files (common with Kafka Connect or Dataflow), add a compaction step: Dataproc Serverless Spark job that reads small Parquet files and rewrites them as fewer 256 MB files, then load the compacted files into BigQuery.

6.8 Ingestion Decision Flowchart

QuestionIf Yes →
Data in GCS as Parquet/Avro files?Batch Load (LOAD DATA — free, fast)
Need real-time streaming (<5s)?Storage Write API (committed mode — free)
Simple event logging from Pub/Sub? No transform?Pub/Sub → BQ Subscription (zero code)
Complex streaming with enrichment/windowing?Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BQ (Storage Write API)
CDC from MySQL/PostgreSQL/Oracle?Datastream → BQ (auto MERGE, zero code)
SaaS data (Google Analytics, Ads, YouTube)?BQ Data Transfer Service (free, scheduled)
SaaS data (Salesforce, Stripe, HubSpot, 500+)?Fivetran / Airbyte → BQ (managed connectors)
Query data without loading (exploration)?Federated Query / External Table
Need Iceberg/Delta table format on GCS?BigLake Table (DML + security on external data)
🎯 Interview Tip — "How would you ingest real-time events into BigQuery?" Three options by complexity: (1) Simplest: Pub/Sub → BigQuery Subscription — zero code, messages land in BQ automatically. Use when no transformation needed. (2) With transformation: Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BQ via Storage Write API — full Apache Beam for enrichment, windowing, exactly-once. (3) Direct from app: Application writes directly via Storage Write API committed mode — when you need maximum control or don't use Pub/Sub. In all cases, the destination table should be partitioned by event timestamp and clustered by event type.

Section 6 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Batch LoadFree, TB/hour, from GCS filesDefault for batch: LOAD DATA from Parquet. OVERWRITE per partition for idempotency.
Storage Write APIModern streaming: free (committed), millions rows/secReplace legacy streaming inserts. Use committed mode for free exactly-once.
Pub/Sub → BQ SubZero-code streaming: messages auto-land in BQSimplest real-time path. Use when no transformation needed.
Federated queriesQuery GCS/Bigtable/CloudSQL in-placeAd-hoc only. For frequent queries: load into native BQ tables.
Datastream CDCZero-code CDC with auto MERGEDefault for RDBMS → BQ replication. Handles snapshot + ongoing CDC.
File formatParquet: always preferred for batch loadingTarget 128–512 MB files. Use Snappy compression. Avoid CSV.
BigLakeIceberg/Delta on GCS with BQ DML + securityUse for open table format with BigQuery as the query engine.
Cost hierarchyBatch (free) > Storage Write committed (free) > Buffered ($0.025/GB) > Legacy ($0.01/200MB)Use the cheapest method that meets your latency requirement.
Part II — Section 7

BigQuery SQL — Advanced Patterns

Window functions, QUALIFY, PIVOT/UNPIVOT, STRUCT/ARRAY operations, SEARCH index, BigQuery ML, JSON, scripting — production SQL patterns for senior engineers.

Section 7 — BigQuery SQL — Advanced Patterns

7.1 Why Advanced SQL Matters in BigQuery

BigQuery is not just a warehouse — it's a full SQL processing engine. Mastering its SQL dialect unlocks capabilities that would otherwise require Spark, Python scripts, or external ML platforms. BigQuery's SQL extends standard SQL with powerful features: QUALIFY (filter on window functions without subqueries), PIVOT/UNPIVOT (reshape data), native STRUCT/ARRAY operations (denormalized queries), SEARCH (full-text search), BigQuery ML (train models in SQL), and scripting (procedural logic). This section covers each pattern with production-ready examples.

Every pattern is chosen for its practical impact: these are the SQL techniques that experienced BigQuery engineers use daily in production — for building Dataform/dbt models, powering Looker dashboards, building feature engineering pipelines, and answering ad-hoc questions on terabyte-scale datasets.

7.2 Window Functions — The Most Powerful SQL Feature

Window functions compute values across a set of rows related to the current row — without collapsing rows like GROUP BY. They enable running totals, rankings, lag/lead analysis, moving averages, and percentile calculations. In BigQuery, window functions are highly optimized and often faster than equivalent self-JOINs or subqueries.

SQLwindow_functions.sql
-- ═══ RUNNING TOTAL + RANK within each country ═══
SELECT
    sale_date,
    country,
    revenue,
    SUM(revenue) OVER (
        PARTITION BY country
        ORDER BY sale_date
        ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS cumulative_revenue,
    RANK() OVER (
        PARTITION BY country
        ORDER BY revenue DESC
    ) AS revenue_rank
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
WHERE sale_date BETWEEN '2024-01-01' AND '2024-03-31';

-- ═══ 7-DAY MOVING AVERAGE ═══
SELECT
    sale_date,
    revenue,
    AVG(revenue) OVER (
        ORDER BY sale_date
        ROWS BETWEEN 6 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS moving_avg_7d
FROM `project.dataset.daily_revenue`;

-- ═══ LAG / LEAD — Compare with previous/next row ═══
SELECT
    sale_date,
    revenue,
    LAG(revenue, 1) OVER (ORDER BY sale_date) AS prev_day_revenue,
    revenue - LAG(revenue, 1) OVER (ORDER BY sale_date) AS day_over_day_change,
    ROUND(
        SAFE_DIVIDE(
            revenue - LAG(revenue, 1) OVER (ORDER BY sale_date),
            LAG(revenue, 1) OVER (ORDER BY sale_date)
        ) * 100, 2
    ) AS pct_change
FROM `project.dataset.daily_revenue`;

-- ═══ FIRST_VALUE / LAST_VALUE — within each partition ═══
SELECT
    customer_id,
    order_date,
    product_id,
    FIRST_VALUE(product_id) OVER (
        PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date
    ) AS first_product_ever,
    LAST_VALUE(product_id) OVER (
        PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date
        ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND UNBOUNDED FOLLOWING
    ) AS latest_product
FROM `project.dataset.orders`;

-- ═══ NTILE — Divide into equal buckets ═══
SELECT
    customer_id,
    lifetime_revenue,
    NTILE(10) OVER (ORDER BY lifetime_revenue DESC) AS revenue_decile
-- decile 1 = top 10% customers, decile 10 = bottom 10%
FROM `project.dataset.customer_ltv`;

7.3 QUALIFY — Filter on Window Functions (BigQuery-Specific)

QUALIFY is a BigQuery-specific clause (not in standard SQL) that filters rows after window functions are computed — eliminating the need for a subquery or CTE. It's the single most useful BigQuery SQL extension for production work.

SQLqualify_patterns.sql
-- ═══ TOP-N PER GROUP — Without subquery (QUALIFY) ═══
SELECT
    country,
    customer_id,
    revenue
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
QUALIFY ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
    PARTITION BY country ORDER BY revenue DESC
) <= 5;
-- Top 5 customers per country — no subquery, no CTE needed

-- ═══ COMPARE: Same query WITHOUT QUALIFY (requires subquery) ═══
SELECT * FROM (
    SELECT country, customer_id, revenue,
        ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY country ORDER BY revenue DESC) AS rn
    FROM `project.dataset.sales`
    WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
)
WHERE rn <= 5;
-- QUALIFY version is cleaner and often more efficient

-- ═══ DEDUPLICATION — Keep latest version per key ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.cdc_events`
QUALIFY ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
    PARTITION BY record_id
    ORDER BY updated_at DESC
) = 1;
-- Keeps only the latest version of each record_id
-- Critical pattern for CDC deduplication in dbt/Dataform models

-- ═══ SESSIONIZATION — Assign session IDs based on gaps ═══
SELECT
    user_id,
    event_time,
    event_type,
    SUM(new_session) OVER (
        PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY event_time
    ) AS session_id
FROM (
    SELECT *,
        CASE WHEN TIMESTAMP_DIFF(
            event_time,
            LAG(event_time) OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY event_time),
            MINUTE
        ) > 30 OR LAG(event_time) OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY event_time) IS NULL
        THEN 1 ELSE 0 END AS new_session
    FROM `project.dataset.clickstream`
);
-- Creates session boundaries at 30-minute gaps
🎯 Interview Tip — "How do you deduplicate in BigQuery?" The canonical answer: SELECT * FROM table QUALIFY ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY key ORDER BY ts DESC) = 1. This keeps the latest version of each key. It's the most common pattern in dbt/Dataform models for CDC deduplication. Mentioning QUALIFY (instead of a subquery) shows you know BigQuery-specific SQL.

7.4 PIVOT and UNPIVOT — Reshape Data

SQLpivot_unpivot.sql
-- ═══ PIVOT — Rows to columns ═══
SELECT * FROM
    (SELECT country, quarter, revenue FROM `project.dataset.quarterly_sales`)
PIVOT (
    SUM(revenue) FOR quarter IN ('Q1', 'Q2', 'Q3', 'Q4')
);
-- Result: country | Q1 | Q2 | Q3 | Q4
--         US      | 1M | 1.2M | 1.1M | 1.5M

-- ═══ PIVOT with multiple aggregations ═══
SELECT * FROM
    (SELECT country, quarter, revenue, orders FROM sales)
PIVOT (
    SUM(revenue) AS rev,
    COUNT(orders) AS cnt
    FOR quarter IN ('Q1', 'Q2')
);
-- Result: country | Q1_rev | Q1_cnt | Q2_rev | Q2_cnt

-- ═══ UNPIVOT — Columns to rows ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.sales_wide`
UNPIVOT (
    revenue FOR quarter IN (
        q1_revenue AS 'Q1',
        q2_revenue AS 'Q2',
        q3_revenue AS 'Q3',
        q4_revenue AS 'Q4'
    )
);
-- Converts wide format (one col per quarter) to long format

7.5 STRUCT and ARRAY Operations — Denormalized Queries

SQLstruct_array_operations.sql
-- ═══ BUILD STRUCT on the fly ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    STRUCT(customer_id AS id, name, email) AS customer
FROM `project.dataset.users`;

-- ═══ BUILD ARRAY from aggregation ═══
SELECT
    customer_id,
    ARRAY_AGG(
        STRUCT(product_name, quantity, price)
        ORDER BY price DESC
    ) AS items
FROM `project.dataset.order_items`
GROUP BY customer_id;

-- ═══ UNNEST — Flatten array for querying ═══
SELECT
    o.order_id,
    o.customer.name,
    item.product_name,
    item.quantity * item.unit_price AS line_total
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm` o,
UNNEST(o.items) AS item
WHERE o.order_date = '2024-03-15';

-- ═══ ARRAY contains check ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE 'premium' IN UNNEST(tags);

-- ═══ ARRAY_LENGTH — count elements ═══
SELECT order_id, ARRAY_LENGTH(items) AS num_items
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE ARRAY_LENGTH(items) > 10;

-- ═══ Correlated subquery inside ARRAY ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    (SELECT SUM(i.quantity * i.unit_price)
     FROM UNNEST(items) AS i) AS order_total,
    (SELECT MAX(i.unit_price)
     FROM UNNEST(items) AS i) AS max_item_price
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`;

-- ═══ ARRAY_AGG with DISTINCT and LIMIT ═══
SELECT
    country,
    ARRAY_AGG(DISTINCT product_category LIMIT 5) AS top_categories
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY country;

-- ═══ Flatten nested STRUCTs ═══
SELECT
    order_id,
    customer.name,
    customer.address.city,
    customer.address.state,
    customer.address.zip
FROM `project.dataset.orders_denorm`
WHERE customer.address.state = 'CA';

7.6 SEARCH Index and Function — Full-Text Search

BigQuery's SEARCH function provides full-text search across string columns — much faster than LIKE '%keyword%' patterns. Combined with a SEARCH index, it can scan billions of log messages in seconds.

SQLsearch_index.sql
-- ═══ CREATE SEARCH INDEX (on text columns) ═══
CREATE SEARCH INDEX my_search_idx
ON `project.dataset.application_logs` (ALL COLUMNS);
-- Indexes all STRING and JSON columns for full-text search
-- Can also specify specific columns: (log_message, error_details)

-- ═══ USE SEARCH function ═══
SELECT log_time, severity, service, log_message
FROM `project.dataset.application_logs`
WHERE SEARCH(log_message, 'error timeout connection refused')
  AND log_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 HOUR);
-- Full-text search: finds rows containing ANY of the search terms
-- 100–1000× faster than LIKE '%error%' on large tables

-- ═══ SEARCH on specific column ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.support_tickets`
WHERE SEARCH(description, 'billing refund credit card');

-- ═══ COMPARE: LIKE pattern (slow, no index) ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.application_logs`
WHERE log_message LIKE '%error%'
   OR log_message LIKE '%timeout%'
   OR log_message LIKE '%connection refused%';
-- This scans every byte of every row — extremely slow on large tables
-- SEARCH() with an index is 100–1000× faster

7.7 JSON Operations — Semi-Structured Data

SQLjson_operations.sql
-- ═══ BigQuery native JSON type (recommended) ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.events` (
    event_id STRING,
    event_ts TIMESTAMP,
    payload  JSON       -- Native JSON column type
);

-- ═══ QUERY JSON fields with dot notation ═══
SELECT
    event_id,
    JSON_VALUE(payload, '$.user.id') AS user_id,
    JSON_VALUE(payload, '$.action') AS action,
    CAST(JSON_VALUE(payload, '$.amount') AS NUMERIC) AS amount
FROM `project.dataset.events`
WHERE JSON_VALUE(payload, '$.action') = 'purchase';

-- ═══ JSON_QUERY — Extract nested object/array ═══
SELECT
    event_id,
    JSON_QUERY(payload, '$.items') AS items_array,
    JSON_QUERY(payload, '$.user') AS user_object
FROM `project.dataset.events`;

-- ═══ JSON_QUERY_ARRAY — Iterate over JSON arrays ═══
SELECT
    event_id,
    item
FROM `project.dataset.events`,
UNNEST(JSON_QUERY_ARRAY(payload, '$.items')) AS item;

-- ═══ LAX vs strict JSON extraction ═══
SELECT
    JSON_VALUE(payload, '$.missing_field'),  -- Returns NULL if missing (lax)
    STRING(payload.user.name),              -- Dot notation (BigQuery extension)
    INT64(payload.amount)                   -- Direct type extraction
FROM `project.dataset.events`;
⚙️ Engineering Insight — JSON vs STRUCT: When to Use Which Use STRUCT when the schema is known and stable — STRUCT columns are fully typed, support clustering, and have the best query performance. Use JSON when the schema is unknown, evolving, or semi-structured — JSON provides schema flexibility at the cost of slightly slower queries and no clustering support. In practice: use STRUCT for your core data model (orders, customers, products) and JSON for extensible metadata or raw event payloads that vary across event types.

7.8 BigQuery ML — Train Models in SQL

BigQuery ML lets you create, train, evaluate, and predict with ML models using standard SQL — no Python, no TensorFlow, no data export needed. Data stays in BigQuery, models run on BigQuery compute, and predictions are just SQL queries. This dramatically lowers the barrier to ML for data engineers and analysts.

SQLbigquery_ml.sql
-- ═══ LOGISTIC REGRESSION — Churn prediction ═══
CREATE OR REPLACE MODEL `project.dataset.churn_model`
OPTIONS (
    model_type        = 'LOGISTIC_REG',
    input_label_cols  = ['churned'],
    auto_class_weights = TRUE,      -- Handle imbalanced classes
    max_iterations    = 20,
    l2_reg           = 0.01
) AS
SELECT
    country,
    plan_type,
    tenure_months,
    monthly_spend,
    support_tickets_last_90d,
    churned                         -- Label: TRUE/FALSE
FROM `project.dataset.customer_features`
WHERE snapshot_date = '2024-03-01';

-- ═══ EVALUATE model performance ═══
SELECT * FROM ML.EVALUATE(MODEL `project.dataset.churn_model`);
-- Returns: precision, recall, accuracy, f1_score, log_loss, roc_auc

-- ═══ PREDICT on new data ═══
SELECT
    customer_id,
    predicted_churned,
    predicted_churned_probs[OFFSET(0)].prob AS churn_probability
FROM ML.PREDICT(
    MODEL `project.dataset.churn_model`,
    (SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.active_customers`)
)
WHERE predicted_churned_probs[OFFSET(0)].prob > 0.7
ORDER BY churn_probability DESC;

-- ═══ TIME-SERIES FORECASTING (ARIMA_PLUS) ═══
CREATE OR REPLACE MODEL `project.dataset.revenue_forecast`
OPTIONS (
    model_type            = 'ARIMA_PLUS',
    time_series_timestamp_col = 'sale_date',
    time_series_data_col  = 'daily_revenue',
    auto_arima            = TRUE,
    holiday_region        = 'US'       -- Auto-detect US holidays
) AS
SELECT sale_date, SUM(revenue) AS daily_revenue
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY sale_date;

-- Forecast next 30 days
SELECT * FROM ML.FORECAST(
    MODEL `project.dataset.revenue_forecast`,
    STRUCT(30 AS horizon, 0.95 AS confidence_level)
);

-- ═══ XGBOOST CLASSIFIER ═══
CREATE OR REPLACE MODEL `project.dataset.fraud_detector`
OPTIONS (
    model_type = 'BOOSTED_TREE_CLASSIFIER',
    input_label_cols = ['is_fraud'],
    num_parallel_tree = 5,
    max_tree_depth = 6,
    subsample = 0.8,
    auto_class_weights = TRUE
) AS
SELECT * EXCEPT(transaction_id)
FROM `project.dataset.transaction_features`;

BigQuery ML Model Types

Model TypeSQL NameUse Case
Linear RegressionLINEAR_REGPredict numeric values (revenue, price)
Logistic RegressionLOGISTIC_REGBinary/multi-class classification (churn, fraud)
K-Means ClusteringKMEANSCustomer segmentation, anomaly detection
XGBoost ClassifierBOOSTED_TREE_CLASSIFIERHigh-accuracy classification (fraud, conversion)
XGBoost RegressorBOOSTED_TREE_REGRESSORHigh-accuracy numeric prediction (LTV, demand)
DNN ClassifierDNN_CLASSIFIERDeep learning classification
ARIMA+ ForecastingARIMA_PLUSTime-series forecasting with holidays
Matrix FactorizationMATRIX_FACTORIZATIONRecommendation systems
AutoMLAUTOML_CLASSIFIER/REGRESSORAutomated feature engineering + model selection
Imported TensorFlowIMPORTED_TENSORFLOWImport TF SavedModel → predict in SQL

7.9 Approximate Functions — Trading Accuracy for Speed

SQLapproximate_functions.sql
-- ═══ APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT — ~100× faster than COUNT(DISTINCT) ═══
SELECT
    country,
    APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT(customer_id) AS approx_unique_customers,
    COUNT(DISTINCT customer_id) AS exact_unique_customers
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY country;
-- approx is ~1% error but 100× faster on large tables
-- Use for dashboards; use exact for financial reporting

-- ═══ APPROX_QUANTILES — Fast percentile calculation ═══
SELECT
    country,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(revenue, 100)[OFFSET(50)] AS p50_median,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(revenue, 100)[OFFSET(90)] AS p90,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(revenue, 100)[OFFSET(95)] AS p95,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(revenue, 100)[OFFSET(99)] AS p99
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY country;

-- ═══ APPROX_TOP_COUNT — Top-N most frequent values ═══
SELECT
    APPROX_TOP_COUNT(product_category, 10) AS top_categories
FROM `project.dataset.sales`;
-- Returns array of {value, count} for top 10 categories
💰 Cost Tip — APPROX Functions Save Compute COUNT(DISTINCT customer_id) on 1B rows requires maintaining a full hash set in memory — expensive in both slot-time and shuffle. APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT uses HyperLogLog++ with ~1% error but 100× less memory and compute. For dashboard queries where exact precision isn't needed, APPROX functions reduce both cost (slot-time on Editions) and latency. Rule: use APPROX for dashboards and exploration; use exact for financial/compliance reporting.

7.10 Scripting and Stored Procedures

SQLscripting_procedures.sql
-- ═══ MULTI-STATEMENT SCRIPTING ═══
DECLARE target_date DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE() - 1;
DECLARE row_count INT64;

-- Step 1: Load data
LOAD DATA OVERWRITE `project.staging.daily_sales`
PARTITION BY sale_date
FROM FILES (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris = [CONCAT('gs://bucket/sales/', FORMAT_DATE('%Y/%m/%d', target_date), '/*.parquet')]
);

-- Step 2: Validate
SET row_count = (SELECT COUNT(*) FROM `project.staging.daily_sales`
    WHERE sale_date = target_date);

IF row_count = 0 THEN
    RAISE USING MESSAGE = CONCAT('No data loaded for ', CAST(target_date AS STRING));
END IF;

-- Step 3: Transform
CREATE OR REPLACE TABLE `project.marts.daily_summary`
PARTITION BY sale_date AS
SELECT sale_date, country, SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue
FROM `project.staging.daily_sales`
WHERE sale_date = target_date
GROUP BY 1, 2;

SELECT CONCAT('Loaded ', CAST(row_count AS STRING), ' rows for ', CAST(target_date AS STRING));

-- ═══ STORED PROCEDURE ═══
CREATE OR REPLACE PROCEDURE `project.dataset.sp_daily_load`(
    IN target DATE
)
BEGIN
    MERGE INTO `project.marts.sales_summary` t
    USING (SELECT * FROM `project.staging.daily` WHERE sale_date = target) s
    ON t.sale_date = s.sale_date AND t.country = s.country
    WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET t.revenue = s.revenue
    WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT (sale_date, country, revenue)
        VALUES (s.sale_date, s.country, s.revenue);
END;

-- Call the procedure
CALL `project.dataset.sp_daily_load`('2024-03-15');

7.11 Remote Functions — Call Cloud Functions from SQL

SQLremote_functions.sql
-- ═══ CREATE remote function (calls Cloud Function/Cloud Run) ═══
CREATE FUNCTION `project.dataset.sentiment_score`(text STRING)
RETURNS FLOAT64
REMOTE WITH CONNECTION `project.us.my-connection`
OPTIONS (
    endpoint = 'https://my-function-xyz.run.app',
    max_batching_rows = 1000     -- Batch rows for efficiency
);

-- ═══ USE in queries ═══
SELECT
    review_id,
    review_text,
    sentiment_score(review_text) AS sentiment
FROM `project.dataset.product_reviews`
WHERE review_date = '2024-03-15';
-- Each row's review_text is sent to the Cloud Function in batches
-- Results returned inline as a column
-- Use cases: NLP, geocoding, custom ML inference, API enrichment

7.12 Advanced Analytical Patterns

SQLadvanced_analytics.sql
-- ═══ FUNNEL ANALYSIS (conditional aggregation) ═══
SELECT
    COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'view' THEN user_id END) AS step1_view,
    COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'cart' THEN user_id END) AS step2_cart,
    COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'checkout' THEN user_id END) AS step3_checkout,
    COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'purchase' THEN user_id END) AS step4_purchase,
    ROUND(SAFE_DIVIDE(
        COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'purchase' THEN user_id END),
        COUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step = 'view' THEN user_id END)
    ) * 100, 2) AS overall_conversion_pct
FROM `project.dataset.funnel_events`
WHERE event_date = '2024-03-15';

-- ═══ COHORT RETENTION (self-join on signup month) ═══
WITH cohorts AS (
    SELECT
        user_id,
        DATE_TRUNC(signup_date, MONTH) AS cohort_month,
        DATE_TRUNC(activity_date, MONTH) AS activity_month
    FROM `project.dataset.user_activity`
)
SELECT
    cohort_month,
    DATE_DIFF(activity_month, cohort_month, MONTH) AS months_since_signup,
    COUNT(DISTINCT user_id) AS active_users,
    ROUND(SAFE_DIVIDE(
        COUNT(DISTINCT user_id),
        FIRST_VALUE(COUNT(DISTINCT user_id)) OVER (
            PARTITION BY cohort_month ORDER BY activity_month
        )
    ) * 100, 1) AS retention_pct
FROM cohorts
GROUP BY cohort_month, activity_month
ORDER BY cohort_month, months_since_signup;

-- ═══ GEOGRAPHY — Distance between coordinates ═══
SELECT
    store_name,
    customer_name,
    ST_DISTANCE(
        ST_GEOGPOINT(store_lng, store_lat),
        ST_GEOGPOINT(customer_lng, customer_lat)
    ) / 1000 AS distance_km
FROM `project.dataset.deliveries`
WHERE ST_DISTANCE(
    ST_GEOGPOINT(store_lng, store_lat),
    ST_GEOGPOINT(customer_lng, customer_lat)
) < 50000;   -- Within 50km

-- ═══ EXPORT DATA to GCS ═══
EXPORT DATA
OPTIONS (
    uri = 'gs://bucket/exports/sales_*.parquet',
    format = 'PARQUET',
    overwrite = TRUE,
    compression = 'SNAPPY'
) AS
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.sales`
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15';

-- ═══ WILDCARD TABLES — Query across table shards ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.events_*`
WHERE _TABLE_SUFFIX BETWEEN '20240301' AND '20240315';
-- Queries events_20240301, events_20240302, ... events_20240315
🎯 Interview Tip — "Write a cohort retention query" This is a classic data engineering/analytics interview question. The key pattern: (1) Determine each user's cohort (signup month). (2) Find each user's activity months. (3) Calculate months_since_signup. (4) Count distinct users per cohort × month pair. (5) Divide by cohort size (Month 0 count) for retention percentage. Using FIRST_VALUE window function for the cohort size denominator shows advanced SQL knowledge.

7.13 BigQuery SQL Pattern Quick Reference

PatternKey SQLUse Case
Top-N per groupQUALIFY ROW_NUMBER() OVER (...) <= NTop products per category, top customers per region
DeduplicationQUALIFY ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY key ORDER BY ts DESC) = 1CDC dedup, keep latest version per key
Running totalSUM(x) OVER (ORDER BY date ROWS UNBOUNDED PRECEDING)Cumulative revenue, running balance
Moving averageAVG(x) OVER (ORDER BY date ROWS 6 PRECEDING)7-day moving average for dashboards
Day-over-day changex - LAG(x) OVER (ORDER BY date)Daily growth, change detection
PivotPIVOT (SUM(x) FOR col IN (...))Rows → columns (quarterly reports)
Unnest arrayFROM table, UNNEST(array_col) AS itemQuery denormalized ARRAY fields
Array contains'value' IN UNNEST(array_col)Filter rows by array membership
Full-text searchSEARCH(col, 'keywords')Log search, ticket search (with SEARCH index)
Approx distinctAPPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT(col)Fast unique counts for dashboards (~1% error)
Funnel analysisCOUNT(DISTINCT CASE WHEN step='x' THEN id END)Conversion funnel (view → cart → purchase)
Cohort retentionDATE_DIFF(activity, cohort, MONTH)Monthly retention curves per signup cohort
ML predictionML.PREDICT(MODEL ..., (SELECT ...))Churn prediction, forecasting in SQL
Remote functionCREATE FUNCTION ... REMOTE WITH CONNECTIONCall Cloud Function for NLP/geocoding from SQL
Export to GCSEXPORT DATA OPTIONS (uri=..., format='PARQUET')Export query results as Parquet to GCS

Section 7 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Window functionsCompute over related rows without GROUP BYUse for running totals, ranks, lag/lead, moving averages. Most powerful SQL feature.
QUALIFYFilter on window functions — no subquery neededTop-N per group + deduplication = two most common production patterns.
PIVOT/UNPIVOTReshape data (rows ↔ columns)Use for quarterly/monthly report pivots. UNPIVOT for normalizing wide tables.
STRUCT/ARRAYDenormalized query patterns on nested dataUNNEST for flattening, ARRAY_AGG for building, dot notation for STRUCT access.
SEARCH indexFull-text search: 100–1000× faster than LIKECreate SEARCH INDEX on log/text tables. Use SEARCH() instead of LIKE '%x%'.
BigQuery MLTrain + predict with SQL — no Python neededLOGISTIC_REG for churn, ARIMA_PLUS for forecasting, BOOSTED_TREE for accuracy.
APPROX functions~1% error, 100× faster for cardinality/quantilesAPPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT for dashboards. Exact for financial reporting.
ScriptingMulti-statement procedures in BigQueryDECLARE variables, IF/ELSE logic, CALL stored procedures for complex ETL.
Part II — Section 8

BigQuery Performance

Query optimization checklist, execution plan analysis, INFORMATION_SCHEMA monitoring, anti-patterns, and the complete performance tuning playbook.

Section 8 — BigQuery Performance — Query Optimization, INFORMATION_SCHEMA, Execution Plans

8.1 The BigQuery Performance Model

BigQuery performance is governed by three primary metrics: bytes scanned (determines on-demand cost and I/O volume), bytes shuffled (determines data redistribution overhead for JOINs/GROUP BYs), and slot-time consumed (determines compute cost on Editions and wall-clock duration). Every optimization technique targets one or more of these three metrics.

Unlike traditional databases where indexing and query plan hints dominate performance tuning, BigQuery optimization centers on data modeling (Section 4), query structure (this section), and capacity management (Section 5). There are no B-tree indexes, no query plan hints, no manual statistics updates. Instead, BigQuery provides automatic columnar statistics, automatic reclustering, and a managed shuffle service — and your job is to write queries that take full advantage of these systems.

MetricWhat It MeasuresHow to Reduce ItImpact
Bytes scannedCapacitor data read from Colossus by leaf workersPartition filter, clustering, column selection, materialized viewsDetermines on-demand cost ($6.25/TB)
Bytes shuffledData moved between workers for JOINs, GROUP BY, WINDOWDenormalize (STRUCT/ARRAY), reduce SELECT columns in JOINs, avoid cross-JOINsDominates cost in complex multi-table queries
Slot-time (ms)Total CPU+memory consumed across all stagesAll the above + avoid expensive functions on full tableDetermines Editions cost and wall-clock time
Wall-clock timeEnd-to-end query durationCombination of above + sufficient slot capacityUser-perceived latency for dashboards

8.2 The Complete Query Optimization Checklist

The following checklist is ordered by impact — highest-impact optimizations first. Apply them in order for any underperforming query. Each item maps to a specific architectural component (see Section 3.12).

#OptimizationWhyHowImpact
1Partition filterSkip entire date rangesWHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15' on partitioned table10–365× less scan
2require_partition_filterPrevent accidental full scansTable option — rejects queries without date filterPrevents worst-case cost
3Cluster by filtered columnsSkip Capacitor files via min/maxCLUSTER BY country, category2–100× less within partition
4SELECT only needed columnsColumnar storage reads only referenced colsNever SELECT * — list specific columns5–50× less I/O
5Denormalize with STRUCT/ARRAYEliminate JOINs → eliminate shuffleEmbed child entities as nested fields2–10× faster + cheaper
6Use APPROX functionsHyperLogLog vs exact hash setAPPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT() instead of COUNT(DISTINCT)10–100× faster for cardinality
7Materialized viewsPre-compute common aggregationsCREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW — auto-refreshed, auto-routedZero cost for matching queries
8BI EngineIn-memory cache for hot dashboardsReserve BI Engine capacity — automatic acceleration10–100× faster (sub-second)
9Avoid ORDER BY without LIMITFull sort = single-node bottleneckAlways pair ORDER BY with LIMITPrevent sort-all bottleneck
10Avoid cross-region queriesData transfer across regions = slow + costlyKeep data and query project in same regionAvoid inter-region latency
11Use SEARCH index for textFull-text search vs LIKE scanCREATE SEARCH INDEX + SEARCH(col, 'terms')100–1000× faster for text
12Incremental modelsOnly process new dataDataform/dbt incremental — only INSERT new rows10–100× cheaper transforms
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — The Optimization Priority Stack Optimizations are not equal. Apply them in strict priority order: (1) Partition filter is always the single biggest win (10–365×). (2) Clustering stacks on top (2–100× within partition). (3) Column selection ensures columnar benefit (5–50×). (4) Denormalization eliminates shuffle (the costliest operation). Only after these four are exhausted should you consider materialized views, BI Engine, or approximate functions. In practice, items 1–4 solve 95% of BigQuery performance and cost problems.

8.3 Execution Plan Analysis — Reading the Execution Details

Every BigQuery query produces an execution plan visible in the Console under "Execution Details" tab. The plan shows stages (sequential steps), each with metrics: input/output rows, bytes read, bytes shuffled, slot-time, and wait time. Understanding these metrics is the foundation of performance debugging.

Figure 8.1 — Execution plan with three stages. Red flags indicate optimization opportunities; green signs confirm healthy query execution.

8.4 INFORMATION_SCHEMA — Production Monitoring Queries

BigQuery's INFORMATION_SCHEMA views provide real-time metadata about jobs, tables, reservations, and streaming. These are the primary monitoring tool for production BigQuery platforms — more useful than Cloud Monitoring dashboards because they're queryable with SQL and can power custom alerts.

8.4.1 — Top Expensive Queries (Cost Attribution)

SQLexpensive_queries.sql
-- ═══ TOP 20 MOST EXPENSIVE QUERIES (last 7 days) ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    user_email,
    SUBSTR(query, 1, 120) AS query_preview,
    ROUND(total_bytes_processed / POW(2,40), 4) AS tb_scanned,
    ROUND(total_bytes_processed / POW(2,40) * 6.25, 2) AS estimated_cost_usd,
    ROUND(total_slot_ms / 1000, 1) AS slot_seconds,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, SECOND) AS elapsed_sec,
    SAFE_DIVIDE(total_slot_ms,
        TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, MILLISECOND)) AS avg_slots
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 7 DAY)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY' AND state = 'DONE'
  AND error_result IS NULL
ORDER BY total_bytes_processed DESC
LIMIT 20;

8.4.2 — Cost by User / Team (Chargeback)

SQLcost_by_user.sql
-- ═══ DAILY COST BY USER (on-demand chargeback) ═══
SELECT
    DATE(creation_time) AS query_date,
    user_email,
    COUNT(*) AS query_count,
    ROUND(SUM(total_bytes_processed) / POW(2,40), 4) AS total_tb,
    ROUND(SUM(total_bytes_processed) / POW(2,40) * 6.25, 2) AS estimated_cost_usd,
    ROUND(SUM(total_slot_ms) / 3600000, 2) AS total_slot_hours
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 30 DAY)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY' AND state = 'DONE'
GROUP BY query_date, user_email
ORDER BY estimated_cost_usd DESC;

8.4.3 — High-Shuffle Queries (JOIN Optimization Targets)

SQLhigh_shuffle_queries.sql
-- ═══ QUERIES WITH EXCESSIVE SHUFFLE ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    user_email,
    SUBSTR(query, 1, 150) AS query_preview,
    ROUND(total_bytes_processed / POW(2,30), 2) AS gb_scanned,
    ROUND(total_bytes_shuffled / POW(2,30), 2) AS gb_shuffled,
    ROUND(SAFE_DIVIDE(total_bytes_shuffled, total_bytes_processed), 2) AS shuffle_ratio
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 7 DAY)
  AND total_bytes_shuffled > 0
  AND job_type = 'QUERY' AND state = 'DONE'
ORDER BY total_bytes_shuffled DESC
LIMIT 20;
-- shuffle_ratio > 1.0 = more bytes shuffled than read → definitely a JOIN problem
-- shuffle_ratio > 0.5 = consider denormalization to reduce shuffle

8.4.4 — Slot Contention Detection

SQLslot_contention.sql
-- ═══ DETECT QUERIES WAITING FOR SLOTS (queueing) ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    user_email,
    creation_time,
    start_time,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(start_time, creation_time, SECOND) AS queue_seconds,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, SECOND) AS execution_seconds,
    total_slot_ms,
    SUBSTR(query, 1, 100) AS query_preview
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 DAY)
  AND TIMESTAMP_DIFF(start_time, creation_time, SECOND) > 5
  AND job_type = 'QUERY'
ORDER BY queue_seconds DESC;
-- queue_seconds > 5 = definite slot contention
-- Fix: increase reservation baseline or autoscale_max_slots

8.4.5 — Table Storage Analysis (Find Optimization Targets)

SQLtable_storage_analysis.sql
-- ═══ LARGEST TABLES + OPTIMIZATION RECOMMENDATIONS ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    ROUND(total_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS logical_gb,
    ROUND(total_physical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS physical_gb,
    ROUND(total_logical_bytes / NULLIF(total_physical_bytes, 0), 1) AS compression_ratio,
    ROUND(active_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS active_gb,
    ROUND(long_term_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS longterm_gb,
    ROUND(time_travel_physical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS time_travel_gb,
    -- Monthly cost estimate (logical billing)
    ROUND(active_logical_bytes / POW(2,30) * 0.02
        + long_term_logical_bytes / POW(2,30) * 0.01, 2) AS monthly_cost_usd,
    -- Recommendation
    CASE
      WHEN total_logical_bytes / NULLIF(total_physical_bytes, 0) > 2
      THEN 'Switch to PHYSICAL billing (saves $)'
      ELSE 'Keep LOGICAL billing'
    END AS billing_recommendation
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.TABLE_STORAGE`
ORDER BY total_logical_bytes DESC;

-- ═══ TABLES MISSING PARTITION OR CLUSTERING ═══
SELECT
    t.table_name,
    t.table_type,
    ROUND(ts.total_logical_bytes / POW(2,30), 2) AS size_gb,
    CASE WHEN tp.partition_column IS NULL THEN '⚠️ NO PARTITION' ELSE tp.partition_column END AS partition_col,
    ARRAY_TO_STRING(
        ARRAY(SELECT column_name FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.COLUMNS` c
              WHERE c.table_name = t.table_name AND c.clustering_ordinal_position IS NOT NULL
              ORDER BY c.clustering_ordinal_position), ', '
    ) AS clustering_cols
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.TABLES` t
LEFT JOIN (SELECT table_name, column_name AS partition_column
           FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.COLUMNS`
           WHERE is_partitioning_column = 'YES') tp ON t.table_name = tp.table_name
LEFT JOIN `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.TABLE_STORAGE` ts ON t.table_name = ts.table_name
WHERE t.table_type = 'BASE TABLE'
  AND ts.total_logical_bytes > 1073741824   -- > 1 GB
ORDER BY ts.total_logical_bytes DESC;

8.4.6 — Slot Usage Timeline (Capacity Planning)

SQLslot_timeline.sql
-- ═══ HOURLY SLOT USAGE WITH PERCENTILES ═══
SELECT
    TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(period_start, HOUR) AS hour,
    ROUND(AVG(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60), 1) AS avg_slots,
    ROUND(MAX(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60), 1) AS peak_slots,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60, 100)[OFFSET(50)] AS p50,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(period_slot_ms / 1000 / 60, 100)[OFFSET(95)] AS p95,
    COUNT(DISTINCT job_id) AS concurrent_queries
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS_TIMELINE
WHERE period_start > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 7 DAY)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY'
GROUP BY hour
ORDER BY hour;
-- Use P50 for Editions baseline, P95×1.2 for autoscale max

8.4.7 — Failed Queries and Error Analysis

SQLfailed_queries.sql
-- ═══ RECENT FAILED QUERIES ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    user_email,
    creation_time,
    error_result.reason AS error_reason,
    error_result.message AS error_message,
    SUBSTR(query, 1, 200) AS query_preview
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 DAY)
  AND error_result IS NOT NULL
ORDER BY creation_time DESC
LIMIT 50;
-- Common errors: quotaExceeded, resourcesExceeded, invalidQuery
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Build an INFORMATION_SCHEMA Dashboard Production BigQuery platforms should have a dedicated monitoring dashboard built on INFORMATION_SCHEMA queries scheduled via Dataform or scheduled queries: (1) Daily cost report by user/team. (2) Top 10 expensive queries with links to optimization opportunities. (3) High-shuffle query alerts (shuffle_ratio > 0.5). (4) Slot contention alerts (queue_seconds > 5). (5) Tables missing partitions (tables > 1 GB without partitioning). (6) Storage optimization (compression ratio for physical billing candidates). Schedule these as Looker Studio dashboards refreshed daily.

8.5 Common Performance Anti-Patterns

⚠️ Anti-Pattern 1 — SELECT * (The Cardinal Sin) SELECT * FROM large_table reads every column from Colossus. On a 50-column, 10 TB table, this reads 10 TB even if you only need 2 columns. With column selection (SELECT country, revenue), you read ~400 GB — a 25× reduction. On on-demand pricing: $62.50 vs $2.50 per query. This is the most common and most costly mistake in BigQuery.
⚠️ Anti-Pattern 2 — Missing Partition Filter Querying a partitioned table without filtering on the partition key scans the entire table — all 365 days, all 10 TB. With require_partition_filter = TRUE, BigQuery rejects such queries. Without it, a single ad-hoc query from an analyst can cost $62.50 instead of $0.17.
⚠️ Anti-Pattern 3 — JOINing Large Tables Instead of Denormalizing SELECT * FROM fact_orders JOIN dim_customers ON ... shuffles both tables' data across workers. If both are 100 GB, you shuffle up to 200 GB. If you denormalize the customer as a STRUCT inside orders, the JOIN disappears entirely — zero shuffle. For frequently-joined dimensions, denormalization is always the better choice in BigQuery.
⚠️ Anti-Pattern 4 — ORDER BY Without LIMIT ORDER BY revenue DESC without LIMIT forces BigQuery to sort the entire result set on a single node — a bottleneck that defeats parallelism. Always pair with LIMIT: ORDER BY revenue DESC LIMIT 100. If you need the full sorted result, consider writing to a destination table with clustering instead.
⚠️ Anti-Pattern 5 — COUNT(DISTINCT) on High-Cardinality Columns COUNT(DISTINCT user_id) on 1 billion rows maintains a full hash set — extremely expensive in memory and slot-time. APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT(user_id) uses HyperLogLog++ with ~1% error but 100× less compute. Use exact only for financial/compliance; use approx for everything else.
⚠️ Anti-Pattern 6 — Repeated Full-Table Queries for Dashboards If 50 Looker dashboards each run SELECT country, SUM(revenue) FROM sales GROUP BY country every hour, that's 50 full scans. Solution: materialized view — BigQuery pre-computes the aggregation, auto-refreshes on data changes, and routes matching queries to the MV at zero cost. Or use BI Engine to cache hot data in RAM.

8.6 Materialized Views — Pre-Computed Aggregations

SQLmaterialized_views.sql
-- ═══ CREATE materialized view ═══
CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW `project.dataset.mv_daily_country_revenue`
PARTITION BY sale_date
CLUSTER BY country
AS
SELECT
    sale_date,
    country,
    SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue,
    COUNT(*) AS order_count,
    APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT(customer_id) AS unique_customers
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY sale_date, country;

-- BigQuery automatically:
-- 1. Refreshes the MV incrementally when base table changes
-- 2. Routes matching queries to MV instead of scanning base table
-- 3. Charges $0 for queries answered entirely from MV

-- ═══ This query automatically uses the MV (zero scan of base) ═══
SELECT country, total_revenue
FROM `project.dataset.sales`           -- queries base table
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
GROUP BY country;
-- BigQuery optimizer detects this matches the MV → reads MV instead
-- Bytes scanned: ~0 (from MV) instead of ~27 GB (from base)

-- ═══ Force refresh (normally automatic) ═══
CALL BQ.REFRESH_MATERIALIZED_VIEW('project.dataset.mv_daily_country_revenue');

-- ═══ Check MV freshness ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    last_refresh_time,
    refresh_watermark
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.MATERIALIZED_VIEWS`;
💰 Cost Tip — Materialized Views = Free Queries When a query can be answered entirely from a materialized view, BigQuery charges $0 for on-demand compute (only storage for the MV itself). For a Looker dashboard that runs the same aggregation 100 times/day, this means: without MV = 100 × 27 GB × $6.25/TB = $16.88/day. With MV = $0/day (just ~$0.02/day for MV storage). Over a year: $6,160 saved per dashboard. Create MVs for every commonly-queried aggregation pattern.

8.7 Dry Run — Estimate Cost Before Executing

BASHdry_run.sh
# ═══ CLI dry run — shows bytes scanned without executing ═══
bq query --dry_run --use_legacy_sql=false \
  'SELECT country, SUM(revenue)
   FROM `project.dataset.sales`
   WHERE sale_date = "2024-03-15"
   GROUP BY country'

# Output: Query successfully validated. Estimated 800 MB will be read.
# Cost estimate: 800 MB × $6.25/TB ≈ $0.005

# ═══ Compare with full table scan (no partition filter) ═══
bq query --dry_run --use_legacy_sql=false \
  'SELECT country, SUM(revenue)
   FROM `project.dataset.sales`
   GROUP BY country'

# Output: 10 TB will be read → $62.50
# The partition filter saved 99.99% of cost
PYTHONdry_run_python.py
# ═══ Python dry run — programmatic cost estimation ═══
from google.cloud import bigquery

client = bigquery.Client()
job_config = bigquery.QueryJobConfig(dry_run=True, use_query_cache=False)

query = """
    SELECT country, SUM(revenue)
    FROM `project.dataset.sales`
    WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
    GROUP BY country
"""

job = client.query(query, job_config=job_config)
bytes_estimate = job.total_bytes_processed
cost_estimate = bytes_estimate / (2**40) * 6.25

print(f"Bytes: {bytes_estimate / 2**20:.1f} MB")
print(f"Cost:  ${cost_estimate:.4f}")
# Integrate into CI/CD to reject PRs that exceed cost threshold
⚙️ Engineering Insight — CI/CD Cost Gates with Dry Run Production teams integrate dry run into their CI/CD pipeline: when a PR modifies a Dataform/dbt model, the CI step runs a dry run of the modified SQL and compares bytes scanned against a threshold. If the query would scan more than, say, 100 GB (indicating a missing partition filter or SELECT *), the PR is automatically blocked with a cost warning. This prevents expensive queries from reaching production. Implementation: Python script calling client.query(dry_run=True) on each modified .sql file, comparing total_bytes_processed against a configurable limit.

8.8 Query Caching — Free Results for Repeated Queries

BigQuery automatically caches query results for ~24 hours. If you run the same query against the same data (no changes to underlying table), BigQuery returns the cached result at zero cost — 0 bytes scanned, 0 slot-time. This is transparent: no configuration needed.

PropertyDetails
Cache duration~24 hours (evicted if table data changes)
Cache scopePer-user (same user, same project, same query text)
Cache invalidationAutomatic: any DML on base table invalidates cache
Cost of cached result$0 — 0 bytes scanned, 0 slot-time
Disable cachejob_config.use_query_cache = False or --nouse_cache
Does NOT cacheQueries with CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), randomness, streaming buffer reads
🎯 Interview Tip — "How does BigQuery caching work?" "BigQuery caches results per-user for ~24 hours. If the same query runs again and the underlying table hasn't changed, results return at zero cost — 0 bytes billed. Cache is invalidated when any DML modifies the base table. Queries using non-deterministic functions (CURRENT_TIMESTAMP, RAND) or reading from the streaming buffer bypass cache. This is transparent — no configuration needed. For dashboard workloads, caching + materialized views + BI Engine combine to minimize repeated computation."

8.9 Performance Debugging Flowchart

SymptomLikely CauseDiagnostic QueryFix
Query costs too muchMissing partition filter or SELECT *Dry run: check bytes estimateAdd partition filter + specify columns
Query is slow (>10s)Large scan or heavy shuffleINFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS: check bytes_read, bytes_shuffledAdd clustering, denormalize JOINs
Query waits before runningSlot contention (Editions)start_time - creation_time > 5sIncrease reservation baseline or autoscale max
High slot-time but fastHighly parallel — good signtotal_slot_ms >> elapsed_msNo action needed (efficient parallelism)
High slot-time AND slowData skew in one stageExecution plan: one stage dominatesRepartition data, avoid skewed JOIN keys
Dashboard always slowRepeated full scansSame query in JOBS 50+ times/dayCreate materialized view or enable BI Engine
Streaming data not visibleBuffer delay (normal)Check streaming buffer in table detailsWait ~seconds; or use Storage Write API committed
Query fails: resources exceededQuery too complex for available slotsCheck total_slot_ms in failed jobSimplify query, break into stages, or increase slots
