Part II — Section 9

BigQuery Security

Defense in depth — IAM, dataset/table ACLs, row-level security, column-level masking, authorized views, VPC Service Controls, CMEK, and audit logging.

Section 9 — BigQuery Security — Row-Level, Column-Level, VPC-SC, CMEK

9.1 BigQuery Security Model — Defense in Depth

BigQuery implements a multi-layered security model where each layer restricts access further. Outer layers control who can access which projects and datasets. Inner layers control which rows and columns a user can see within a table. Network-level controls (VPC-SC) prevent data exfiltration even if IAM is compromised. Encryption (CMEK) ensures data is protected at rest with customer-controlled keys.

This layered approach means no single misconfiguration exposes all data — each layer provides independent protection. Understanding all layers is essential for building compliant data platforms in regulated industries (finance, healthcare, government).

Figure 9.1 — BigQuery's six security layers: VPC-SC (network) → IAM (project/dataset) → Dataset ACLs → Table ACLs → Row-Level Security → Column-Level Security. CMEK and Audit Logging span all layers.

9.2 IAM Roles — Project and Dataset Level

BigQuery IAM follows GCP's standard role model: predefined roles (recommended) grant specific permissions, and can be bound at project, dataset, or table level. Custom roles can be created for fine-grained needs.

RoleScopePermissionsTypical Assignment
bigquery.adminProjectFull control: create/delete datasets, tables, jobs, reservationsPlatform admins only
bigquery.dataOwnerDatasetFull control on dataset's tables: create, delete, modify, grantData engineering team lead
bigquery.dataEditorDatasetRead + write tables, run DML, but cannot delete tables or grantData engineers, ETL service accounts
bigquery.dataViewerDatasetRead-only: query tables, list tables. Cannot write or modify.Analysts, Looker service account
bigquery.jobUserProjectRun query jobs (required to execute queries in a project)All BigQuery users
bigquery.userProjectjobUser + create datasets + list datasetsAnalysts needing sandbox datasets
bigquery.metadataViewerDatasetView table schemas and metadata — cannot read dataData catalog / governance tools
⚠️ Common Pitfall — Overly Broad IAM Grants The most common security mistake: granting bigquery.admin or roles/owner at the project level to analysts or service accounts. This gives them power to delete datasets, modify reservations, and export data to any destination. Instead: grant bigquery.dataViewer at the dataset level for read access, bigquery.jobUser at the project level for query execution, and nothing more. Use authorized views (Section 9.5) when you need to provide controlled access to sensitive data.
BASHiam_configuration.sh
# ═══ Grant dataViewer on a specific dataset ═══
bq update --dataset \
  --source=dataset_acl.json \
  project:sensitive_dataset

# dataset_acl.json:
# {
#   "access": [
#     {"role": "READER", "groupByEmail": "analysts@company.com"},
#     {"role": "WRITER", "groupByEmail": "data-eng@company.com"},
#     {"role": "OWNER", "groupByEmail": "data-admins@company.com"}
#   ]
# }

# ═══ Grant jobUser at project level (required to run queries) ═══
gcloud projects add-iam-policy-binding my-project \
  --member="group:analysts@company.com" \
  --role="roles/bigquery.jobUser"

# ═══ Terraform: Dataset-level IAM ═══
# resource "google_bigquery_dataset_iam_member" "viewer" {
#   dataset_id = "sensitive_dataset"
#   role       = "roles/bigquery.dataViewer"
#   member     = "group:analysts@company.com"
# }

9.3 Row-Level Security — Filter Rows by User

Row-level security (RLS) lets you restrict which rows a user can see within a table. A row access policy is a filter expression evaluated at query time — rows that don't match are completely invisible to the user. They don't appear in results, they're not counted in COUNT(*), and they can't be exported.

SQLrow_level_security.sql
-- ═══ CREATE row access policy — US analysts see only US data ═══
CREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY us_filter
ON `project.dataset.sales`
GRANT TO ("group:us-analysts@company.com")
FILTER USING (country = 'US');

-- ═══ EU analysts see only EU countries ═══
CREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY eu_filter
ON `project.dataset.sales`
GRANT TO ("group:eu-analysts@company.com")
FILTER USING (country IN ('DE', 'FR', 'UK', 'IT', 'ES'));

-- ═══ Admins see all rows (no filter) ═══
CREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY admin_all
ON `project.dataset.sales`
GRANT TO ("group:data-admins@company.com")
FILTER USING (TRUE);
-- FILTER USING (TRUE) = no restriction = see everything

-- ═══ BEHAVIOR: When US analyst runs: ═══
-- SELECT COUNT(*) FROM sales;
-- → Returns count of US rows ONLY. Non-US rows are invisible.
-- → Not filtered out — literally not visible in any form.

-- ═══ List policies on a table ═══
SELECT * FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.ROW_ACCESS_POLICIES`;

-- ═══ DROP a policy ═══
DROP ROW ACCESS POLICY us_filter ON `project.dataset.sales`;

-- ═══ Dynamic RLS using SESSION_USER() ═══
CREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY dynamic_filter
ON `project.dataset.sales`
GRANT TO ("allAuthenticatedUsers")
FILTER USING (
    region IN (
        SELECT allowed_region
        FROM `project.dataset.user_region_mapping`
        WHERE user_email = SESSION_USER()
    )
);
-- Filter based on a lookup table mapping users → allowed regions
⚙️ Engineering Insight — RLS Performance Impact Row-level security adds a filter predicate to every query on the protected table. For simple equality filters (country = 'US'), the performance impact is minimal — BigQuery applies the filter during the scan stage alongside partition/clustering pruning. For complex subquery-based filters (dynamic RLS with SESSION_USER()), the subquery executes once per query and can add 100–500ms overhead. To minimize impact: keep the mapping table small, partition it if possible, and prefer simple equality filters over subqueries.

9.4 Column-Level Security — Policy Tags and Data Masking

Column-level security restricts which columns a user can see. It's implemented via policy tags in Data Catalog — you create a taxonomy (e.g., "PII Classification"), define policy tags within it (e.g., "Email", "SSN", "Phone"), attach tags to BigQuery columns, and configure access: users without the tag's IAM permission see NULL or a masked value instead of the real data.

SQLcolumn_level_security.sql
-- ═══ STEP 1: Create taxonomy and policy tags (via Console or API) ═══
-- In Data Catalog:
--   Taxonomy: "PII Classification"
--     Policy tag: "EMAIL" (projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/101)
--     Policy tag: "PHONE" (projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/102)
--     Policy tag: "SSN"   (projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/103)

-- ═══ STEP 2: Apply policy tag to BQ column ═══
ALTER TABLE `project.dataset.customers`
ALTER COLUMN email
SET OPTIONS (
    policy_tags = ['projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/101']
);

ALTER TABLE `project.dataset.customers`
ALTER COLUMN phone
SET OPTIONS (
    policy_tags = ['projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/102']
);

ALTER TABLE `project.dataset.customers`
ALTER COLUMN ssn
SET OPTIONS (
    policy_tags = ['projects/project/locations/us/taxonomies/1/policyTags/103']
);

-- ═══ STEP 3: Grant fine-grained reader access (IAM) ═══
-- Grant datacatalog.categoryFineGrainedReader on the EMAIL policy tag
-- to group: pii-readers@company.com
-- Users WITHOUT this role see NULL for the email column

-- ═══ BEHAVIOR: Unauthorized user queries: ═══
-- SELECT name, email, phone FROM customers;
-- → Returns: name="Alice", email=NULL, phone=NULL
-- The user doesn't know the values exist — they see NULL.

-- ═══ DATA MASKING (alternative to NULL — shows partial value) ═══
-- Configure masking rules on the policy tag:
--   Email: "a***@company.com" (first char + mask + domain)
--   Phone: "***-***-1234" (last 4 digits visible)
--   SSN: "***-**-6789" (last 4 digits visible)
-- Set via Data Catalog API or Console → column masking rules
🎯 Interview Tip — "How do you protect PII in BigQuery?" Multi-layer approach: (1) DLP API scans tables to discover PII columns automatically (150+ detectors: email, phone, SSN, credit card). (2) Data Catalog policy tags classify discovered PII columns (taxonomy: PII → Email/Phone/SSN). (3) Column-level security enforces access — unauthorized users see NULL or masked values. (4) Row-level security restricts data by region/team. (5) VPC Service Controls prevent data export outside the perimeter. This layered approach satisfies GDPR, HIPAA, and SOC2 requirements.

9.5 Authorized Views — Controlled Access to Sensitive Data

An authorized view is a view that has been granted access to read from a dataset that the view's users don't have direct access to. The view itself has the permission — not the end user. This pattern lets you create curated, filtered, aggregated views of sensitive data without exposing the underlying tables.

SQLauthorized_views.sql
-- ═══ CREATE a view in the "public" dataset ═══
CREATE VIEW `project.public_dataset.safe_sales_summary` AS
SELECT
    sale_date,
    country,
    SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue,
    COUNT(*) AS order_count
-- Note: no customer_id, no PII, only aggregates
FROM `project.sensitive_dataset.sales`
GROUP BY sale_date, country;

-- ═══ AUTHORIZE the view to read from the sensitive dataset ═══
-- In the sensitive_dataset's ACL, add the VIEW as an authorized view:
-- bq update --dataset --source=auth_view.json project:sensitive_dataset
-- auth_view.json:
-- {
--   "access": [
--     ...(existing ACLs)...,
--     {"view": {"projectId": "project", "datasetId": "public_dataset",
--               "tableId": "safe_sales_summary"}}
--   ]
-- }

-- ═══ Grant analysts access to the VIEW (not the base table) ═══
-- Analysts can query public_dataset.safe_sales_summary
-- They CANNOT query sensitive_dataset.sales directly
-- The VIEW has authorization to read sensitive data on their behalf

-- ═══ PATTERN: Authorized view with row-level filtering ═══
CREATE VIEW `project.public_dataset.my_region_sales` AS
SELECT sale_date, country, product_id, revenue
FROM `project.sensitive_dataset.sales`
WHERE region = (
    SELECT allowed_region
    FROM `project.access_control.user_regions`
    WHERE user_email = SESSION_USER()
);
-- Each user sees only their region's data through the view
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — Three-Dataset Security Model Production platforms typically use three dataset tiers: (1) sensitive_dataset: raw data with PII. Only service accounts and data engineers have access. Column-level security on PII columns. (2) staging_dataset: Dataform/dbt models that read from sensitive, apply transforms, strip PII. Data engineering team has access. (3) public_dataset: Authorized views and aggregated tables. Analysts and Looker have access. This pattern ensures PII is never directly accessible to analysts — they only see aggregated or anonymized data through authorized views.

9.6 VPC Service Controls — Network-Level Data Exfiltration Prevention

VPC Service Controls (VPC-SC) create a security perimeter around GCP resources that restricts data movement across project boundaries. Even if a user has IAM permission to read BigQuery data, VPC-SC can prevent them from copying it to an external project or exporting it to a non-approved GCS bucket.

This is the most powerful security control for preventing data exfiltration. IAM controls who can access data. VPC-SC controls where data can go. Together they provide defense in depth against both unauthorized access and authorized-but-inappropriate data movement.

What VPC-SC Prevents

Attack ScenarioWithout VPC-SCWith VPC-SC
Compromised service account exports BQ data to attacker's project✗ Succeeds — SA has dataViewer permission✓ Blocked — destination outside perimeter
Employee copies sensitive data to personal GCS bucket✗ Succeeds if they have GCS write permission✓ Blocked — personal bucket outside perimeter
Malicious insider runs EXPORT DATA to external location✗ Succeeds with jobUser + storage write✓ Blocked — export target outside perimeter
Cross-project query copies data to less-secure project✗ Succeeds with dataViewer on both projects✓ Blocked — target project outside perimeter
BASHvpc_service_controls.sh
# ═══ CREATE access policy (org-level) ═══
gcloud access-context-manager policies create \
  --organization=123456789 \
  --title="Data Platform Policy"

# ═══ CREATE service perimeter ═══
gcloud access-context-manager perimeters create data-perimeter \
  --title="Data Platform Perimeter" \
  --resources="projects/data-prod,projects/data-staging" \
  --restricted-services="bigquery.googleapis.com,storage.googleapis.com" \
  --policy=POLICY_ID

# Now: data in data-prod and data-staging cannot be exported
# to any project outside the perimeter — even with valid IAM.
# BigQuery queries and GCS operations are restricted to within perimeter.

# ═══ Allow specific cross-perimeter access (ingress/egress rules) ═══
# For Looker Cloud (needs to read BQ): create an ingress rule
# For data sharing with a partner: create an egress rule with conditions

# ═══ Terraform: VPC-SC Perimeter ═══
# resource "google_access_context_manager_service_perimeter" "data" {
#   parent = "accessPolicies/${var.policy_id}"
#   name   = "accessPolicies/${var.policy_id}/servicePerimeters/data"
#   title  = "Data Platform Perimeter"
#   status {
#     resources           = ["projects/${var.data_prod_number}"]
#     restricted_services = ["bigquery.googleapis.com", "storage.googleapis.com"]
#   }
# }
⚠️ Common Pitfall — VPC-SC Breaks Things If Not Planned VPC-SC is very restrictive by default — it blocks ALL cross-perimeter access, including legitimate use cases. Common breakages when enabling VPC-SC: (1) Looker/Looker Studio can't reach BigQuery (Looker runs outside your perimeter). Fix: ingress rule for Looker's service identity. (2) Cloud Composer can't reach BigQuery (Composer runs on GKE which may be outside perimeter). Fix: include Composer's project in perimeter or add ingress rule. (3) Fivetran/Airbyte can't write to BigQuery (external SaaS). Fix: ingress rule for connector's service account. Always test VPC-SC in a dry-run perimeter first — it logs violations without blocking them.

9.7 CMEK — Customer-Managed Encryption Keys

By default, all BigQuery data is encrypted at rest with Google-managed keys (AES-256). With CMEK (Customer-Managed Encryption Keys), you create and manage the encryption keys in Cloud KMS, giving you control over key creation, rotation, and revocation. If you revoke the key, BigQuery cannot read the data — effectively destroying access without deleting data.

SQLcmek_configuration.sql
-- ═══ CREATE BigQuery dataset with CMEK ═══
CREATE SCHEMA `project.encrypted_dataset`
OPTIONS (
    location            = 'US',
    default_kms_key_name = 'projects/project/locations/us/keyRings/bq-ring/cryptoKeys/bq-key'
);
-- All tables in this dataset encrypted with YOUR key, not Google's

-- ═══ CREATE table with explicit CMEK ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.encrypted_dataset.sensitive_data`
(
    id      INT64,
    name    STRING,
    ssn     STRING,
    revenue NUMERIC
)
OPTIONS (
    kms_key_name = 'projects/project/locations/us/keyRings/bq-ring/cryptoKeys/bq-key'
);

-- ═══ Key rotation (automatic via Cloud KMS) ═══
-- gcloud kms keys update bq-key \
--   --keyring=bq-ring --location=us \
--   --rotation-period=90d \
--   --next-rotation-time=$(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ -d "+90 days")

-- ═══ Revoke key → data becomes inaccessible ═══
-- gcloud kms keys versions disable 1 \
--   --key=bq-key --keyring=bq-ring --location=us
-- → All queries on this dataset now fail with "key is disabled"
-- → Data is NOT deleted — just inaccessible until key is re-enabled
PropertyGoogle-Managed Keys (default)CMEK
Who manages keysGoogle — fully automaticYou — via Cloud KMS
Key rotationAutomatic (Google's schedule)You configure rotation (e.g., 90 days)
Key revocationNot possible (Google always has access)You can disable/destroy key → data inaccessible
ComplianceBasic complianceRequired for HIPAA, FedRAMP, PCI-DSS, SOC2
CostFreeCloud KMS key cost (~$0.06/key/month + $0.03/10K ops)
Edition requirementAnyEnterprise or Enterprise Plus

9.8 Audit Logging — Who Did What, When

BigQuery generates Cloud Audit Logs for all administrative and data access operations. These logs are essential for security monitoring, compliance (SOC2, HIPAA), and incident investigation.

Log TypeWhat It CapturesEnabled By Default?
Admin ActivityDataset/table creation, deletion, IAM changes, reservation changes✅ Always on (cannot be disabled)
Data AccessQueries run, data read, data exported, streaming inserts❌ Must be enabled per project
System EventAutomatic operations (reclustering, long-term pricing transition)✅ Always on
BASHaudit_logging.sh
# ═══ Enable Data Access audit logs for BigQuery ═══
gcloud projects get-iam-policy my-project --format=json > policy.json
# Edit policy.json to add:
# "auditConfigs": [
#   {
#     "service": "bigquery.googleapis.com",
#     "auditLogConfigs": [
#       {"logType": "DATA_READ"},
#       {"logType": "DATA_WRITE"}
#     ]
#   }
# ]
gcloud projects set-iam-policy my-project policy.json

# ═══ Route audit logs to BigQuery for analysis ═══
gcloud logging sinks create bq-audit-sink \
  bigquery.googleapis.com/projects/my-project/datasets/audit_logs \
  --log-filter='resource.type="bigquery_resource"'

# Now query audit logs in BigQuery:
# SELECT * FROM audit_logs.cloudaudit_googleapis_com_data_access
# WHERE timestamp > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 DAY)

9.9 DLP API Integration — Automated PII Discovery

The DLP (Data Loss Prevention) API automatically scans BigQuery tables and GCS files for sensitive data — detecting 150+ info types including email addresses, phone numbers, SSNs, credit card numbers, passport numbers, and more. DLP findings feed into Data Catalog policy tags for column-level security enforcement.

PYTHONdlp_scan.py
# ═══ Scan a BigQuery table for PII ═══
from google.cloud import dlp_v2

dlp = dlp_v2.DlpServiceClient()

inspect_config = {
    "info_types": [
        {"name": "EMAIL_ADDRESS"},
        {"name": "PHONE_NUMBER"},
        {"name": "CREDIT_CARD_NUMBER"},
        {"name": "US_SOCIAL_SECURITY_NUMBER"},
    ],
    "min_likelihood": "LIKELY",
}

storage_config = {
    "big_query_options": {
        "table_reference": {
            "project_id": "my-project",
            "dataset_id": "raw",
            "table_id": "customers"
        },
        "rows_limit": 10000,   # Sample rows for faster scan
        "sample_method": "RANDOM_START"
    }
}

response = dlp.create_dlp_job(request={
    "parent": "projects/my-project/locations/us",
    "inspect_job": {
        "inspect_config": inspect_config,
        "storage_config": storage_config,
        "actions": [{
            "save_findings": {
                "output_config": {
                    "table": {
                        "project_id": "my-project",
                        "dataset_id": "security",
                        "table_id": "dlp_findings"
                    }
                }
            }
        }]
    }
})
# Results saved to security.dlp_findings:
# → column_name: "email", info_type: "EMAIL_ADDRESS", likelihood: "VERY_LIKELY"
# → column_name: "phone", info_type: "PHONE_NUMBER", likelihood: "LIKELY"
# → Feed these findings into Data Catalog policy tags for enforcement

9.10 Production Security Checklist

#ActionHowWhy
1Least privilege IAMdataViewer per dataset, jobUser per project. Never roles/owner.Prevent unauthorized writes and deletions
2VPC Service ControlsPerimeter around all data projectsPrevent data exfiltration even with valid IAM
3CMEK encryptionCloud KMS keys for sensitive datasetsCustomer-controlled encryption (HIPAA, PCI, SOC2)
4Column-level securityPolicy tags on PII columnsUnauthorized users see NULL/masked for PII
5Row-level securityRow access policies per user groupUS analysts see only US data
6Authorized viewsViews with dataset-level authorizationControlled, aggregated access to sensitive data
7DLP scanningAutomated PII discovery via DLP APIFind and classify PII before it's exposed
8Audit loggingEnable Data Access logs, route to BQWho queried what, when (compliance, investigation)
9No public IPsPrivate Google Access on all subnetsNo data traverses public internet
10Service accountsPer-pipeline SA with minimal permissionsBlast radius: compromised SA affects only its pipeline
🎯 Interview Tip — "How do you implement data security in BigQuery?" Walk through the layers: "I implement defense in depth with six layers: (1) IAM at dataset level — least privilege, never project-wide admin. (2) Row-level security — filter rows by user group (US analysts see US data). (3) Column-level security via policy tags — PII columns show NULL to unauthorized users. (4) Authorized views — aggregated views that hide raw PII. (5) VPC Service Controls — perimeter prevents data leaving the project even with valid IAM. (6) CMEK — customer-managed encryption for regulated data. Plus DLP API for automated PII discovery and audit logging for compliance."

Section 9 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Defense in depth6 independent security layersVPC-SC → IAM → Dataset ACL → Table ACL → Row-level → Column-level
IAMLeast privilege at dataset leveldataViewer for reads, dataEditor for writes, jobUser for execution. Never roles/owner.
Row-level securityFilter rows by user/groupCREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY with FILTER USING. Invisible rows: not even in COUNT(*).
Column-level securityMask/restrict PII columnsPolicy tags in Data Catalog. Unauthorized users see NULL or masked values.
Authorized viewsView has permission, not the userCreate aggregated views in public dataset. Authorize to read from sensitive dataset.
VPC Service ControlsNetwork perimeter prevents exfiltrationBlock data export outside perimeter — even with valid IAM. Test with dry-run first.
CMEKCustomer-controlled encryption keysCloud KMS keys with rotation. Revoke key → data inaccessible. Enterprise Edition required.
DLP + AuditDiscover PII and track accessDLP API scans for PII → policy tags. Audit logs to BQ for compliance reporting.
Part II — Section 10

BigQuery Advanced

BI Engine, Materialized Views, BigLake, Omni, Remote Functions, Scheduled Queries, and Vector Search — extending BigQuery beyond traditional warehousing.

Section 10 — BigQuery Advanced — BI Engine, Materialized Views, BigLake, Omni

10.1 BigQuery's Extended Capabilities

BigQuery extends far beyond traditional data warehousing. It simultaneously functions as an in-memory analytics cache (BI Engine), a pre-computation engine (Materialized Views), a lakehouse query engine (BigLake with Iceberg/Delta), a cross-cloud analytics platform (Omni on AWS S3 / Azure Blob), a custom function gateway (Remote Functions calling Cloud Run/Functions), and a vector similarity search engine (Vector Search for embeddings). This section covers each capability with production-ready SQL, architecture context, and cost implications.

CapabilityWhat It DoesKey BenefitCost
BI EngineIn-memory cache for hot columnsSub-second dashboard queries (10–100× faster)~$40.50/GB/month reserved
Materialized ViewsPre-compute aggregations, auto-refresh$0 compute for matching queriesStorage only (~$0.02/GB/mo)
BigLakeQuery Iceberg/Delta/Parquet on GCS with BQ securityOpen table format + BQ row/column securityPer-TB scanned (like external tables)
OmniRun BQ queries on AWS S3 / Azure BlobSingle engine for multi-cloud analyticsEnterprise Plus + per-TB in remote cloud
Remote FunctionsCall Cloud Functions/Run from SQLNLP, geocoding, custom ML in SQL queriesCloud Function cost + BQ compute
Scheduled QueriesRun SQL on cron scheduleSimple recurring transforms without ComposerStandard BQ compute
Vector SearchNearest-neighbor search on embeddingsSemantic search, RAG, recommendationsStandard BQ compute

10.2 BI Engine — In-Memory Acceleration for Dashboards

BI Engine is an in-memory caching layer that sits between Dremel and Colossus. It automatically caches frequently-accessed columns in reserved RAM, providing sub-100ms query responses for dashboard workloads. No code changes needed — existing queries automatically accelerate when data is cached.

How BI Engine Works

  • You reserve capacity (in GB) per project per region — BI Engine allocates that amount of RAM
  • BigQuery automatically identifies hot columns from frequently-queried tables and caches them
  • When a query can be served from cache, it bypasses Colossus entirely — pure in-memory scan
  • If the query needs columns not in cache, it falls back to standard Dremel execution (transparent)
  • Cache is automatically invalidated when base table data changes (DML, streaming inserts)
SQLbi_engine.sql
-- ═══ CREATE BI Engine reservation (100 GB RAM cache) ═══
CREATE BI_CAPACITY `project.region-us.dashboard_cache`
OPTIONS (size_gb = 100);

-- ═══ CHECK BI Engine status and utilization ═══
SELECT *
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.BI_CAPACITIES;

-- ═══ CHECK if a specific query used BI Engine ═══
SELECT
    job_id,
    bi_engine_statistics.bi_engine_mode,
    -- FULL = entirely from cache (fastest)
    -- PARTIAL = some columns cached, rest from Colossus
    -- DISABLED = BI Engine not used (cache miss or unsupported query)
    bi_engine_statistics.bi_engine_reasons,
    total_bytes_processed,
    TIMESTAMP_DIFF(end_time, start_time, MILLISECOND) AS elapsed_ms
FROM `region-us`.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS
WHERE creation_time > TIMESTAMP_SUB(CURRENT_TIMESTAMP(), INTERVAL 1 HOUR)
  AND job_type = 'QUERY'
ORDER BY creation_time DESC;

-- ═══ DROP BI Engine reservation ═══
DROP BI_CAPACITY `project.region-us.dashboard_cache`;

BI Engine Sizing Guide

Dashboard WorkloadRecommended BI Engine SizeMonthly Cost
Small (1–5 dashboards, few tables)10–25 GB$405–$1,013
Medium (10–20 dashboards, moderate tables)50–100 GB$2,025–$4,050
Large (50+ dashboards, many hot tables)100–250 GB$4,050–$10,125
💰 Cost Tip — BI Engine ROI Calculation A Looker dashboard querying a 50 GB table 200 times/day on on-demand: 200 × 50 GB × $6.25/TB = $62.50/day = $1,875/month. With BI Engine (50 GB reservation at $2,025/month), those 200 queries return from cache at $0 compute cost. Net saving: $1,875 - $2,025 = -$150 (slightly more expensive for 1 dashboard). But add a second similar dashboard: savings = $3,750 - $2,025 = $1,725/month profit. BI Engine pays for itself at ~2 heavy dashboards. On Editions pricing, the savings are even more dramatic since you're freeing up slot-time for other queries.
⚠️ Common Pitfall — BI Engine Limitations BI Engine does NOT accelerate all queries. Unsupported patterns: (1) Queries with JOINs across many tables. (2) Queries on external tables / federated sources. (3) Queries with complex UDFs or procedural scripting. (4) Queries scanning columns that exceed the BI Engine reservation size. Check bi_engine_statistics.bi_engine_reasons in INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS to understand why a query wasn't accelerated. The most common reason: "INPUT_TOO_LARGE" — the queried columns exceed the reservation size.

10.3 Materialized Views — Pre-Computed Aggregations

Materialized views (MVs) are pre-computed query results that BigQuery automatically refreshes when the base table changes. When a user query matches an MV's definition (even partially), BigQuery automatically routes the query to the MV instead of scanning the base table — at zero additional compute cost on on-demand pricing.

How Materialized Views Work Internally

  • Incremental refresh: When base table changes, BigQuery only processes the delta (new/changed rows), not the entire table. This makes refresh fast and cheap.
  • Automatic query routing: The optimizer detects when a user query can be satisfied by an MV — even if the query doesn't reference the MV directly. Example: user queries SUM(revenue) ... GROUP BY country on the base table, and BigQuery silently reads from the MV instead.
  • Zero compute for matched queries: On on-demand pricing, queries answered from MVs are billed at 0 bytes scanned. On Editions, they consume minimal slot-time (reading pre-aggregated data).
  • Storage cost only: MVs incur storage cost (~$0.02/GB/month) for the pre-computed results. For aggregation MVs, this is typically tiny (e.g., a table with 1B rows grouped to 200 countries = a few KB).
SQLmaterialized_views.sql
-- ═══ CREATE materialized view — daily revenue by country ═══
CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW `project.dataset.mv_daily_country_revenue`
PARTITION BY sale_date
CLUSTER BY country
AS
SELECT
    sale_date,
    country,
    SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue,
    COUNT(*) AS order_count,
    APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT(customer_id) AS approx_unique_customers
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
GROUP BY sale_date, country;

-- ═══ This query AUTOMATICALLY uses the MV (zero scan of base) ═══
SELECT country, SUM(total_revenue)
FROM `project.dataset.sales`   -- queries BASE table
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
GROUP BY country;
-- BigQuery optimizer detects this matches MV definition
-- → Reads from MV instead of scanning base table
-- → On-demand: $0 compute. Editions: minimal slot-time.

-- ═══ MV with complex aggregations ═══
CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW `project.dataset.mv_hourly_metrics`
PARTITION BY DATE(event_hour)
AS
SELECT
    TIMESTAMP_TRUNC(event_ts, HOUR) AS event_hour,
    event_type,
    COUNT(*) AS event_count,
    SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue,
    AVG(latency_ms) AS avg_latency,
    APPROX_QUANTILES(latency_ms, 100)[OFFSET(95)] AS p95_latency
FROM `project.dataset.events`
GROUP BY event_hour, event_type;

-- ═══ MANUAL REFRESH (normally automatic) ═══
CALL BQ.REFRESH_MATERIALIZED_VIEW('project.dataset.mv_daily_country_revenue');

-- ═══ CHECK MV freshness ═══
SELECT
    table_name,
    last_refresh_time,
    refresh_watermark,
    enable_refresh
FROM `project.dataset.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.MATERIALIZED_VIEWS`;

-- ═══ CONFIGURE refresh settings ═══
ALTER MATERIALIZED VIEW `project.dataset.mv_daily_country_revenue`
SET OPTIONS (
    enable_refresh = TRUE,
    refresh_interval_minutes = 30   -- Refresh every 30 min (default: auto)
);

Materialized View Limitations

SupportedNot Supported
✅ SUM, COUNT, AVG, MIN, MAX, APPROX_COUNT_DISTINCT✗ JOINs (single table only)
✅ GROUP BY with any columns✗ HAVING clause
✅ PARTITION BY on MV output✗ Non-deterministic functions (CURRENT_TIMESTAMP)
✅ CLUSTER BY on MV output✗ Window functions
✅ APPROX_QUANTILES✗ Subqueries in MV definition
✅ WHERE filters (static predicates)✗ UNION ALL (single table source only)
⚙️ Engineering Insight — MV Strategy for Dashboards The ideal MV strategy: identify your top 5–10 most-queried aggregation patterns (from INFORMATION_SCHEMA.JOBS — look for repeated GROUP BY queries). Create an MV for each pattern. Partition the MV by date and cluster by the most-filtered dimension. The result: dashboards that previously scanned TB per day now read from pre-computed MVs at zero compute cost. For a platform with 50 dashboards, this commonly saves $5,000–$20,000/month on on-demand pricing.

10.4 BigLake — Unified Storage API for Lakehouse

BigLake extends BigQuery to query data stored in GCS (Parquet, ORC, Avro, Iceberg, Delta) with native BigQuery security (row-level, column-level) applied to external data. This is BigQuery's answer to the lakehouse pattern — open table formats on cloud storage, queried by BigQuery with full security enforcement.

BigLake vs External Tables vs Native Tables

FeatureNative BQ TableExternal TableBigLake Table
Data locationColossus (BQ-managed)GCS (user-managed)GCS (user-managed)
FormatCapacitor (proprietary)Parquet/Avro/ORC/JSONIceberg/Delta/Parquet
Row/Column security✅ Full✗ None (or limited)✅ Full (same as native)
BigQuery DML✅ INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE✗ Read-only✅ Full DML (on Iceberg)
Clustering/partitioning✅ BQ-managed auto-recluster✗ Hive-style only✅ Iceberg partition pruning
PerformanceBest (Capacitor optimized)Limited (GCS throughput)Good (Iceberg metadata for pruning)
Multi-engine accessBigQuery onlyBigQuery + Spark (read)BigQuery + Spark + Trino (read/write)
Best forPrimary warehouse tablesAd-hoc GCS queriesLakehouse: open format + multi-engine + BQ security
SQLbiglake_tables.sql
-- ═══ CREATE BigLake managed Iceberg table ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
WITH CONNECTION `project.us.biglake-connection`
OPTIONS (
    table_format = 'ICEBERG',
    file_format  = 'PARQUET',
    storage_uri  = 'gs://bucket/iceberg/orders'
);

-- ═══ Full DML on Iceberg — same as native BQ tables ═══
INSERT INTO `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
VALUES (1001, '2024-03-15', 'US', 99.99);

UPDATE `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
SET revenue = 109.99
WHERE order_id = 1001;

DELETE FROM `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
WHERE order_date < '2023-01-01';

MERGE INTO `project.dataset.iceberg_orders` t
USING staging s ON t.order_id = s.order_id
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET t.revenue = s.revenue
WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT VALUES (s.order_id, s.order_date, s.country, s.revenue);

-- ═══ Apply row-level security (same as native) ═══
CREATE ROW ACCESS POLICY us_filter
ON `project.dataset.iceberg_orders`
GRANT TO ("group:us-team@company.com")
FILTER USING (country = 'US');

-- ═══ This Iceberg table is also accessible by Spark on Dataproc ═══
-- spark.read.format("iceberg").load("gs://bucket/iceberg/orders")
-- Multi-engine: BigQuery for SQL analytics, Spark for ML/complex ETL
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — BigLake Lakehouse The BigLake lakehouse pattern: store data in Apache Iceberg format on GCS. Use BigQuery BigLake tables for SQL analytics with full row/column security. Use Dataproc Spark for ML training and complex Python/Scala ETL. Both engines read/write the same Iceberg tables — no data duplication. BigQuery provides the governance (security, lineage, discovery via Dataplex), while Spark provides the ML and complex processing. This is the most sophisticated GCP data architecture and is suitable for Stage 4 (Data Mesh) maturity.

10.5 BigQuery Omni — Cross-Cloud Analytics

BigQuery Omni lets you run BigQuery queries on data stored in AWS S3 or Azure Blob Storage — without moving the data to GCP. BigQuery compute runs in the remote cloud (using Google-managed infrastructure in that cloud's region), reads the data locally, and returns results to the BigQuery console.

PropertyDetails
Data stays in placeData remains in S3/Azure — never copied to GCP
Compute runs remotelyGoogle deploys BQ compute in AWS/Azure region near your data
SQL compatibilityFull BigQuery SQL — same as querying native tables
Cross-cloud JOINCan JOIN Omni tables (AWS/Azure) with native BQ tables (GCP)
Edition requirementEnterprise Plus only
Use caseMulti-cloud orgs wanting single analytics engine without data migration
SQLomni_cross_cloud.sql
-- ═══ CREATE external connection to AWS S3 ═══
-- (Done via Console or API — provides AWS IAM role for BQ to assume)

-- ═══ CREATE BigLake table pointing to S3 data ═══
CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE `project.dataset.aws_sales`
WITH CONNECTION `project.aws-us-east-1.my-aws-connection`
OPTIONS (
    format     = 'PARQUET',
    uris       = ['s3://my-aws-bucket/sales/2024/*.parquet'],
    max_staleness = INTERVAL 1 HOUR    -- Cache metadata for 1 hour
);

-- ═══ Query AWS data with standard BigQuery SQL ═══
SELECT country, SUM(revenue) AS total
FROM `project.dataset.aws_sales`
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
GROUP BY country;
-- Compute runs in AWS us-east-1 → reads S3 locally → returns to BQ

-- ═══ Cross-cloud JOIN: AWS data + GCP data ═══
SELECT
    aws.country,
    aws.revenue AS aws_revenue,
    gcp.revenue AS gcp_revenue
FROM `project.dataset.aws_sales` aws
JOIN `project.dataset.gcp_sales` gcp
ON aws.country = gcp.country AND aws.sale_date = gcp.sale_date
WHERE aws.sale_date = '2024-03-15';
-- Single query spanning two clouds — unified analytics

10.6 Remote Functions — Call External Code from SQL

Remote functions let you call Cloud Functions or Cloud Run services directly from BigQuery SQL. Each row's column value is sent to the external endpoint (batched for efficiency), and the result is returned as a column in the query output. This enables NLP, geocoding, custom ML inference, API enrichment, and any custom logic without extracting data from BigQuery.

SQLremote_functions.sql
-- ═══ CREATE remote function pointing to Cloud Run ═══
CREATE FUNCTION `project.dataset.sentiment_score`(text STRING)
RETURNS FLOAT64
REMOTE WITH CONNECTION `project.us.my-connection`
OPTIONS (
    endpoint          = 'https://sentiment-service-xyz.run.app',
    max_batching_rows = 1000     -- Batch 1000 rows per HTTP call
);

-- ═══ USE remote function in queries ═══
SELECT
    review_id,
    review_text,
    sentiment_score(review_text) AS sentiment
FROM `project.dataset.product_reviews`
WHERE review_date = '2024-03-15';

-- ═══ GEOCODING remote function ═══
CREATE FUNCTION `project.dataset.geocode`(address STRING)
RETURNS STRUCT<lat FLOAT64, lng FLOAT64>
REMOTE WITH CONNECTION `project.us.my-connection`
OPTIONS (endpoint = 'https://geocoder-xyz.run.app');

SELECT
    customer_id,
    address,
    geocode(address).lat AS latitude,
    geocode(address).lng AS longitude
FROM `project.dataset.customers`
WHERE latitude IS NULL;  -- Only geocode missing addresses

-- ═══ PII MASKING remote function ═══
CREATE FUNCTION `project.dataset.mask_email`(email STRING)
RETURNS STRING
REMOTE WITH CONNECTION `project.us.my-connection`
OPTIONS (endpoint = 'https://masker-xyz.run.app');
-- Returns: "a***@company.com" — custom masking logic in Cloud Function
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Remote Function Performance Remote functions add significant latency — each batch of rows makes an HTTP call to the external endpoint. For 1M rows with max_batching_rows=1000, that's 1000 HTTP calls. Typical latency: 100–500ms per call × 1000 = 2–8 minutes total. To optimize: (1) Increase max_batching_rows (up to 10,000). (2) Run remote functions on a subset first and store results — don't call on every query. (3) Use remote functions in Dataform/dbt incremental models to process only new rows. (4) Pre-compute results in a dedicated enrichment table.

10.7 Scheduled Queries — Simple Recurring Transforms

Scheduled queries run SQL on a cron schedule — simpler than Cloud Composer for basic recurring transforms. They support parameterization (@run_date), destination table configuration, email notifications on failure, and retry policies.

SQLscheduled_queries.sql
-- ═══ CREATE scheduled query (via Console, CLI, or Terraform) ═══
-- Schedule: daily at 6:00 AM UTC
-- Destination: project.marts.daily_summary$20240315 (partition decorator)

-- Query with run_date parameter:
SELECT
    @run_date AS report_date,
    country,
    SUM(revenue) AS total_revenue,
    COUNT(DISTINCT customer_id) AS unique_customers
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
WHERE sale_date = @run_date
GROUP BY country;
-- @run_date is auto-populated with the scheduled execution date
BASHscheduled_query_cli.sh
# ═══ CREATE via bq CLI ═══
bq mk --transfer_config \
  --project_id=my-project \
  --data_source=scheduled_query \
  --target_dataset=marts \
  --display_name="Daily Revenue Summary" \
  --schedule="every day 06:00" \
  --params='{"query":"SELECT @run_date AS report_date, country, SUM(revenue) AS total FROM `project.dataset.sales` WHERE sale_date = @run_date GROUP BY country","destination_table_name_template":"daily_summary_{run_date}","write_disposition":"WRITE_TRUNCATE"}'

Scheduled Queries vs Cloud Composer vs Dataform

FeatureScheduled QueryDataformCloud Composer
ComplexitySingle SQL statementMulti-model DAG with dependenciesArbitrary DAG (BQ + Dataflow + GCS + ...)
Dependencies✗ None (single query)✅ Model-level (ref() function)✅ Task-level (any DAG shape)
Testing✗ No assertions✅ Assertions (data quality tests)✅ Custom test tasks
Cost$0 (BQ compute only)$0 (BQ compute only)$300–1000+/mo (always-on GKE)
Best forSimple recurring single-query transformsSQL-based ELT with dependencies + testsComplex multi-service orchestration

10.8 Vector Search — Embedding-Based Similarity

BigQuery supports vector similarity search on embedding columns — enabling semantic search, RAG (Retrieval Augmented Generation), and recommendation systems directly in SQL. Store embeddings as ARRAY<FLOAT64> columns, create a vector index, and use VECTOR_SEARCH to find nearest neighbors.

SQLvector_search.sql
-- ═══ TABLE with embedding column ═══
CREATE TABLE `project.dataset.product_embeddings` (
    product_id   STRING,
    product_name STRING,
    description  STRING,
    embedding    ARRAY<FLOAT64>   -- 768-dim embedding from Vertex AI
);

-- ═══ CREATE vector index ═══
CREATE VECTOR INDEX product_embedding_idx
ON `project.dataset.product_embeddings`(embedding)
OPTIONS (
    index_type     = 'IVF',         -- Inverted File Index
    distance_type  = 'COSINE',      -- or EUCLIDEAN, DOT_PRODUCT
    ivf_options    = '{"num_lists": 100}'
);

-- ═══ VECTOR SEARCH — Find similar products ═══
SELECT
    query.product_name AS query_product,
    base.product_name AS similar_product,
    distance
FROM VECTOR_SEARCH(
    TABLE `project.dataset.product_embeddings`,   -- base table
    'embedding',                                    -- embedding column
    (SELECT embedding FROM `project.dataset.product_embeddings`
     WHERE product_id = 'P123'),                     -- query embedding
    top_k => 10                                     -- return 10 nearest neighbors
);

-- ═══ GENERATE embedding using Vertex AI remote model ═══
SELECT *
FROM ML.GENERATE_TEXT_EMBEDDING(
    MODEL `project.dataset.embedding_model`,
    (SELECT description AS content FROM `project.dataset.products`),
    STRUCT(TRUE AS flatten_json_output)
);
🎯 Interview Tip — "Can BigQuery do semantic search?" "Yes — BigQuery supports vector similarity search natively. Store embeddings as ARRAY<FLOAT64> columns, create a VECTOR INDEX (IVF with cosine/euclidean distance), and use VECTOR_SEARCH() to find k-nearest neighbors. This enables RAG pipelines: store document embeddings in BigQuery, semantic-search with a query embedding, retrieve top-k documents, pass to an LLM for generation. You can even generate embeddings in BigQuery using ML.GENERATE_TEXT_EMBEDDING with a Vertex AI model. All in SQL — no Python needed."

10.9 When to Use Each Advanced Feature

ScenarioFeatureWhy
Dashboards are slow (>5 sec)BI EngineIn-memory cache → sub-100ms responses
Same aggregation queried 100× dailyMaterialized ViewPre-computed, auto-refreshed, $0 compute for matched queries
Need open table format (Iceberg)BigLakeBQ security on GCS data + multi-engine (Spark + BQ)
Data in AWS S3 / Azure BlobOmniQuery remote cloud data without moving it
Need NLP/geocoding/custom ML in SQLRemote FunctionsCall Cloud Functions from SQL, process per-row

Section 10 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action

Materialized ViewsPre-computed aggregations, auto-refreshed, auto-routedCreate MVs for top 5–10 dashboard query patterns. $0 compute for matched queries. Saves thousands/month.

Part III — Section 11

Cloud Storage Architecture

Objects, buckets, strong consistency, storage classes, lifecycle management, signed URLs, versioning, and integration with every GCP data service.

Section 11 — Cloud Storage Architecture — Objects, Buckets, Consistency, Classes

11.1 Cloud Storage in the GCP Data Platform

Cloud Storage (GCS) is Google's object storage service and the foundation of every GCS-based data lake. It stores files (objects) in buckets with four storage classes, automatic lifecycle management, and strong consistency. GCS serves as the landing zone for raw data, the archive for cold data, the staging area for Dataflow and Dataproc, and the underlying storage for BigLake and Iceberg tables.

Every GCP data architecture uses GCS somewhere — whether as a data lake, a staging area between services, a backup destination, or the physical storage for open table formats. Understanding GCS's architecture, consistency model, storage classes, and lifecycle management is essential for cost optimization and reliable data pipelines.

Figure 11.1 — GCS in the data platform: producers write, consumers read, lifecycle rules auto-transition between storage classes. 10 TB costs $2,400/yr on Standard vs $144/yr on Archive.

11.2 Core Concepts — Objects, Buckets, and Namespace

ConceptDescriptionKey Properties
ObjectA single file stored in GCS (Parquet, Avro, JSON, image, any binary)Immutable (overwrite creates new version). Max 5 TiB per object. No partial updates.
BucketContainer for objects. Has a globally unique name, a location, and a default storage class.Global namespace (bucket name unique across ALL GCP). Cannot be renamed. Location immutable after creation.
Object nameFull path within a bucket: gs://bucket/raw/sales/2024/03/15/data.parquetFlat namespace — "/" is just a character, not a directory. Console shows "folders" as a UI convenience.
MetadataKey-value pairs on each object: content-type, cache-control, custom metadataUp to 8 KB total metadata per object. Used for tagging, lifecycle conditions.
Generation numberUnique version ID assigned on each write (monotonically increasing)Enables versioning, conditional writes (if-generation-match), and concurrency control.
⚠️ Common Pitfall — GCS "Folders" Don't Exist GCS has a flat namespace — there are no directories or folders. The path gs://bucket/raw/sales/2024/data.parquet is a single object named raw/sales/2024/data.parquet. The "/" characters are just part of the name. The Console displays "folders" as a UI convenience by listing common prefixes. This means: (1) You can't "move" a folder — you must copy each object individually. (2) An "empty folder" is actually a zero-byte object with a trailing "/" in its name. (3) Listing objects is a prefix scan, not a directory listing — listing gs://bucket/raw/sales/ scans ALL object names starting with that prefix.

11.3 Consistency Model — Strong Consistency Since 2021

Since February 2021, GCS provides strong consistency for all operations — read-after-write, read-after-update, read-after-delete, and list-after-write. After a successful write, all subsequent reads from any client, anywhere in the world, immediately see the new data. There is no eventual consistency window.

OperationConsistencyWhat It Means
Read after writeStrongly consistentAfter PUT succeeds, all GET requests return the new object
Read after update (overwrite)Strongly consistentAfter overwriting an object, all reads return the new version
Read after deleteStrongly consistentAfter DELETE succeeds, all GET requests return 404
List after writeStrongly consistentAfter PUT succeeds, the object appears in LIST immediately
Metadata read after updateStrongly consistentAfter PATCH metadata, all reads return updated metadata
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Why Strong Consistency Matters for Data Pipelines Before strong consistency (and still an issue on some S3 configurations), data pipelines had a common bug: a Dataflow job writes Parquet files to GCS, then triggers a BigQuery LOAD — but the LOAD doesn't see all the files because the listing was eventually consistent. This resulted in silent data loss (missing files in the load). GCS's strong consistency eliminates this entirely: once the Dataflow write completes, a LIST immediately returns all files, and the LOAD sees everything. This is one less thing to worry about on GCP vs some other cloud storage systems.

11.4 Storage Classes — Detailed Comparison

ClassStorage $/GB/moRetrieval $/GBMin DurationAccess LatencyUse Case
Standard$0.020$0 (free)NoneMillisecondsFrequently accessed: active data lake, Dataflow staging, hot data
Nearline$0.010$0.0130 daysMillisecondsMonthly access: monthly reports, log archives accessed monthly
Coldline$0.004$0.0290 daysMillisecondsQuarterly access: quarterly backups, compliance archives
Archive$0.0012$0.05365 daysMilliseconds*Annual access: long-term compliance, disaster recovery backups

*All storage classes have the same access latency (milliseconds) — unlike AWS Glacier which has hours-long retrieval. GCS Archive is accessible immediately, but charges a higher per-GB retrieval fee. This makes GCS lifecycle transitions completely transparent to pipelines — a Nearline object reads at the same speed as Standard.

💰 Cost Tip — Lifecycle Rules Save 80%+ on Storage Without lifecycle rules, 10 TB of data lake files on Standard costs $200/month ($2,400/year). With lifecycle rules (Standard→Nearline at 30d, →Coldline at 90d), the blended cost drops to approximately $60/month ($720/year) — a 70% reduction. If most data transitions to Archive after a year, annual cost drops to ~$200/year (92% savings). These rules apply automatically with zero pipeline changes — the only configuration is a JSON lifecycle policy on the bucket.

11.5 Object Lifecycle Management

Lifecycle rules automatically transition objects between storage classes, delete old objects, or delete old versions — based on age, storage class, version count, or custom conditions. This is the #1 GCS cost optimization.

JSONlifecycle_config.json
{
  "lifecycle": {
    "rule": [
      {
        "action": {"type": "SetStorageClass", "storageClass": "NEARLINE"},
        "condition": {"age": 30}
        // After 30 days: Standard → Nearline (50% cheaper)
      },
      {
        "action": {"type": "SetStorageClass", "storageClass": "COLDLINE"},
        "condition": {"age": 90}
        // After 90 days: Nearline → Coldline (60% cheaper)
      },
      {
        "action": {"type": "SetStorageClass", "storageClass": "ARCHIVE"},
        "condition": {"age": 365}
        // After 1 year: Coldline → Archive (70% cheaper)
      },
      {
        "action": {"type": "Delete"},
        "condition": {"age": 730}
        // After 2 years: auto-delete (compliance policy)
      },
      {
        "action": {"type": "Delete"},
        "condition": {"isLive": false, "numNewerVersions": 3}
        // Keep only 3 old versions (with versioning enabled)
      }
    ]
  }
}
BASHlifecycle_management.sh
# ═══ APPLY lifecycle rules to a bucket ═══
gsutil lifecycle set lifecycle_config.json gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ VIEW current lifecycle rules ═══
gsutil lifecycle get gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ CREATE bucket with Terraform (lifecycle included) ═══
# See Section 1.16 for full Terraform example

# ═══ CHECK object storage class ═══
gsutil ls -L gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/2024/03/data.parquet
# Shows: Storage class: STANDARD (or NEARLINE, COLDLINE, ARCHIVE)

# ═══ MANUALLY change storage class for a specific object ═══
gsutil rewrite -s COLDLINE gs://my-data-lake/raw/old_data.parquet

11.6 Object Versioning — Recovery and Audit Trail

When versioning is enabled on a bucket, overwriting or deleting an object creates a new version rather than replacing/removing the old one. This provides data recovery (undelete), audit trail (who changed what), and protection against accidental overwrites.

BASHversioning.sh
# ═══ ENABLE versioning on a bucket ═══
gsutil versioning set on gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ LIST all versions of an object ═══
gsutil ls -a gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/data.parquet
# gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/data.parquet#1710500400000000  (current)
# gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/data.parquet#1710414000000000  (previous)

# ═══ RESTORE a previous version ═══
gsutil cp \
  gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/data.parquet#1710414000000000 \
  gs://my-data-lake/raw/sales/data.parquet

# ═══ DELETE old versions via lifecycle rule (see 11.5) ═══
# Keep only 3 old versions: {"isLive": false, "numNewerVersions": 3}
⚠️ Common Pitfall — Versioning Storage Cost Explosion Versioning keeps every overwritten version of every object. If a pipeline overwrites a 1 GB file daily, after 365 days you have 365 GB stored (365 versions). Without a lifecycle rule to delete old versions, storage costs grow unbounded. Always pair versioning with a lifecycle rule that limits the number of old versions (e.g., numNewerVersions: 3) or deletes old versions after a set age.

11.7 Access Control — Uniform vs Fine-Grained

ModelHow It WorksRecommendation
Uniform bucket-level access (recommended)IAM only. All objects inherit bucket-level IAM policies. No per-object ACLs.✅ Use for all data lake buckets. Simpler, more secure, consistent with BQ IAM model.
Fine-grained (legacy)Per-object ACLs. Each object can have different permissions.Avoid. Complex, error-prone. Only needed for rare edge cases (per-file user access).
BASHaccess_control.sh
# ═══ ENABLE uniform bucket-level access (recommended) ═══
gsutil uniformbucketlevelaccess set on gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ GRANT read access to a service account ═══
gsutil iam ch \
  serviceAccount:dataflow-sa@project.iam.gserviceaccount.com:roles/storage.objectViewer \
  gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ GRANT write access to data engineering team ═══
gsutil iam ch \
  group:data-eng@company.com:roles/storage.objectCreator \
  gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ SIGNED URLS — Time-limited access without IAM ═══
gsutil signurl -d 1h \
  /path/to/service-account-key.json \
  gs://my-data-lake/exports/report.csv
# Generates a URL valid for 1 hour — anyone with the URL can download
# Use for: sharing files with external partners, temporary download links

11.8 GCS Performance — Throughput and Optimization

OperationThroughputOptimization
Single object read~200 MB/s per object (Standard class)Larger objects = better throughput (avoid many tiny files)
Parallel readsNear-unlimited aggregate (auto-scaling)Read multiple objects in parallel — GCS scales with request count
Object listing~5,000 objects/sec per prefixUse delimiter + prefix for efficient listing. Avoid listing millions of objects.
Write throughputOne write at a time per object nameFor high-throughput writes: use unique object names (timestamps, UUIDs)
gsutil parallelMulti-threaded uploads/downloadsgsutil -m cp -r for parallel operations
ComposeCombine up to 32 objects server-sidegsutil compose to merge small files without downloading
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Optimal File Sizes for Data Lakes File size is the #1 GCS performance consideration for data pipelines. Ideal: 128–512 MB per Parquet file. Too small (<10 MB): excessive metadata overhead, slow listing (~5000 obj/sec limit), many small reads instead of few large reads. Too large (>1 GB): slow retry on failure, poor parallelism for downstream processing (Dataflow/Spark prefer many medium files over few huge ones). If your pipeline produces many small files (common with Kafka Connect, Datastream, or streaming Dataflow), add a compaction step: Dataproc Serverless Spark job reads small files, coalesces into 256 MB Parquet files, writes them back, and deletes the originals.

11.9 GCS Notifications — Event-Driven Pipelines

GCS can publish notifications to Pub/Sub when objects are created, deleted, archived, or metadata-updated. This enables event-driven pipelines: a file lands in GCS → Pub/Sub notification → Cloud Function triggers BigQuery LOAD or Dataflow pipeline.

BASHgcs_notifications.sh
# ═══ CREATE notification: object creation → Pub/Sub ═══
gsutil notification create \
  -t projects/my-project/topics/gcs-events \
  -f json \
  -e OBJECT_FINALIZE \
  gs://my-data-lake

# When any object is created/overwritten in my-data-lake,
# a Pub/Sub message is published with:
#   - bucket name
#   - object name (full path)
#   - size, content type, generation

# ═══ CLOUD FUNCTION triggered by GCS event ═══
# Deploy a Cloud Function that listens to the Pub/Sub topic
# and triggers BigQuery LOAD for each new Parquet file:
# gcloud functions deploy load-to-bq \
#   --runtime python312 \
#   --trigger-topic gcs-events \
#   --entry-point handle_gcs_event

# ═══ LIST existing notifications ═══
gsutil notification list gs://my-data-lake

# ═══ DELETE notification ═══
gsutil notification delete gs://my-data-lake
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — Event-Driven Ingestion The event-driven pattern: (1) External system writes Parquet file to gs://data-lake/raw/. (2) GCS notification → Pub/Sub. (3) Cloud Function or Eventarc triggers LOAD DATA into BigQuery. No scheduling, no polling — data arrives in BigQuery within seconds of landing in GCS. This is simpler than Cloud Composer for file-triggered loads and eliminates the "how frequently should I poll GCS?" question. Use Composer when you need complex dependencies between multiple loads; use event-driven for simple file-to-table pipelines.

11.10 Terraform — Production GCS Bucket

HCLgcs_bucket.tf
resource "google_storage_bucket" "data_lake" {
  name          = "${var.project_id}-data-lake-${var.env}"
  location      = "US"                       # Multi-region for durability
  storage_class = "STANDARD"                # Default class for new objects
  force_destroy = false                     # Prevent accidental deletion

  uniform_bucket_level_access = true       # IAM only (no per-object ACLs)
  public_access_prevention    = "enforced"  # Block all public access

  versioning { enabled = true }

  # Lifecycle: Standard → Nearline → Coldline → Delete
  lifecycle_rule {
    action { type = "SetStorageClass"  storage_class = "NEARLINE" }
    condition { age = 30  matches_storage_class = ["STANDARD"] }
  }
  lifecycle_rule {
    action { type = "SetStorageClass"  storage_class = "COLDLINE" }
    condition { age = 90  matches_storage_class = ["NEARLINE"] }
  }
  lifecycle_rule {
    action { type = "Delete" }
    condition { age = 730 }  # Delete after 2 years
  }
  lifecycle_rule {
    action { type = "Delete" }
    condition { num_newer_versions = 3 }  # Keep max 3 old versions
  }

  labels = {
    environment = var.env
    managed_by  = "terraform"
    purpose     = "data-lake"
  }
}

# ═══ NOTIFICATION → Pub/Sub on object creation ═══
resource "google_storage_notification" "gcs_to_pubsub" {
  bucket         = google_storage_bucket.data_lake.name
  payload_format = "JSON_API_V1"
  topic          = google_pubsub_topic.gcs_events.id
  event_types    = ["OBJECT_FINALIZE"]

  depends_on = [google_pubsub_topic_iam_member.gcs_publisher]
}

11.11 GCS vs AWS S3 vs Azure Blob Storage

FeatureGCSAWS S3Azure Blob / ADLS
ConsistencyStrong (all ops, since 2021)Strong (since Dec 2020)Strong
Storage classes4 (Standard, Nearline, Coldline, Archive)6+ (Standard, IA, Glacier, Deep Glacier, etc.)Hot, Cool, Cold, Archive
Archive retrievalMilliseconds (immediate)Minutes to hours (Glacier/Deep)Hours (Archive rehydrate)
Max object size5 TiB5 TiB~190 TiB (block blob)
NamespaceFlat (virtual folders)Flat (virtual folders)ADLS Gen2: hierarchical
BigQuery integrationNative (LOAD, external tables, BigLake)Via Omni (Enterprise Plus)Via Omni (Enterprise Plus)
LifecycleJSON rules on bucketXML/JSON rules on bucketPolicy-based rules
Key advantageImmediate Archive access, strong BQ integrationLargest ecosystem, most regionsHierarchical namespace (ADLS)
🎯 Interview Tip — "Why GCS over S3 for a GCP data platform?" Three reasons: (1) Immediate Archive access — GCS Archive objects are readable in milliseconds, vs S3 Glacier's hours-long retrieval. This means lifecycle transitions are transparent to pipelines. (2) Native BigQuery integration — LOAD DATA, external tables, BigLake Iceberg, GCS notifications → all first-class. S3 requires Omni (Enterprise Plus). (3) Unified Colossus backend — GCS, BigQuery, and Bigtable all share the same storage infrastructure, meaning GCS-to-BQ data movement happens internally at Jupiter network speed without internet hops.

11.12 gcloud / gsutil Quick Reference

BASHgcs_commands.sh
# ═══ BUCKET OPERATIONS ═══
gsutil mb -l US -c STANDARD gs://my-new-bucket     # Create bucket
gsutil ls                                            # List buckets
gsutil du -s gs://my-bucket/                         # Total bucket size
gsutil du -s gs://my-bucket/raw/2024/                # Prefix size

# ═══ OBJECT OPERATIONS ═══
gsutil cp file.parquet gs://my-bucket/raw/           # Upload
gsutil cp gs://my-bucket/raw/file.parquet ./         # Download
gsutil -m cp -r local_dir/ gs://my-bucket/raw/       # Parallel recursive upload
gsutil -m cp -r gs://my-bucket/raw/ ./local_dir/     # Parallel recursive download
gsutil mv gs://my-bucket/old gs://my-bucket/new      # Rename/move
gsutil rm gs://my-bucket/raw/old_file.parquet        # Delete
gsutil rm -r gs://my-bucket/raw/2023/                # Delete prefix (recursive)

# ═══ INSPECTION ═══
gsutil ls -L gs://my-bucket/raw/file.parquet         # Object metadata
gsutil stat gs://my-bucket/raw/file.parquet          # Quick stat (size, class, hash)
gsutil cat gs://my-bucket/raw/small_file.json        # Print contents (small files)

# ═══ CROSS-BUCKET OPERATIONS ═══
gsutil -m rsync -r gs://source-bucket/ gs://dest-bucket/  # Sync buckets

# ═══ STORAGE CLASS OPERATIONS ═══
gsutil rewrite -s COLDLINE gs://my-bucket/old_data.parquet  # Change class
gsutil ls -L gs://my-bucket/file.parquet | grep "Storage class"

Part III — Section 12

Data Lake Design on GCS

Directory layout, partitioning strategies, file formats, compaction, medallion architecture, and integration with BigQuery, Dataflow, and Dataproc.

Section 12 — Data Lake Design on GCS — Directory Layout, Formats, Integration

12.1 Why Data Lake Design Matters

A GCS-based data lake isn't just a bucket full of files — it's a structured, governed, multi-layer storage system that feeds every downstream service: BigQuery batch loads, Dataflow pipelines, Dataproc Spark jobs, and BigLake Iceberg tables. Poor data lake design leads to slow listing performance, expensive full-bucket scans, impossible debugging, and pipeline failures. Good design enables fast prefix-based queries, efficient lifecycle management, and clean separation of raw, cleaned, and curated data.

This section covers the directory layout conventions used by production GCP data platforms, the medallion architecture (Bronze → Silver → Gold), file format selection, compaction strategies, Hive-style partitioning for BigQuery integration, and the complete patterns for connecting GCS to downstream consumers.

12.2 Standard Directory Layout — The GCP Convention

Figure 12.1 — Standard GCS data lake directory layout: raw (Bronze), staging (Silver), curated (Gold), temp, archive, exports, and iceberg zones with Hive-style partitioning.

12.3 Medallion Architecture — Bronze → Silver → Gold

The medallion architecture (popularized by Databricks but universally applicable) organizes data into three quality tiers. On GCP, the Bronze and Silver layers typically live in GCS, while the Gold layer lives in BigQuery (or both GCS + BigQuery).

LayerGCS PathDescriptionFormatWho WritesWho Reads
Bronze (raw/)raw/{source}/{entity}/{partitions}/Immutable landing zone. Exact copy from source. Never modified after write.Source format (JSON, Avro, CSV, Parquet)Fivetran, Datastream, Pub/Sub, APIsDataflow, Dataproc, BQ LOAD
Silver (staging/)staging/{entity}/{partitions}/Cleaned, deduplicated, typed, validated. Schema enforced. PII tagged.Parquet (always)Dataflow, Dataproc, DataformDataproc ML, BQ LOAD, BigLake
Gold (curated/)curated/{domain}/{partitions}/ or BigQuery datasetsAnalytics-ready aggregates, business metrics, feature tables. Domain-aligned.Parquet on GCS / Native BQ tablesDataform/dbt, BQ SQLLooker, analysts, Vertex AI
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — GCS Bronze + BigQuery Silver/Gold The most common GCP pattern: Bronze in GCS, Silver and Gold in BigQuery. Raw data lands in GCS (from Fivetran, Datastream, application uploads). A batch load job (LOAD DATA) or Dataflow pipeline moves data to BigQuery's raw dataset (effectively Silver). Dataform/dbt models transform raw → staging → marts within BigQuery (Gold). GCS serves as the immutable archive and the staging area, while BigQuery is the primary query engine. This pattern avoids maintaining two parallel data stores (GCS lake + BQ warehouse) and instead uses GCS as the ingest buffer and BigQuery as the single source of truth.

12.4 File Format Strategy

FormatWhen to UseCompressionBigQuery SupportSpark/Dataflow Support
ParquetDefault for everything. Analytics, BQ loads, Spark.Snappy (default), Zstd, Gzip✅ Fastest load, external tables, BigLake✅ Native in both
AvroStreaming writes, schema evolution neededDeflate, Snappy✅ Supported (batch load)✅ Native in both
JSON (NDJSON)Raw event landing (keep for debugging), APIsGzip✅ Supported (autodetect)✅ Supported
CSVLegacy imports, human-readable exports onlyGzip✅ Supported (fragile)✅ Supported
ORCHive/Dataproc migration workloadsZlib, Snappy✅ Supported✅ Native in Spark
Delta LakeDatabricks migration, ACID on GCSSnappy✅ BigLake✅ Spark with delta-spark
Apache IcebergMulti-engine lakehouse, schema evolution, time travelSnappy, Zstd✅ BigLake (full DML)✅ Spark, Trino, Flink
⚙️ Engineering Insight — Parquet Configuration for GCS Data Lakes For production data lakes, standardize on these Parquet settings: (1) Row group size: 128 MB — aligns with GCS read optimization. (2) Compression: Snappy (default, fast decompress) or Zstd level 3 (20% better compression, slightly slower). (3) Target file size: 256 MB — optimal for BQ LOAD, Spark reads, and GCS operations. (4) Statistics: enabled (default in most writers) — enables predicate pushdown in Spark and BQ BigLake. (5) Dictionary encoding: enabled for low-cardinality string columns — massive compression gains.

12.5 Hive-Style Partitioning on GCS

Hive-style partitioning encodes partition values in the directory path: year=2024/month=03/day=15/. BigQuery, Spark, and other engines can extract these values and use them for partition pruning — dramatically reducing the amount of data scanned when querying external GCS data.

SQLhive_partitioning.sql
-- ═══ BQ external table with Hive-style partition detection ═══
CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE `project.dataset.gcs_events`
WITH PARTITION COLUMNS (
    year  INT64,
    month INT64,
    day   INT64
)
WITH CONNECTION `project.us.biglake-conn`
OPTIONS (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/*'],
    hive_partition_uri_prefix = 'gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/'
);

-- ═══ QUERY with partition pruning (reads only 1 day) ═══
SELECT event_type, COUNT(*) AS cnt
FROM `project.dataset.gcs_events`
WHERE year = 2024 AND month = 3 AND day = 15
GROUP BY event_type;
-- BigQuery only reads files under year=2024/month=03/day=15/

-- ═══ LOAD DATA with Hive partitioning ═══
LOAD DATA INTO `project.dataset.events`
FROM FILES (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/*.parquet'],
    hive_partition_uri_prefix = 'gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/'
);
PYTHONwrite_hive_partitioned.py
# ═══ PySpark: Write Hive-style partitioned Parquet to GCS ═══
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession

spark = SparkSession.builder.appName("write_partitioned").getOrCreate()

df = spark.read.parquet("gs://data-lake/raw/events/")

# Write with Hive-style partitioning
(df
 .repartition("year", "month", "day")
 .write
 .partitionBy("year", "month", "day")
 .mode("overwrite")
 .parquet("gs://data-lake/staging/events/")
)
# Output: gs://data-lake/staging/events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/part-00000.parquet

# ═══ Control output file count per partition ═══
(df
 .repartition(4, "year", "month", "day")  # 4 files per partition
 .write
 .partitionBy("year", "month", "day")
 .option("maxRecordsPerFile", 5000000)   # ~256 MB per file
 .mode("overwrite")
 .parquet("gs://data-lake/staging/events/")
)

12.6 File Compaction — Solving the Small Files Problem

The small files problem is the #1 performance issue in GCS data lakes. Streaming pipelines (Dataflow, Kafka Connect, Datastream) produce many small files (1–10 MB). These small files cause: slow GCS listing (5,000 objects/sec limit), excessive metadata overhead in BigQuery and Spark, and poor I/O efficiency (many small reads instead of few large reads).

File SizeImpactAction
<10 MBCritical — severe perf degradation, listing bottleneckCompact immediately
10–64 MBSuboptimal — too many files per partitionCompact during off-peak
64–512 MBIdeal range — good balance of parallelism and I/ONo action needed
>1 GBToo large — slow retries, poor parallelismSplit into 256 MB files
PYTHONcompaction_spark.py
# ═══ Dataproc Serverless Spark: Compact small files ═══
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession

spark = SparkSession.builder.appName("compact").getOrCreate()

# Read all small files from a partition
df = spark.read.parquet(
    "gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/"
)

# Coalesce into fewer, larger files (target: ~256 MB each)
total_bytes = df.rdd.map(lambda r: len(str(r))).sum()
num_files = max(1, int(total_bytes / (256 * 1024 * 1024)))

(df
 .coalesce(num_files)
 .write
 .mode("overwrite")
 .parquet("gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/")
)
print(f"Compacted to {num_files} files")
BASHcompact_serverless.sh
# ═══ Submit compaction job to Dataproc Serverless ═══
gcloud dataproc batches submit pyspark \
  gs://scripts/compact_partition.py \
  --region=us-central1 \
  --subnet=default \
  --deps-bucket=gs://dataproc-staging/ \
  -- --input=gs://data-lake/raw/app_events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/ \
     --target-size-mb=256

# Serverless: no cluster to manage, auto-scales, stops when done
# Schedule daily via Cloud Composer for automated compaction
💰 Cost Tip — Compaction Saves Downstream Costs A partition with 1,000 small files (5 MB each = 5 GB total): BigQuery LOAD takes ~30 seconds and lists 1,000 objects. After compacting to 20 files (256 MB each): LOAD takes ~5 seconds and lists 20 objects. Spark read: 1,000 tasks vs 20 tasks — 50× less scheduling overhead. The compaction job itself costs ~$0.10 on Dataproc Serverless, but saves dollars in downstream processing per partition per day. At scale (100 partitions/day × 365 days), compaction saves thousands per year.

12.7 GCS ↔ BigQuery Integration Patterns

PatternHowWhen to Use
GCS → BQ (batch)LOAD DATA FROM FILES (free, Parquet)Daily/hourly batch loads from data lake
GCS → BQ (event-driven)GCS notification → Pub/Sub → Cloud Function → LOAD DATALoad on file arrival (no polling)
GCS → BQ (external)CREATE EXTERNAL TABLE (Hive partitioned)Ad-hoc queries without loading
GCS → BQ (BigLake)CREATE TABLE ... OPTIONS (table_format='ICEBERG')Lakehouse: BQ security + Spark access on same data
BQ → GCS (export)EXPORT DATA OPTIONS (uri=..., format='PARQUET')Share data with Spark, partners, or other clouds
BQ → GCS (extract)bq extract --destination_format=PARQUETCLI-based export for automation
SQLgcs_bq_integration.sql
-- ═══ LOAD from GCS data lake (batch — free) ═══
LOAD DATA OVERWRITE `project.raw.app_events`
PARTITION BY event_date
CLUSTER BY event_type, user_id
FROM FILES (
    format = 'PARQUET',
    uris   = ['gs://data-lake/staging/app_events/year=2024/month=03/day=15/*.parquet']
);

-- ═══ EXPORT from BQ to GCS (for Spark or partner sharing) ═══
EXPORT DATA
OPTIONS (
    uri         = 'gs://data-lake/exports/daily_revenue/dt=2024-03-15/*.parquet',
    format      = 'PARQUET',
    overwrite   = TRUE,
    compression = 'SNAPPY'
) AS
SELECT sale_date, country, SUM(revenue) AS total
FROM `project.dataset.sales`
WHERE sale_date = '2024-03-15'
GROUP BY sale_date, country;

12.9 Data Lake Production Checklist

#ActionWhy
1Separate raw/staging/curated zonesLayer separation: immutable raw, cleaned staging, analytics curated
2Hive-style partitioningEnables partition pruning in BQ external tables and Spark
3Parquet as default formatColumnar, compressed, typed, best performance everywhere
4Target 128–512 MB filesOptimal for GCS reads, BQ loads, Spark parallelism
5Compaction pipeline for small filesFix small files from streaming pipelines (Dataproc Serverless)
6Lifecycle rules on every bucketAuto-transition Standard → Nearline → Coldline → Delete
7Versioning + version lifecycleRecovery from overwrites, but limit old versions to control cost
8Uniform bucket-level accessIAM only, no per-object ACLs — simpler and more secure
9GCS notifications for event-driven loadsTrigger BQ LOAD on file arrival — no polling, no scheduling
10Register in DataplexAuto-discovery, data quality, lineage, catalog integration
🎯 Interview Tip — "How would you design a data lake on GCP?" "I'd use GCS with a medallion architecture: raw/ (Bronze — immutable landing zone in source format), staging/ (Silver — cleaned Parquet with Hive-style partitioning), and curated/ (Gold — analytics-ready, often loaded into BigQuery). Key design decisions: Parquet as the standard format (256 MB target files), lifecycle rules for automatic tiering (Standard→Nearline→Coldline), compaction pipeline for small files from streaming, GCS notifications for event-driven BigQuery loads, and Dataplex for governance. For multi-engine access (BigQuery + Spark), I'd use Apache Iceberg via BigLake tables."

Section 12 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Medallion architectureraw → staging → curated (Bronze → Silver → Gold)Never modify raw/. Clean and type in staging/. Aggregate in curated/ or BQ.
Directory layoutsource-first grouping + Hive-style date partitioningraw/{source}/{entity}/year=YYYY/month=MM/day=DD/*.parquet
File formatParquet always (Snappy, 256 MB, dict encoding)Standardize on Parquet for all Silver/Gold zones. JSON acceptable for raw.
Small files problemStreaming produces tiny files → perf degradationRun daily compaction with Dataproc Serverless Spark (target 256 MB).
GCS → BQ integration6 patterns: batch, event-driven, external, BigLake, export, extractBatch LOAD for scheduled, event-driven for file-arrival, BigLake for lakehouse.
