GovernanceDataplex for discovery, quality, lineageRegister buckets in Dataplex. Define quality rules per zone.
Part IV — Section 13

Pub/Sub

Architecture, Exactly-Once Delivery, Ordering Keys, Dead-Letter Topics, and BigQuery Subscription — GCP's fully managed event-streaming backbone.

Section 13 — Pub/Sub — Architecture, Exactly-Once, Ordering, Dead-Letter

13.1 Pub/Sub Architecture

Cloud Pub/Sub is Google's fully managed, globally distributed message-streaming service. It decouples producers (publishers) from consumers (subscribers) via a topic → subscription model. Publishers write messages to a topic; each subscription independently receives every message — enabling true fan-out to multiple consumers without any coordination code.

Pub/Sub supports three subscription types: Pull (consumer calls the API to retrieve messages — used by Dataflow, batch jobs), Push (Pub/Sub calls an HTTPS endpoint — used by Cloud Run, Cloud Functions, webhooks), and BigQuery subscription (Pub/Sub writes directly into a BigQuery table with zero application code — the simplest streaming-to-analytics path on GCP).

Figure 13.1 — How Pub/Sub distributes messages to consumers

13.2 Key Features

Pub/Sub provides a rich set of enterprise-grade features beyond basic publish-subscribe. The table below covers the most important capabilities for production data engineering.

FeatureDescription
Exactly-once delivery With Dataflow subscriber — deduplicate by message ID
Ordering keys Messages with same key delivered in publish order (per-key FIFO)
Dead-letter topics Failed messages routed to DLT after N retries (configurable 1–100)
Message retention Up to 31 days (configurable); default 7 days — allows replay without data loss
Seek / Replay Replay messages from a timestamp or a named snapshot — supports backfill pipelines
Schemas Enforce Avro or Protobuf schemas on messages; rejects non-conforming messages at publish time
BigQuery subscription Direct-to-BigQuery without Dataflow — simplest streaming path on GCP; supports topic schema + metadata columns
Pub/Sub Lite Zonal, cheaper alternative for high-volume workloads where cross-zone redundancy is not required (up to 10× lower cost)

13.3 Pub/Sub to BigQuery — Direct Subscription (No Dataflow)

The BigQuery subscription is the simplest streaming path on GCP. Pub/Sub writes messages directly into a BigQuery table — no Dataflow pipeline, no application code, no infrastructure to manage. Use this when you need simple event logging or data capture without any transformation logic.

Create BigQuery Subscription — zero-code streaming to BigQueryBASH
# ── Step 1: Create a topic with Avro schema ──────────────────────────
gcloud pubsub schemas create events-schema \
    --type=AVRO \
    --definition='{
      "type": "record",
      "name": "Event",
      "fields": [
        {"name": "user_id",    "type": "string"},
        {"name": "event_type", "type": "string"},
        {"name": "ts",         "type": "long"}
      ]
    }'

gcloud pubsub topics create my-topic \
    --schema=events-schema \
    --message-encoding=JSON

# ── Step 2: BigQuery subscription (direct write, no pipeline code) ───
gcloud pubsub subscriptions create my-bq-sub \
    --topic=my-topic \
    --bigquery-table=project:dataset.events \
    --use-topic-schema \     # Use the Avro/Protobuf schema defined on the topic
    --write-metadata         # Adds messageId, publishTime, attributes columns

# Messages are automatically written to BigQuery table.
# Best for: simple event logging without transformation.
# Use Dataflow when you need: filtering, enrichment, windowing, joins.

# ── Step 3: Dead-letter topic for failed messages ────────────────────
gcloud pubsub topics create my-dead-letter-topic

gcloud pubsub subscriptions modify-push-config my-bq-sub \
    --dead-letter-topic=my-dead-letter-topic \
    --max-delivery-attempts=5   # Route to DLT after 5 failed attempts

# ── Step 4: Replay from a timestamp (backfill) ──────────────────────
gcloud pubsub subscriptions seek my-bq-sub \
    --time="2026-03-01T00:00:00Z"   # Replay all messages from this timestamp
Production Note — BigQuery Subscription vs Dataflow Use BigQuery subscription when: no transformation needed, schema matches the BQ table, and you want zero infrastructure. Switch to Dataflow when: you need windowed aggregations, enrichment/joins, filtering, or exactly-once guarantees across complex state. A common pattern is to start with a BigQuery subscription for raw event capture and then add a Dataflow job on a second subscription for transformed output.

13.4 Pub/Sub vs Kafka on GCP

Kafka is available on GCP via Confluent Cloud (fully managed SaaS) or self-managed on GKE. The choice between Pub/Sub and Kafka is primarily driven by operational appetite, ecosystem requirements, and ordering guarantees.

FactorPub/SubKafka (GKE / Confluent)
Operational overhead Zero (fully managed) High — broker management, partition rebalancing, ZooKeeper/KRaft
Ordering Per-key (ordering keys) Per-partition (stronger total ordering within a partition)
Exactly-once With Dataflow subscriber Native (idempotent producers + transactions)
Replay Seek to timestamp / snapshot (up to 31 days) Offset-based — more flexible; unlimited retention possible
Throughput Millions msg/sec (auto-scales) Millions msg/sec (manual partition scaling)
Ecosystem GCP-native (Dataflow, BigQuery, Cloud Run) Kafka Connect, Kafka Streams, ksqlDB, 200+ connectors
Cost model Per-message ($0.04/GB ingested) VM + disk cost; Confluent charges per CKU
Recommendation GCP-native, serverless, new projects Complex stream processing, multi-cloud, Kafka ecosystem required
🎯 Interview Tip — Pub/Sub vs Kafka on GCP "When should you use Pub/Sub vs Kafka on GCP?" — Use Pub/Sub for GCP-native workloads where operational simplicity matters — it's fully managed, auto-scales, and integrates natively with Dataflow and BigQuery. Use Kafka (Confluent on GCP or self-managed on GKE) when you need the Kafka ecosystem (Kafka Connect, Kafka Streams, ksqlDB), stronger per-partition ordering guarantees, or are in a multi-cloud environment where Kafka is already the standard. The operational cost of Kafka is significant — justify it with a concrete ecosystem or ordering requirement, not just familiarity.

Section 13 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Topics & Subscriptions Publishers write to topics; subscriptions fan-out independently Create one subscription per consumer — each gets a full copy of every message
BigQuery Subscription Zero-code direct write to BigQuery Use for raw event capture. Attach a second Dataflow subscription for transformed output
Ordering Keys Same key → same subscriber, in order Use for user-session events, per-entity state changes. Keys must be low-cardinality
Dead-Letter Topics Route undeliverable messages after N retries Always configure a DLT in production. Monitor DLT message count as a pipeline health signal
Message Retention / Seek Up to 31 days; replay from timestamp or snapshot Set retention to cover your SLA for backfill. Use Seek for incident replay — no re-ingestion needed
Schema Enforcement Avro/Protobuf validated at publish time Define schemas early. Use --use-topic-schema on BigQuery subscriptions for automatic column mapping
Pub/Sub vs Kafka Pub/Sub = managed simplicity; Kafka = ecosystem & control Default to Pub/Sub for GCP-native projects. Choose Kafka only when Kafka ecosystem is a hard requirement
Part IV — Section 14

Dataflow — Apache Beam on GCP

Managed Apache Beam for unified batch and streaming — autoscaling workers, Shuffle Service, Streaming Engine, windowing, and watermarks.

Section 14 — Dataflow — Apache Beam on GCP: Batch, Streaming, Windowing

14.1 Dataflow Architecture

Cloud Dataflow is Google's fully managed service for executing Apache Beam pipelines. It handles the complete lifecycle: receiving your Beam pipeline code, optimizing the DAG (fusing transforms to reduce I/O, lifting combiners), planning the execution graph, allocating and autoscaling worker VMs, and monitoring progress — all without any infrastructure management by the user.

Dataflow's architecture separates the control plane (the Dataflow Service — runs in Google infrastructure, manages job metadata, scheduling, and autoscaling decisions) from the data plane (Worker VMs — your code runs here on GCE VMs in your project). Workers run the Beam SDK harness, which handles element processing, serialization, and communication with the Dataflow service. Two key managed services further offload heavy work from workers: the Shuffle Service (handles GroupByKey/CoGroupByKey operations in managed infrastructure — reduces worker memory and disk 10×) and the Streaming Engine (offloads per-key streaming state, watermark tracking, and timer management to Google infrastructure — dramatically reduces VM size requirements for stateful streaming).

Figure 14.1 — How Dataflow executes Beam pipelines: user submits code → Dataflow Service plans and schedules → Worker VMs execute with Shuffle Service and Streaming Engine offloading heavy operations

14.2 Batch vs Streaming — Unified Model

Apache Beam's key insight is the unified batch/streaming model. The same pipeline code runs in both modes — the only difference is the runner flag (--streaming) and the I/O connectors used. Batch pipelines read bounded datasets (GCS files, BigQuery tables) and terminate when all data is processed. Streaming pipelines read unbounded sources (Pub/Sub, Kafka) and run indefinitely, processing data as it arrives.

The mental model: batch is a special case of streaming where the source is bounded. In practice, this means you can develop and test a pipeline in batch mode using a historical file, then deploy it in streaming mode against Pub/Sub with minimal code changes — a powerful pattern for iterative development.

Batch Pipeline — GCS → BigQuery (ETL)Python
import apache_beam as beam
from apache_beam.options.pipeline_options import PipelineOptions

# ── Batch pipeline: GCS files → transform → BigQuery ─────────────────
options = PipelineOptions([
    '--runner=DataflowRunner',
    '--project=my-project',
    '--region=us-central1',
    '--temp_location=gs://bucket/temp',
    '--staging_location=gs://bucket/staging',
    '--num_workers=10',         # Fixed worker count for batch
    '--machine_type=n1-standard-4',
    '--use_public_ips=false'
])

def parse_csv(line):
    fields = line.split(',')
    return {'user_id': fields[0], 'event': fields[1], 'amount': float(fields[2])}

def filter_valid(record):
    return record['amount'] > 0

with beam.Pipeline(options=options) as p:
    (p
     | 'Read'   >> beam.io.ReadFromText('gs://bucket/data/events-*.csv', skip_header_lines=1)
     | 'Parse'  >> beam.Map(parse_csv)
     | 'Filter' >> beam.Filter(filter_valid)
     | 'Write'  >> beam.io.WriteToBigQuery(
            'project:dataset.events',
            write_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.WRITE_TRUNCATE,
            create_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.CREATE_IF_NEEDED
        )
    )
Streaming Pipeline — Pub/Sub → Window → BigQueryPython
import apache_beam as beam
from apache_beam.options.pipeline_options import PipelineOptions
import json

options = PipelineOptions([
    '--runner=DataflowRunner',
    '--project=my-project',
    '--region=us-central1',
    '--temp_location=gs://bucket/temp',
    '--streaming',                          # ← this flag switches to streaming mode
    '--experiments=use_runner_v2',           # Enable Streaming Engine
    '--enable_streaming_engine',
    '--autoscaling_algorithm=THROUGHPUT_BASED'
])

def parse_json(message):
    record = json.loads(message)
    return (record['country'], record['amount'])   # (key, value) for CombinePerKey

with beam.Pipeline(options=options) as p:
    (p
     | 'Read'      >> beam.io.ReadFromPubSub(topic='projects/my-project/topics/events')
     | 'Decode'    >> beam.Map(lambda x: x.decode('utf-8'))
     | 'Parse'     >> beam.Map(parse_json)
     | 'Window'    >> beam.WindowInto(beam.window.FixedWindows(300))  # 5-minute fixed windows
     | 'Aggregate' >> beam.CombinePerKey(sum)
     | 'Format'    >> beam.Map(lambda kv: {'country': kv[0], 'total_amount': kv[1]})
     | 'Write'     >> beam.io.WriteToBigQuery(
            'project:dataset.aggregated_events',
            write_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.WRITE_APPEND,
            create_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.CREATE_IF_NEEDED
        )
    )
Key Difference — Batch vs Streaming Pipeline The only code differences between batch and streaming are: (1) --streaming flag in options, (2) the source connector (ReadFromText/ReadFromBigQuery for batch vs ReadFromPubSub/ReadFromKafka for streaming), (3) windowing (required for streaming to produce bounded output), and (4) write disposition (WRITE_TRUNCATE for batch replaces the table; WRITE_APPEND for streaming adds rows continuously). Everything else — transforms, business logic, schema — is identical.

14.3 Key Dataflow Features

Beyond basic pipeline execution, Dataflow provides a rich set of production features that differentiate it from self-managed Spark Structured Streaming or Flink.

FeatureDescriptionWhen to Use
Autoscaling Adjusts worker count based on Pub/Sub backlog, throughput, or processing time. Scales to 0 when idle. Always enabled for streaming. Set --max_num_workers to cap costs.
Streaming Engine Offloads per-key streaming state, watermarks, and timers to Google-managed infrastructure. Workers need much less memory. All stateful streaming pipelines. Reduces VM cost 3–5×.
Shuffle Service Managed shuffle for GroupByKey, CoGroupByKey, joins. Handled off-VM — no disk thrashing. Batch pipelines with heavy GroupByKey/join operations. Default for batch Dataflow.
Flex Templates Package pipeline as a Docker image, store in GCR/Artifact Registry. Launch via API, gcloud, or Cloud Scheduler without code deployment. Production pipelines. Enables zero-code launches — key for Composer DAGs calling Dataflow.
Exactly-once delivery Guaranteed end-to-end exactly-once for streaming pipelines reading from Pub/Sub, via Pub/Sub's deduplication + Beam's deterministic processing model. Financial transactions, event deduplication, audit logs.
Dataflow Prime Next-generation execution: vertical autoscaling (CPU + memory per worker independently), right-fitting (different machine types per pipeline step), and intelligent resource management. CPU-heavy ML inference steps or memory-heavy join steps in the same pipeline.
GPU support Attach NVIDIA GPUs to Dataflow workers for in-pipeline ML inference (TensorFlow, PyTorch), image processing, or NLP enrichment. Real-time ML scoring pipelines — embed model inference directly in the streaming pipeline.
Cross-language transforms Use Java transforms from a Python pipeline (and vice versa) via the multi-language Beam expansion service. Python ML code + Java Kafka connector in the same pipeline.

14.4 Windowing and Watermarks

In streaming, data arrives continuously and out-of-order. Windowing slices the infinite stream into finite chunks that can be aggregated. Watermarks are Dataflow's estimate of how far behind real-time the data is — when the watermark passes a window's end time, Dataflow considers the window complete and emits results. Three window types cover most use cases:

Window TypeDefinitionExample Use Case
Fixed (Tumbling) Non-overlapping fixed-duration buckets. Every event belongs to exactly one window. Hourly revenue totals, 5-minute event counts, daily batch aggregations.
Sliding Overlapping windows. A window of size S starts every P seconds. An event belongs to S/P windows. Rolling 10-min average CPU over the last hour (size=10min, period=1min).
Session Dynamic windows based on activity gaps. A window closes after a gap > timeout with no events for that key. User session analysis — group all user events until 30-min idle gap.
Global Single window covering all time. Used with triggers to produce periodic outputs. Running totals, stateful counters without time boundaries.
Event-time windowing with triggers and allowed latenessPython
import apache_beam as beam

# ── Fixed windows — simplest, most common ─────────────────────────────
fixed = beam.WindowInto(beam.window.FixedWindows(300))   # 5-minute buckets

# ── Sliding windows — rolling averages ────────────────────────────────
sliding = beam.WindowInto(
    beam.window.SlidingWindows(size=600, period=60)   # 10-min window, slide every 1 min
)

# ── Session windows — per-key activity gaps ───────────────────────────
session = beam.WindowInto(
    beam.window.Sessions(gap_size=1800)   # Close window after 30-min gap per key
)

# ── Advanced: triggers + allowed lateness ─────────────────────────────
# Use when late-arriving data must still be processed (e.g. mobile events)
advanced = beam.WindowInto(
    beam.window.FixedWindows(300),
    trigger=beam.trigger.AfterWatermark(
        early=beam.trigger.AfterProcessingTime(60),    # Early result every 60s (before watermark)
        late=beam.trigger.AfterCount(1)               # Fire on every late element arrival
    ),
    accumulation_mode=beam.trigger.AccumulationMode.ACCUMULATING,  # Include previous panes
    allowed_lateness=beam.window.Duration(seconds=3600)           # Accept late data up to 1 hour
)

# ── Full streaming pipeline with advanced windowing ───────────────────
(events
 | 'Window'    >> advanced
 | 'Aggregate' >> beam.CombinePerKey(sum)
 | 'Log'       >> beam.Map(lambda kv: print(f'Window result: {kv}'))
)
Pitfall — Watermarks and Late Data By default, Dataflow drops all late-arriving data (data that arrives after the watermark passes the window end). If your data source has unreliable delivery (mobile apps, IoT sensors, Kafka with consumer lag), always set allowed_lateness and configure a late trigger. Without this, you silently lose data that arrives slightly out-of-order — a common production bug in streaming pipelines.

14.5 When to Use Dataflow — vs BigQuery, Dataproc, Pub/Sub Direct

Dataflow is the right choice for complex, multi-step stream or batch transformations that need exactly-once semantics, custom business logic, and stateful processing. It is not always the simplest or cheapest option.

ScenarioBest ChoiceWhy
Simple event capture — write Pub/Sub events to BQ as-isPub/Sub BigQuery SubscriptionZero code, zero infrastructure, lowest latency
Complex enrichment, joins, stateful windowing on streaming dataDataflowFull Beam model, exactly-once, stateful DoFns
Batch ETL on large historical files in GCSDataflow or DataprocDataflow: serverless. Dataproc: existing Spark code.
SQL-based transformation on data already in BigQueryBigQuery SQL / DataformSimpler, cheaper, no infrastructure
ML inference on streaming dataDataflow + GPU workersEmbed model inference in pipeline, no extra hops
Exactly-once streaming with deduplicationDataflow + Pub/SubOnly Dataflow provides exactly-once in this stack
Multi-language pipeline (Java + Python)DataflowBeam cross-language expansion service
🎯 Interview Tip — Dataflow vs Spark Structured Streaming "When would you choose Dataflow over Spark Structured Streaming on Dataproc?" — Choose Dataflow when: (1) you want a fully serverless solution with zero cluster management, (2) you need Apache Beam's portable model across batch and streaming with no code change, (3) you need Streaming Engine for low-cost stateful streaming, or (4) you're writing a new GCP-native pipeline. Choose Spark Structured Streaming (Dataproc) when: (1) you have existing Spark code, (2) your team knows Spark better, (3) you need Spark-specific features (Delta Lake, complex ML pipelines with MLlib), or (4) the job is primarily batch with occasional streaming. Cost-wise, Dataflow charges per vCPU-hour + shuffle; Dataproc charges for the cluster (even when idle) — but Dataproc Serverless eliminates idle cost.

Section 14 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Unified ModelSame Beam code runs batch and streamingDevelop pipelines in batch mode, test on historical GCS files, flip --streaming for production
Streaming EngineOffloads state + watermarks off-VMAlways enable --enable_streaming_engine for stateful streaming — 3–5× VM size reduction
Shuffle ServiceManaged GroupByKey/join shuffleEnabled by default for batch. Eliminates worker OOM from large shuffles
Flex TemplatesPipeline as Docker imagePackage all production pipelines as Flex Templates. Launch from Composer, Cloud Scheduler, or API
WindowingSlice infinite stream into finite chunksUse Fixed for periodic aggregations, Sessions for user behavior, Sliding for rolling metrics
Allowed LatenessAccept out-of-order data after watermarkSet allowed_lateness for any pipeline with mobile/IoT sources — default drops late data silently
Dataflow vs BQ SubBQ Sub = zero code; Dataflow = complex logicStart with BQ subscription. Add Dataflow only when you need windowing, enrichment, or stateful logic
Part IV — Section 15

Dataflow Advanced

Stateful processing, side inputs, Flex Templates, Dataflow Prime, and production monitoring — advanced patterns for real-world Dataflow engineering.

Section 15 — Dataflow Advanced — Stateful Processing, Flex Templates, Prime

15.1 Stateful Processing — State and Timers API

Standard Beam transforms (Map, Filter, CombinePerKey) are stateless — each element is processed independently. The State and Timers API enables per-key, persistent state across elements within a window (or globally). This is essential for patterns that cannot be expressed as simple aggregations: rate limiting, session stitching, exactly-once deduplication, micro-batching for API calls, or maintaining running counters per entity.

State lives per-key and is managed by the Streaming Engine (off-VM in Google infrastructure). There are four state types: BagState (append-only list), CombiningValueState (aggregates using a combiner function — most memory-efficient), ValueState (single mutable value), and MapState (key-value map). Timers allow you to fire callbacks at a specific event-time or processing-time — used for flushing batched state, session timeouts, or deadlines.

Stateful DoFn — per-key batch flush + timer-based deadlinePython
import apache_beam as beam
from apache_beam.transforms.userstate import (
    BagStateSpec, CombiningValueStateSpec, TimerSpec, on_timer
)
from apache_beam.transforms.timeutil import TimeDomain
from apache_beam.coders import IterableCoder, VarIntCoder

# ── Example 1: CombiningValueState — memory-efficient running counter ──
class StatefulCounter(beam.DoFn):
    COUNT_STATE = CombiningValueStateSpec('count', sum)

    def process(self, element,
                count=beam.DoFn.StateParam(COUNT_STATE)):
        key, value = element
        count.add(1)
        current = count.read()
        if current >= 100:
            yield (key, current)   # Emit when threshold reached
            count.clear()

# ── Example 2: BagState + Timer — micro-batch API calls ───────────────
class BatchedApiCaller(beam.DoFn):
    BUFFER_STATE = BagStateSpec('buffer', beam.coders.FastPrimitivesCoder())
    FLUSH_TIMER  = TimerSpec('flush', TimeDomain.REAL_TIME)

    def process(self, element,
                buf=beam.DoFn.StateParam(BUFFER_STATE),
                timer=beam.DoFn.TimerParam(FLUSH_TIMER)):
        key, record = element
        buf.add(record)

        # Set a processing-time timer 10s from now to flush the buffer
        # Calling set() repeatedly just moves the deadline forward
        import time
        timer.set(time.time() + 10)

    @on_timer(FLUSH_TIMER)
    def flush(self, buf=beam.DoFn.StateParam(BUFFER_STATE)):
        records = list(buf.read())
        if records:
            result = call_external_api_batch(records)   # Single batch API call
            yield from result
            buf.clear()

# ── Pipeline usage ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
(events
 | 'KeyByUser'   >> beam.Map(lambda e: (e['user_id'], e))
 | 'BatchedCall' >> beam.ParDo(BatchedApiCaller())
 | 'WriteBQ'     >> beam.io.WriteToBigQuery('project:dataset.enriched')
)
State TypeAPIUse CaseMemory
CombiningValueStateCombiningValueStateSpec('name', combiner)Running sum, count, max — most commonO(1) — only stores combined value
ValueStateReadModifyWriteStateSpec('name', coder)Last-seen value, single mutable flagO(1) — single value
BagStateBagStateSpec('name', coder)Buffering elements for batch flushO(n) — grows with elements
MapStateMapStateSpec('name', key_coder, val_coder)Per-key lookup table, deduplication setO(k) — grows with unique keys
SetStateSetStateSpec('name', coder)Exactly-once deduplication by message IDO(n) — grows with unique elements
Pitfall — State Memory with BagState BagState grows unboundedly until cleared. Always pair BagState with a timer that periodically flushes and clears state. Without this, workers accumulate memory until OOM — a classic stateful streaming bug. For counting use cases, always prefer CombiningValueState over BagState + len().

15.2 Side Inputs — Broadcast Enrichment

Side inputs allow a transform to read from a secondary PCollection (in addition to its main input). The secondary collection is broadcast to all workers — enabling enrichment joins without a full CoGroupByKey shuffle. Side inputs are ideal for small-to-medium lookup tables (country codes, product catalogue, user segments) that need to be applied to a large streaming dataset.

Three side input types: AsList (materialises the entire PCollection as a Python list on each worker — simplest), AsDict (materialises as a dict — best for keyed lookup), and AsSingleton (single value broadcast — used for configuration or thresholds). For streaming, side inputs are refreshed periodically (configurable) to pick up updates to the lookup table.

Side input enrichment — broadcast lookup table to all workersPython
import apache_beam as beam

with beam.Pipeline() as p:

    # ── Step 1: Build the lookup table as a PCollection ─────────────────
    country_lookup = (
        p
        | 'ReadLookup' >> beam.io.ReadFromBigQuery(
            query='SELECT country_code, country_name, region FROM ref.countries',
            use_standard_sql=True
          )
        | 'ToDict' >> beam.Map(lambda row: (row['country_code'], row))
    )

    # ── Step 2: Main pipeline with side input ───────────────────────────
    events = (
        p
        | 'ReadEvents' >> beam.io.ReadFromPubSub(
            topic='projects/my-project/topics/events'
          )
        | 'Decode'     >> beam.Map(lambda x: x.decode('utf-8'))
    )

    enriched = (
        events
        | 'Enrich' >> beam.Map(
            lambda event, lookup: {
                **event,
                'country_name':  dict(lookup).get(event['country'], {}).get('country_name', 'Unknown'),
                'region':         dict(lookup).get(event['country'], {}).get('region', 'Unknown')
            },
            lookup=beam.pvalue.AsList(country_lookup)   # broadcast to all workers
        )
    )

    # ── Use AsDict for O(1) lookup instead of O(n) list scan ────────────
    enriched_v2 = (
        events
        | 'EnrichV2' >> beam.Map(
            lambda event, lookup: {
                **event,
                'country_name': lookup.get(event['country'], {}).get('country_name', 'Unknown')
            },
            lookup=beam.pvalue.AsDict(country_lookup)   # O(1) dict lookup — preferred
        )
        | 'WriteBQ' >> beam.io.WriteToBigQuery('project:dataset.enriched_events')
    )
Production Note — Side Inputs vs CoGroupByKey Use side inputs when the lookup table is small enough to fit in worker memory (typically <500MB). For large-scale joins where both datasets are big (GB to TB), use CoGroupByKey — it shuffles both datasets and performs a proper distributed join. Rule of thumb: if the lookup table has <1M rows and fits in memory, side input with AsDict is faster and simpler than a shuffle join.

15.3 Flex Templates — Production Pipeline Deployment

Flex Templates are the production-grade way to deploy Dataflow pipelines. Instead of launching a pipeline by running Python/Java code directly, you package the pipeline as a Docker image, push it to Artifact Registry (or GCR), and register a template metadata file in GCS. From that point, anyone (or any system — Cloud Composer, Cloud Scheduler, Cloud Run) can launch the pipeline via the Dataflow API or gcloud without having the source code or a Python environment.

Flex Templates supersede Classic Templates (which required pre-compiling a templated pipeline at build time and had limited parameter flexibility). Flex Templates support any Docker-compatible environment, custom dependencies, GPU workers, and full pipeline parameter customization at launch time.

FeatureClassic TemplatesFlex Templates
PackagingPre-compiled runner graph (JSON)Docker image in Artifact Registry
DependenciesLimited — must be available at build timeFull — anything installable in Docker
ParametersStatic — defined at compile timeDynamic — any parameter at launch time
GPU / custom hardwareNot supportedSupported — specify in template metadata
Pipeline updatesRequires recompile + redeployRebuild Docker image, push new tag
Launch mechanismgcloud, API, Composergcloud, API, Composer, Cloud Scheduler, Cloud Run
RecommendationLegacy — avoid for new pipelines✅ Standard for all production pipelines
Build, register, and launch a Flex TemplateBASH
# ── Step 1: Write a requirements.txt for the pipeline ─────────────────
# requirements.txt
# apache-beam[gcp]==2.55.0
# google-cloud-bigquery==3.x

# ── Step 2: Build the Docker image ────────────────────────────────────
gcloud dataflow flex-template build \
    gs://my-bucket/templates/my-pipeline.json \             # template spec output location
    --image-gcr-path=us-central1-docker.pkg.dev/my-project/dataflow/my-pipeline:latest \
    --sdk-language=PYTHON \
    --flex-template-base-image=PYTHON3 \
    --py-path=. \                                           # directory containing main.py
    --env=FLEX_TEMPLATE_PYTHON_PY_FILE=main.py \
    --env=FLEX_TEMPLATE_PYTHON_REQUIREMENTS_FILE=requirements.txt

# This command:
# 1. Builds a Docker image with your code + dependencies
# 2. Pushes to Artifact Registry
# 3. Creates template spec JSON at gs://my-bucket/templates/my-pipeline.json

# ── Step 3: Launch a job from the Flex Template ───────────────────────
gcloud dataflow flex-template run my-streaming-job-$(date +%s) \
    --template-file-gcs-location=gs://my-bucket/templates/my-pipeline.json \
    --region=us-central1 \
    --parameters=\
input_topic=projects/my-project/topics/events,\
output_table=my-project:dataset.events,\
window_size=300 \
    --max-workers=20 \
    --worker-machine-type=n1-standard-4 \
    --enable-streaming-engine

# ── Step 4: Launch from Cloud Composer (Airflow) ──────────────────────
# In your Airflow DAG:
# from airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.dataflow import DataflowStartFlexTemplateOperator
#
# run_pipeline = DataflowStartFlexTemplateOperator(
#     task_id='run_dataflow_pipeline',
#     body={
#         'launchParameter': {
#             'jobName': 'my-pipeline-{{ ds_nodash }}',
#             'parameters': {'input_topic': '...', 'output_table': '...'},
#             'containerSpecGcsPath': 'gs://my-bucket/templates/my-pipeline.json',
#         }
#     },
#     location='us-central1',
# )

# ── Step 5: Update a running streaming job (in-place update) ──────────
# Rebuild + push new image, then use --update flag
gcloud dataflow flex-template run my-streaming-job \
    --template-file-gcs-location=gs://my-bucket/templates/my-pipeline.json \
    --update \                       # drain current job and restart with new code
    --region=us-central1

15.4 Dataflow Prime — Next-Generation Execution

Dataflow Prime is the next-generation execution mode that introduces right-fitting and vertical autoscaling. In standard Dataflow, all workers use the same machine type for the entire pipeline. Prime allows Dataflow to assign different CPU/memory configurations to different pipeline stages — a CPU-heavy ML inference step gets more CPU; a memory-heavy join step gets more RAM — without any user configuration. This reduces cost by 20–40% on heterogeneous pipelines.

FeatureStandard DataflowDataflow Prime
Worker sizingSingle machine type for all stagesRight-fitting: different CPU/memory per stage
Vertical scalingFixed worker sizeCPU and memory scale independently per step
Horizontal scalingThroughput-based autoscalingThroughput + resource utilization signals
Cost optimizationManual machine type selectionAutomatic — 20–40% cheaper on mixed workloads
EnableDefault--dataflow_service_options=enable_prime
Enable Dataflow PrimePython
from apache_beam.options.pipeline_options import PipelineOptions

options = PipelineOptions([
    '--runner=DataflowRunner',
    '--project=my-project',
    '--region=us-central1',
    '--temp_location=gs://bucket/temp',
    '--dataflow_service_options=enable_prime',  # ← enable Prime
    '--experiments=use_runner_v2',              # required for Prime
    '--enable_streaming_engine',                # recommended with Prime for streaming
])
# No other code changes — Prime is purely a service-side optimization

15.5 Monitoring and Debugging Production Pipelines

Dataflow pipelines expose rich monitoring via the Dataflow UI (job graph with per-step metrics), Cloud Monitoring (custom dashboards and alerting), and Cloud Logging (worker logs, user logs via logging.info()). The most important signals for a healthy streaming pipeline are: system lag, data freshness, worker CPU/memory, and element count per step.

MetricWhat It MeansThreshold / Action
system_lagHow far behind the watermark is vs real-time (seconds)>60s → investigate source throughput, increase workers
data_freshnessAge of the oldest unprocessed element>5min for SLA-sensitive → alert + autoscale trigger
elements_produced_countElements output per stepDrop to 0 mid-pipeline → step is blocked or crashing
worker_cpu_utilizationAverage CPU across workers<30% → over-provisioned; >85% → scale up
backlog_bytes (Pub/Sub)Unacknowledged bytes in subscriptionGrowing unboundedly → pipeline cannot keep up with input rate
throttling_msTime spent being throttled by sink (BQ/Bigtable)>0 regularly → sink is the bottleneck; tune write batch size
OutOfMemoryError in logsWorker ran out of heapUpgrade machine type, enable Streaming Engine, reduce state size
Cloud Monitoring alert — system_lag and data_freshnessBASH
# Create a Cloud Monitoring alerting policy for Dataflow system_lag
gcloud monitoring policies create \
    --notification-channels=projects/my-project/notificationChannels/my-channel \
    --display-name="Dataflow system_lag too high" \
    --condition-display-name="system_lag > 120s" \
    --condition-filter='resource.type="dataflow_job" AND metric.type="dataflow.googleapis.com/job/system_lag" AND metric.label.job_id=my-job-id' \
    --condition-threshold-value=120 \
    --condition-threshold-comparison=COMPARISON_GT \
    --condition-duration=300s    # alert only if sustained for 5 minutes

# Query Dataflow job logs for worker errors
gcloud logging read \
    'resource.type="dataflow_step" severity>=ERROR' \
    --project=my-project \
    --freshness=1h \
    --format='table(timestamp,jsonPayload.message)'
Production Note — Debugging Tips (1) Stuck pipeline: look at the Dataflow job graph — find the step with 0 elements output and check its worker logs. (2) High system_lag: usually caused by insufficient workers — check autoscaling_decisions log; if capped at max_num_workers, increase the cap. (3) OOM workers: enable Streaming Engine (moves state off-heap), reduce BagState size, or upgrade to a memory-optimized machine type. (4) Slow BigQuery writes: use STORAGE_WRITE_API as the BigQuery write method — it's 5–10× faster than the default streaming inserts for high-throughput pipelines.

Part V — Section 16

Dataproc — Managed Spark/Hadoop

Autoscaling Spark clusters, Dataproc Serverless, Iceberg integration, initialization actions, and when to choose Dataproc over Dataflow or BigQuery.

Section 16 — Dataproc — Managed Spark/Hadoop: Autoscaling, Serverless, Iceberg

16.1 Dataproc Architecture

Cloud Dataproc is Google's fully managed service for running Apache Spark, Hadoop, Flink, and Presto clusters. Unlike self-managed Hadoop where you provision VMs, install distributions, configure YARN, and manage upgrades — Dataproc handles all of this in under 90 seconds. You submit jobs; Dataproc manages the infrastructure.

A Dataproc cluster consists of three node types: Master nodes (1 for standard, 3 for HA) running the YARN ResourceManager, Spark Driver, Hive Metastore, and HDFS NameNode; Primary workers (N, autoscaling) running YARN NodeManagers and Spark executors with local SSDs for shuffle; and Secondary workers (optional, preemptible/Spot VMs) that provide cheap burst capacity — if preempted, YARN gracefully reschedules tasks. All nodes connect to GCS as the primary data lake (not HDFS), enabling storage-compute separation: clusters can be ephemeral (create → run job → delete) because data persists in GCS.

The key architectural insight: Dataproc clusters should be ephemeral and job-specific. Instead of a single long-running cluster shared by the team, create a cluster per job (or per workflow), run the Spark job, then delete the cluster. This eliminates idle cost, avoids resource contention, and allows per-job machine type tuning. Dataproc Serverless takes this further by eliminating cluster management entirely.

Figure 16.1 — Managed Spark/Hadoop cluster with autoscaling, Spot VMs, and storage-compute separation via GCS

16.2 Dataproc Serverless — No Cluster Management

Dataproc Serverless eliminates cluster management entirely. You submit a Spark batch job — Google creates an auto-sized, right-fitted cluster, runs your job, and tears it down. No cluster creation, no YARN config, no idle cost. You pay only for the vCPU-hours and memory-hours consumed during execution.

Serverless is ideal for scheduled batch ETL (daily transforms, Iceberg compaction), ad-hoc analysis (data scientists running one-off PySpark notebooks), and Composer-orchestrated workflows where you want zero infrastructure between runs. The trade-off: you lose fine-grained control over machine types, local SSDs, and initialization actions. For long-running streaming jobs or workloads requiring custom cluster configuration, use standard Dataproc clusters.

Dataproc Serverless — submit Spark job without managing a clusterBASH
# ── Submit a Spark JAR job (Scala/Java) ───────────────────────────────
gcloud dataproc batches submit spark \
    --region=us-central1 \
    --jars=gs://bucket/jars/my-spark-job.jar \
    --class=com.example.MySparkJob \
    --properties="spark.executor.memory=8g,spark.sql.shuffle.partitions=200" \
    -- --input=gs://bucket/raw/ --output=gs://bucket/curated/

# Google automatically:
# 1. Provisions right-sized workers (autoscales based on data volume)
# 2. Runs your Spark job
# 3. Tears down all infrastructure when complete
# 4. Charges only for vCPU-hours + memory-hours consumed

# ── Submit a PySpark job ──────────────────────────────────────────────
gcloud dataproc batches submit pyspark \
    gs://bucket/scripts/transform.py \
    --region=us-central1 \
    --py-files=gs://bucket/libs/utils.zip \
    --properties="spark.sql.catalog.lakehouse=org.apache.iceberg.spark.SparkCatalog" \
    --jars=gs://bucket/jars/iceberg-spark-runtime-3.5_2.12.jar \
    -- --date=2026-04-03

# ── Submit from Cloud Composer (Airflow DAG) ──────────────────────────
# from airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.dataproc import (
#     DataprocCreateBatchOperator
# )
# batch_task = DataprocCreateBatchOperator(
#     task_id='run_spark_etl',
#     batch={
#         'pyspark_batch': {
#             'main_python_file_uri': 'gs://bucket/scripts/etl.py',
#             'args': ['--date={{ ds }}'],
#         },
#         'runtime_config': {
#             'properties': {'spark.sql.shuffle.partitions': '200'}
#         }
#     },
#     region='us-central1',
# )
FeatureDataproc ClusterDataproc Serverless
Cluster managementYou create/delete clustersNone — fully managed lifecycle
Startup time~90 seconds~60 seconds (pre-warmed pools)
Idle costCluster runs until deletedZero — runs only during job execution
Machine typesFull control (highmem, highcpu, GPU)Auto-sized by Dataproc
Init actionsCustom shell scripts at startupCustom Docker images (limited)

16.3 Dataproc + Apache Iceberg — Open Lakehouse on GCP

Apache Iceberg on Dataproc enables a true open lakehouse architecture: ACID transactions, schema evolution, time travel, and partition evolution on data stored in GCS — queryable by both Spark (Dataproc) and BigQuery (via BigLake). The Dataproc Metastore (managed Hive Metastore) serves as the shared Iceberg catalog, ensuring both engines see the same tables.

This pattern is the GCP answer to Databricks Delta Lake: open format (no vendor lock-in), multi-engine access, and full lakehouse semantics on commodity GCS storage.

PySpark on Dataproc with Apache Iceberg — complete setupPython
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession

# ── Configure Spark with Iceberg catalog using Dataproc Metastore ─────
spark = SparkSession.builder \
    .config("spark.sql.catalog.lakehouse",
            "org.apache.iceberg.spark.SparkCatalog") \
    .config("spark.sql.catalog.lakehouse.type", "hive") \
    .config("spark.sql.catalog.lakehouse.uri",
            "thrift://dataproc-metastore:9083") \
    .config("spark.sql.catalog.lakehouse.warehouse",
            "gs://my-bucket/iceberg-warehouse") \
    .config("spark.sql.extensions",
            "org.apache.iceberg.spark.extensions.IcebergSparkSessionExtensions") \
    .getOrCreate()

# ── Create an Iceberg table with partition evolution ──────────────────
spark.sql("""
    CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS lakehouse.analytics.events (
        event_id     BIGINT,
        user_id      STRING,
        event_type   STRING,
        event_time   TIMESTAMP,
        revenue      DOUBLE,
        country      STRING
    ) USING iceberg
    PARTITIONED BY (day(event_time))
    TBLPROPERTIES (
        'write.format.default' = 'parquet',
        'write.parquet.compression-codec' = 'zstd'
    )
""")

# ── Write data (append) ───────────────────────────────────────────────
df = spark.read.parquet("gs://my-bucket/raw/events/dt=2026-04-03/")
df.writeTo("lakehouse.analytics.events").append()

# ── Time-travel query — read snapshot as of 24 hours ago ─────────────
spark.read \
    .option("as-of-timestamp", "1743638400000") \
    .table("lakehouse.analytics.events") \
    .show()

# ── Expire old snapshots (maintenance — schedule weekly) ─────────────
spark.sql("""
    CALL lakehouse.system.expire_snapshots(
        table => 'analytics.events',
        older_than => TIMESTAMP '2026-03-27 00:00:00',
        retain_last => 5
    )
""")

# ── Compact small files (maintenance — schedule after heavy writes) ──
spark.sql("""
    CALL lakehouse.system.rewrite_data_files(
        table => 'analytics.events',
        strategy => 'sort',
        sort_order => 'event_time ASC'
    )
""")
Production Note — Iceberg on GCP: Dataproc + BigLake Integration Once your Iceberg tables are registered in Dataproc Metastore, create a BigLake external table pointing to the same Iceberg metadata. This gives BigQuery read access to the same data — Spark writes via Dataproc, analysts query via BigQuery SQL. Both engines share the same Iceberg metadata, ensuring consistency. This is the GCP open lakehouse pattern: Spark (write engine) → Iceberg on GCS → BigLake → BigQuery (query engine).

16.4 Autoscaling and Ephemeral Cluster Patterns

Dataproc supports YARN-metric-based autoscaling: when pending YARN containers exceed a threshold, secondary workers are added; when utilization drops, they are removed. The autoscaling policy defines min/max workers, scale-up/down factors, cooldown periods, and a graceful decommission timeout (to avoid killing active tasks).

Autoscaling policy + ephemeral cluster for a batch jobBASH
# ── Step 1: Create an autoscaling policy ──────────────────────────────
gcloud dataproc autoscaling-policies create my-policy \
    --region=us-central1 \
    --min-workers=2 \
    --max-workers=50 \
    --scale-up-factor=1.0 \
    --scale-down-factor=1.0 \
    --cooldown-period=120s \
    --graceful-decommission-timeout=1h   # Let running tasks finish before removing worker

# ── Step 2: Create ephemeral cluster → submit job → auto-delete ──────
gcloud dataproc clusters create etl-$(date +%Y%m%d) \
    --region=us-central1 \
    --master-machine-type=n2-standard-4 \
    --worker-machine-type=n2-standard-8 \
    --num-workers=4 \
    --num-secondary-workers=10 \
    --secondary-worker-type=preemptible \   # Spot VMs — 70% cheaper
    --autoscaling-policy=my-policy \
    --max-idle=600s \                       # Delete cluster after 10 min idle
    --image-version=2.2-debian12 \
    --optional-components=JUPYTER \
    --initialization-actions=gs://bucket/init/install-iceberg.sh \
    --metadata="ICEBERG_VERSION=1.5.0"

# ── Step 3: Submit Spark job to the cluster ───────────────────────────
gcloud dataproc jobs submit pyspark \
    gs://bucket/scripts/daily_etl.py \
    --cluster=etl-$(date +%Y%m%d) \
    --region=us-central1 \
    -- --date=2026-04-03

# Cluster will auto-delete after 10 min idle (--max-idle)
# Total cost: only for the ~15 min the job actually runs
🎯 Interview Tip — Ephemeral vs Long-Running Clusters "How would you manage Dataproc costs?" — The #1 pattern is ephemeral clusters: create per-job or per-workflow, use --max-idle for auto-deletion, store data in GCS (not HDFS), and use secondary workers (Spot VMs) for 70% cost savings. Long-running clusters make sense only for interactive Jupyter notebooks, streaming jobs, or shared environments where startup latency matters. For scheduled batch ETL, prefer Dataproc Serverless — it eliminates even the cluster creation step.

Section 16 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Cluster ArchitectureMaster + Primary workers + Secondary (Spot) workersUse 3 masters for HA. Use Spot VMs for secondary workers — 70% cheaper.
Storage-Compute SeparationData in GCS, not HDFS — clusters are ephemeralNever store data in HDFS. Use gs:// paths for all I/O. Delete clusters after jobs.
Dataproc ServerlessNo cluster management — submit job, pay for execution onlyDefault choice for batch ETL. Use standard clusters only for streaming or custom config.
Iceberg IntegrationACID tables on GCS, queryable by Spark and BigQueryUse Dataproc Metastore as catalog. Create BigLake external tables for BQ access.
AutoscalingYARN-metric-based scaling with graceful decommissionAlways set --graceful-decommission-timeout to avoid killing active tasks during scale-down.
Cost OptimizationEphemeral clusters + Spot VMs + auto-deleteUse --max-idle=600s on every cluster. Never leave clusters running overnight.
Part V — Section 17

Dataproc vs Dataflow vs BigQuery

Choosing the right processing engine — the most common GCP data engineering architecture decision.

Section 17 — Dataproc vs Dataflow vs BigQuery — Choosing the Right Engine

17.1 Processing Engine Selection — The Complete Comparison

The single most frequent architecture decision in GCP data engineering is: "Should I use BigQuery, Dataflow, or Dataproc for this workload?" The answer depends on the data's shape, the required processing model (SQL vs code), streaming requirements, existing codebase, and team skillset. This section provides a definitive comparison matrix.

The default mental model: BigQuery first, Dataflow second, Dataproc last. BigQuery handles everything SQL-expressible with zero infrastructure. Dataflow adds streaming, Beam-level transformations, and exactly-once semantics. Dataproc is reserved for workloads that specifically need Spark, Hadoop, or Presto — typically existing codebases, Spark MLlib, or Iceberg write operations.

FactorBigQueryDataflowDataproc
Language SQL (+ JavaScript UDFs, remote functions) Java / Python / Go (Apache Beam) Python / Scala / Java / R (Spark, Hadoop, Presto)
Best for SQL transformations, analytics, dashboards Streaming + batch, exactly-once, complex enrichment Spark ML, complex ETL, Hadoop migration, Iceberg
Operational overhead Zero — fully serverless Low — managed autoscaling workers Medium — cluster management (even with autoscaling)
Streaming Limited (scheduled queries, BQ subscription inserts) ✅ Native (Apache Beam) — first-class streaming Spark Structured Streaming (micro-batch, seconds latency)
ML capabilities BigQuery ML (SQL-based: linear, XGBoost, LLM embeddings) TensorFlow / PyTorch integration in pipeline Spark MLlib (full ML pipeline), XGBoost, custom Python
Exactly-once DML is transactional; streaming inserts are at-least-once ✅ Guaranteed with Pub/Sub source Depends on checkpointing and source connector
Cost model Per-TB scanned (on-demand) or per-slot-hour (editions) Per-vCPU-hour + shuffle bytes (worker time) Per-VM-hour (cluster) — idle cost unless Serverless
Data format Native Capacitor (columnar) — fastest Any (Avro, Parquet, JSON, CSV) Any (Parquet, ORC, Avro, Iceberg, Delta Lake)
Latency Seconds (query) — interactive analytics Milliseconds to seconds (streaming) Seconds to minutes (batch); seconds (structured streaming)
Open format support BigLake (Iceberg, Delta, Hudi read); limited DML Any format via Beam I/O connectors Full Iceberg/Delta/Hudi DML (read + write + compact)

17.2 Decision Framework — When to Use What

Use the following decision tree for every data processing task:

ScenarioBest EngineReasoning
SQL-expressible transformation on data in/to BigQueryBigQuery + DataformSimplest, cheapest, zero infrastructure. Always the first choice.
Simple event streaming to BigQuery (no transformation)Pub/Sub BigQuery SubscriptionZero code, zero infrastructure — even simpler than Dataflow.
Streaming with windowing, enrichment, joins, stateful logicDataflowFull Beam model, exactly-once, Streaming Engine, autoscaling.
Batch ETL on GCS files (Parquet → transform → GCS/BQ)Dataflow or Dataproc ServerlessDataflow: new pipelines. Dataproc: existing Spark code.
ML training (Spark MLlib, XGBoost, feature engineering)DataprocFull Spark MLlib ecosystem, GPU support, notebook integration.
Iceberg/Delta Lake table writes (insert, update, compact)DataprocSpark has full Iceberg DML. BigQuery has read-only BigLake access.
Hadoop migration (existing MapReduce, Hive, Pig jobs)DataprocDrop-in replacement for on-prem Hadoop — same APIs, managed infra.
Interactive SQL on GCS data (analyst exploration)BigQuery + BigLakeBigQuery SQL on external GCS data — no data movement needed.
ML inference in streaming pipelineDataflow + GPUEmbed TensorFlow/PyTorch model in Beam pipeline — real-time scoring.
Complex Python ETL (pandas, custom libraries)Dataproc ServerlessSubmit PySpark job with custom dependencies — serverless execution.
🏗️ Architecture Pattern — The Default GCP Data Engineering Stack The battle-tested GCP data engineering pattern is: BigQuery for everything SQL-expressible (ELT via Dataform or dbt). Use Dataflow only when you need true streaming or Beam-specific features (exactly-once, stateful processing, side inputs). Use Dataproc only when you need Spark (ML, complex Python, Hadoop migration, Iceberg). Most GCP data platforms use 80% BigQuery SQL, 15% Dataflow, 5% Dataproc.

17.3 Cost Comparison — Worked Example

Consider a daily ETL job that processes 500GB of data from GCS, applies transformations, and writes results to BigQuery.

EngineConfigurationRun TimeEstimated Daily Cost
BigQueryLOAD → SQL transform → CREATE TABLE (on-demand)~3 min~$2.50 (500GB × $5/TB scanned)
Dataflow10 workers × n1-standard-4, batch, Shuffle Service~20 min~$4.50 (10×4 vCPU × 0.33h × $0.056 + shuffle)
Dataproc Cluster4 workers × n2-standard-8, ephemeral~15 min~$2.80 (4×$0.38/hr×0.25h + Dataproc surcharge)
Dataproc ServerlessAuto-sized PySpark batch~18 min~$3.20 (vCPU-hours + memory-hours)
Key Insight — Cost vs Flexibility Trade-Off BigQuery is cheapest for SQL-expressible transforms (pay per TB scanned). Dataproc is cheapest for complex Python/Spark ETL (Spot VMs + ephemeral). Dataflow is most expensive per-job but the cheapest streaming option (no idle clusters). The real cost difference is in engineering time: BigQuery SQL takes minutes to write, Dataflow/Dataproc pipelines take hours to days. Always choose the simplest tool that meets the requirement.
🎯 Interview Tip — The Decision Question "You need to process 10TB of daily logs: parse JSON, filter invalid records, aggregate by region, and write to BigQuery. Which engine?" — Answer: BigQuery. Load the JSON directly into BigQuery (LOAD DATA or external table), then run SQL transformations. No Dataflow, no Dataproc. The data is already landing in GCS → use BigQuery's native JSON/Parquet reader + SQL. Only escalate to Dataflow if you need streaming or stateful logic. Only use Dataproc if you need Spark-specific libraries. The interviewer wants to hear: "simplest tool first."

Section 17 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Default OrderBigQuery → Dataflow → DataprocAlways try BigQuery SQL first. Escalate only when SQL can't express the logic.
BigQuerySQL-first, zero-infra, cheapest for analyticsUse for all SQL transforms, dashboards, ad-hoc analysis, scheduled queries.
DataflowStreaming + exactly-once + BeamUse for streaming pipelines, complex enrichment, stateful windowing, GPU inference.
DataprocSpark/Hadoop ecosystemUse for MLlib, Iceberg writes, Hadoop migration, existing Spark codebases.
Serverless OptionsDataflow and Dataproc Serverless eliminate clustersDefault to serverless for all batch workloads. Use clusters only for streaming or custom config.
CostBQ (per-TB) < Dataproc (per-VM) < Dataflow (per-vCPU) for batchBiggest cost is engineering time — simpler tools = lower total cost of ownership.
Part VI — Section 18

Datastream — CDC from RDBMS

Real-time Change Data Capture from Cloud SQL, AlloyDB, Oracle, and MySQL — automatic replication to BigQuery and GCS with schema drift handling.

Section 18 — Datastream — CDC Architecture and Merge Patterns

18.1 Datastream Architecture

Cloud Datastream is a serverless Change Data Capture (CDC) service that replicates data from relational databases to GCP destinations in real-time. It reads database transaction logs (MySQL binlog, PostgreSQL WAL, Oracle LogMiner) and streams the changes — inserts, updates, deletes — to BigQuery or GCS, maintaining a near-real-time replica.

Datastream supports two modes: Initial snapshot (full backfill of existing table data — essential for new streams) followed by ongoing CDC (continuous log-based replication of changes). It handles schema drift automatically — when columns are added/removed/renamed in the source database, Datastream detects the change and propagates it to the destination.

Two destination types serve different patterns: BigQuery (direct) automatically applies MERGE operations to maintain an up-to-date replica table (INSERT new rows, UPDATE changed rows, DELETE removed rows). GCS writes raw CDC events as Avro or JSON files — useful when you need custom transformation before loading (e.g., via Dataflow or BigQuery SQL MERGE).

Figure 18.1 — Real-time database replication to BigQuery and GCS via Datastream CDC

18.2 Datastream to BigQuery — Direct MERGE Replication

The BigQuery direct destination is the simplest CDC path: Datastream reads the source DB's transaction log, performs initial backfill, and then continuously applies changes to BigQuery tables using automatic MERGE operations. Inserts become INSERTs, updates become UPDATEs, deletes become DELETEs — all handled by Datastream. You get a near-real-time BigQuery replica with zero pipeline code.

Datastream adds metadata columns to the BigQuery table: _metadata_timestamp (when the change occurred in the source DB), _metadata_read_timestamp (when Datastream read the change), _metadata_deleted (whether the row was deleted in the source), and _metadata_change_type (INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE). These columns are essential for auditing, debugging, and building SCD Type 2 tables.

Create Datastream stream — Cloud SQL PostgreSQL → BigQuery (direct CDC)BASH
# ── Step 1: Create a connection profile for the source ────────────────
gcloud datastream connection-profiles create my-pg-source \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --type=postgresql \
    --postgresql-hostname=10.0.0.5 \
    --postgresql-port=5432 \
    --postgresql-username=datastream_user \
    --postgresql-password="***" \
    --postgresql-database=production \
    --private-connection=my-vpc-peering    # Private connectivity — no public IP

# ── Step 2: Create a connection profile for the destination ───────────
gcloud datastream connection-profiles create my-bq-dest \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --type=bigquery

# ── Step 3: Create the stream (source → destination) ─────────────────
gcloud datastream streams create my-pg-to-bq-stream \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --display-name="PostgreSQL Production → BigQuery CDC" \
    --source=my-pg-source \
    --postgresql-source-config='{
      "includeObjects": {
        "postgresqlSchemas": [{
          "schema": "public",
          "postgresqlTables": [
            {"table": "orders"},
            {"table": "customers"},
            {"table": "products"}
          ]
        }]
      },
      "replicationSlot": "datastream_slot",
      "publication": "datastream_pub"
    }' \
    --destination=my-bq-dest \
    --bigquery-destination-config='{
      "singleTargetDataset": {
        "datasetId": "projects/my-project/datasets/cdc_replica"
      },
      "merge": {}
    }' \
    --backfill-all                # Initial snapshot of existing data

# Datastream now:
# 1. Backfills all existing rows from orders, customers, products
# 2. Reads PostgreSQL WAL for ongoing changes
# 3. Applies INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE to BigQuery tables automatically
# 4. Handles schema changes (new columns, type changes) automatically

# ── Monitor stream freshness ──────────────────────────────────────────
gcloud datastream streams describe my-pg-to-bq-stream \
    --location=us-central1 \
    --format="table(name,state,errors)"
Pitfall — PostgreSQL Replication Slot WAL Retention Datastream uses a replication slot in PostgreSQL. If the stream is paused or falls behind, the replication slot prevents PostgreSQL from recycling WAL segments — causing disk growth until the database runs out of space. Monitor replication slot lag in Cloud SQL metrics. If you pause a Datastream stream for more than a few hours, drop the replication slot and re-backfill to avoid WAL accumulation.

18.3 Merge Pattern in BigQuery — From GCS-Staged CDC Events

When Datastream writes to GCS (instead of direct BigQuery), you receive raw CDC events as Avro or JSON files. You then write a MERGE statement to apply these changes to a BigQuery target table. This pattern is useful when you need custom transformation, business logic, or SCD Type 2 history before merging.

The MERGE pattern handles all three CDC operation types: INSERT (new rows), UPDATE (changed rows), and DELETE (removed rows, identified by the _metadata_deleted flag). The staging table is an external table or a BigQuery table loaded from the GCS CDC files.

BigQuery MERGE — apply CDC events from Datastream staging tableSQL
-- ── Load CDC events from GCS into a staging table ────────────────────
-- (or create an external table pointing to the GCS path)
LOAD DATA OVERWRITE `project.dataset.orders_cdc_staging`
FROM FILES (
  format = 'AVRO',
  uris   = ['gs://bucket/datastream/public/orders/*.avro']
);

-- ── MERGE: Apply INSERT / UPDATE / DELETE to the target table ────────
MERGE INTO `project.dataset.orders` AS target
USING (
  -- Deduplicate: keep only the latest change per order_id
  SELECT * EXCEPT(rn) FROM (
    SELECT *,
      ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
        PARTITION BY order_id
        ORDER BY _metadata_timestamp DESC
      ) AS rn
    FROM `project.dataset.orders_cdc_staging`
  ) WHERE rn = 1
) AS source
ON target.order_id = source.order_id

-- DELETE: Source marks row as deleted
WHEN MATCHED AND source._metadata_deleted = TRUE THEN DELETE

-- UPDATE: Source has newer version of existing row
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET
    target.customer_id = source.customer_id,
    target.status      = source.status,
    target.amount      = source.amount,
    target.updated_at  = source.updated_at

-- INSERT: New row not in target (and not a delete event)
WHEN NOT MATCHED AND source._metadata_deleted = FALSE THEN INSERT
    (order_id, customer_id, status, amount, created_at, updated_at)
    VALUES (source.order_id, source.customer_id, source.status,
            source.amount, source.created_at, source.updated_at);

-- ── Schedule this MERGE in Dataform or Cloud Composer (every 5 min) ──
-- Pattern: Datastream → GCS (raw CDC) → MERGE SQL → BigQuery (clean)

18.4 Datastream Features and Limitations

Datastream is the simplest CDC tool on GCP, but it has trade-offs compared to alternatives like Debezium or Striim.

FeatureDatastreamDebezium (self-managed on GKE)
Managed✅ Fully serverless❌ Self-managed (Kafka Connect + ZK/KRaft)
SourcesMySQL, PostgreSQL, Oracle, AlloyDB, SQL ServerMySQL, PostgreSQL, Oracle, MongoDB, SQL Server, DB2, Vitess, etc.
DestinationsBigQuery, GCS onlyKafka → any downstream (BQ, GCS, Elasticsearch, S3, etc.)
TransformationNone — raw replication onlySMTs (Single Message Transforms) in Kafka Connect
Schema drift✅ Automatic propagationConfigurable but manual setup
LatencySeconds to low minutesSub-second (with Kafka)
CostPer-GB processed ($0.10/GB)GKE cluster cost + Kafka cost
RecommendationGCP-native, simple CDC, BQ destinationMulti-destination, sub-second latency, transformations needed
🎯 Interview Tip — CDC to BigQuery "How would you replicate a production PostgreSQL database to BigQuery in real-time?" — Use Datastream with BigQuery direct destination. It reads PostgreSQL WAL via logical replication, performs initial backfill, and applies ongoing changes via automatic MERGE. No Dataflow, no Kafka, no custom code. Set up private connectivity (VPC peering) for security. Monitor staleness metrics — alert if replication lag exceeds 5 minutes. For tables needing SCD Type 2 history, use the GCS destination path with a custom MERGE that inserts historical records instead of overwriting.

Section 18 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
DatastreamServerless CDC — reads DB transaction logs, writes to BQ/GCSUse BigQuery direct destination for simple replication. Use GCS when you need custom MERGE logic.
Initial Snapshot + CDCFull backfill first, then ongoing log-based replicationAlways use --backfill-all for new streams. Monitor backfill progress in Datastream UI.
Auto-MERGEBigQuery direct applies INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE automaticallyDefault choice — zero code. Produces a near-real-time replica of the source table.
GCS + Custom MERGERaw CDC to GCS → custom SQL MERGE to BigQueryUse for SCD Type 2, complex business logic, or multi-table joins before loading.
Schema DriftDatastream auto-propagates schema changesNew columns appear automatically. Test with staging streams before production.
Replication Slot RiskPaused streams cause WAL accumulation in PostgreSQLMonitor replication slot lag. Drop slot and re-backfill if stream paused >4 hours.
Staleness MonitoringTrack how far behind the replica is from sourceAlert on >5min staleness. Use Cloud Monitoring + Datastream's built-in freshness metrics.
Part VI — Section 19

Real-Time Ingestion Patterns

Three battle-tested streaming architectures — choose based on latency requirement, transformation complexity, and cost tolerance.

Section 19 — Real-Time Ingestion Patterns — Pub/Sub → BigQuery

19.1 The Three Streaming Ingestion Patterns

Every real-time data ingestion problem on GCP maps to one of three patterns. The choice is driven by three levers: latency requirement (sub-second vs minutes), transformation complexity (none vs enrichment/windowing vs aggregation), and cost tolerance (BigQuery streaming is expensive at scale; GCS-staged micro-batch is cheapest). Start with Pattern 1 (simplest), escalate to Pattern 2 (full Beam) only when transformation is needed, and use Pattern 3 when cost is the primary constraint.

Figure 19.1 — Choose based on latency requirement and complexity tolerance: Pattern 1 for simplicity, Pattern 2 for power, Pattern 3 for cost

19.2 Pattern 1 — Pub/Sub → BigQuery Direct Subscription

The simplest streaming path on GCP. Create a BigQuery subscription on a Pub/Sub topic — messages are automatically written to a BigQuery table with no pipeline code, no Dataflow workers, no infrastructure. Pub/Sub handles the write using the Storage Write API internally. Schema is inferred from the topic's registered Avro/Protobuf schema or you can use the raw message column.

Limitations: no transformation (messages land exactly as published), no deduplication (at-least-once delivery — duplicates possible), and BigQuery streaming write costs apply ($0.01 per 200MB). Use when: messages are already in the final shape, no enrichment is needed, and you want the fastest possible time-to-analytics.

19.3 Pattern 2 — Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BigQuery

The full-power streaming path. A Dataflow (Apache Beam) pipeline reads from Pub/Sub, applies transformations (parse, validate, enrich from side inputs, aggregate with windowing), and writes to BigQuery using the Storage Write API (faster and cheaper than the legacy streaming insert API). This is the only pattern that provides exactly-once semantics end-to-end.

Use when you need: windowed aggregations (5-min revenue by country), enrichment joins (device ID → user profile), complex validation/filtering, dead-letter routing for bad records, or guaranteed exactly-once delivery for financial data.

Pattern 2 — Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BigQuery Storage Write APIPython
import apache_beam as beam
from apache_beam.options.pipeline_options import PipelineOptions
import json, logging

def parse_and_validate(msg):
    try:
        record = json.loads(msg)
        assert record.get('user_id') and record.get('event_type')
        return record
    except Exception as e:
        logging.warning(f'Bad record: {msg} — {e}')
        return None

options = PipelineOptions([
    '--runner=DataflowRunner', '--project=my-project',
    '--region=us-central1', '--temp_location=gs://bucket/temp',
    '--streaming', '--enable_streaming_engine',
    '--experiments=use_runner_v2'
])

with beam.Pipeline(options=options) as p:
    records = (
        p
        | 'Read'     >> beam.io.ReadFromPubSub(subscription='projects/my-project/subscriptions/events-df')
        | 'Decode'   >> beam.Map(lambda x: x.decode('utf-8'))
        | 'Parse'    >> beam.Map(parse_and_validate)
        | 'Filter'   >> beam.Filter(lambda x: x is not None)
    )
    # Write to BigQuery using Storage Write API (faster, cheaper than streaming inserts)
    records | 'WriteBQ' >> beam.io.WriteToBigQuery(
        'my-project:analytics.events',
        method=beam.io.WriteToBigQuery.Method.STORAGE_WRITE_API,  # ← key
        write_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.WRITE_APPEND,
        create_disposition=beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.CREATE_IF_NEEDED
    )

19.4 Pattern 3 — Pub/Sub → Dataflow → GCS → BigQuery (Micro-Batch)

The cost-optimized streaming path. Instead of writing to BigQuery continuously, Dataflow buffers messages into fixed windows (5–15 minutes) and writes Parquet files to GCS. A scheduled BigQuery LOAD job (or BigQuery Transfer Service) then loads these files. This eliminates BigQuery streaming costs, produces well-sized Parquet files for efficient storage, and uses the much cheaper LOAD pricing ($0 for loading from GCS).

The trade-off: latency increases to 5–15 minutes (window + load time). This is acceptable for most analytics use cases — dashboards, reports, and aggregated metrics rarely need sub-second freshness. Use this pattern when data volume is high (>10GB/day) and sub-minute latency is not a hard requirement.

DimensionPattern 1 (Direct Sub)Pattern 2 (Dataflow → BQ)Pattern 3 (Micro-Batch)
LatencySecondsSub-second5–15 minutes
TransformationNoneFull (Beam)Limited (windowed write)
Exactly-onceAt-least-once✅ GuaranteedAt-least-once (dedup needed)
BQ write costStreaming ($0.01/200MB)Storage Write API (cheaper)✅ Free (LOAD from GCS)
InfrastructureNoneDataflow workersDataflow + GCS + scheduler
Best forSimple event loggingComplex streaming ETLCost-sensitive, near-RT OK
🎯 Interview Tip — Which Pattern? "How would you stream 50 million events/day from a mobile app to BigQuery?" — Start with Pattern 1 if messages need no transformation (add schema to Pub/Sub topic → BigQuery subscription). If you need enrichment or windowed aggregations, use Pattern 2 with Dataflow Storage Write API. If cost is critical and 15-min freshness is acceptable, use Pattern 3 — at 50M events/day (~5GB), streaming inserts cost ~$7.50/day vs $0 for LOAD. Mention that you'd add a dead-letter topic in all three patterns to capture failed/malformed messages.

Section 19 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Pattern 1Zero-code direct BQ subscriptionDefault starting point. Add schema to topic, enable --write-metadata. Escalate if transformation needed.
Pattern 2Full Beam power — exactly-once, windowingUse STORAGE_WRITE_API method (not legacy streaming inserts) — faster and cheaper.
Pattern 3Micro-batch to GCS → free LOAD to BQUse when volume >10GB/day and latency >5min is acceptable. Eliminates streaming write cost.
Dead-letter topicsAlways route failed messages to DLTAdd DLT to all three patterns. Alert on DLT message count as pipeline health signal.
Storage Write APIFaster + cheaper BQ write than legacy streaming insertsAlways use Method.STORAGE_WRITE_API in Dataflow → BQ pipelines. Supports exactly-once mode.
Part VII — Section 20

Dataform — SQL Transformation in BigQuery

Google's native SQL transformation tool for BigQuery — SQLX models, incremental tables, assertions, dependency graphs, and Git-based CI/CD.

Section 20 — Dataform — SQL Transformation in BigQuery (SQLX, Incremental)

20.1 Dataform Overview

Dataform is Google's native SQL transformation tool built directly into the GCP console, purpose-built for BigQuery. It uses SQLX — SQL augmented with a JavaScript-based configuration block — to define views, tables, incremental tables, and assertions (data quality tests). Dataform manages dependency resolution automatically: define ${ref("table_name")} in your SQLX and Dataform builds the correct execution order, ensuring upstream tables are materialized before downstream models run.

Core concepts: Models are SQLX files that define how a BigQuery object (view, table, incremental table) is created. Assertions are SQL queries that should return zero rows — if any rows are returned, the pipeline fails. Operations are arbitrary SQL statements (GRANT permissions, DELETE stale partitions). Tags group related models so you can run a subset of the pipeline (e.g., only --tags=daily). Compilation results are immutable snapshots of a workflow — pinned to a Git commit — enabling reproducible deployments.

Dataform vs dbt — Quick Summary Dataform is free, native to GCP console, and BigQuery-only. dbt Core is free and open-source with a huge community and multi-warehouse support. Choose Dataform for BigQuery-only shops that want zero extra tooling. Choose dbt if your team has existing dbt expertise, needs multi-warehouse support (Snowflake + BigQuery), or relies on the dbt package ecosystem.

20.2 Dataform Model Examples — View, Table, Incremental

definitions/staging/stg_stripe_payments.sqlx — Staging ViewSQLX
-- Staging view: clean and rename raw Stripe data
-- type: "view" → runs as BigQuery VIEW (no storage cost)
config {
    type: "view",
    schema: "staging",
    description: "Cleaned Stripe payments — amount converted from cents to dollars",
    tags: ["staging", "stripe"]
}

SELECT
    id                                                          AS payment_id,
    CAST(amount AS NUMERIC) / 100                           AS amount_dollars,
    currency,
    PARSE_TIMESTAMP('%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S', created)          AS created_at,
    customer                                                    AS customer_id,
    status,
    COALESCE(metadata_country, 'unknown')                    AS country
FROM ${ref("raw_stripe_payments")}   -- ref() creates a dependency + correct dataset prefix
WHERE status != 'failed'
  AND amount > 0
definitions/marts/fct_orders.sqlx — Incremental Fact TableSQLX
-- Incremental model: only process new rows on each run
-- bigquery config adds partitioning + clustering for performance
config {
    type: "incremental",
    schema: "marts",
    description: "Daily order fact table — one row per payment",
    uniqueKey: ["order_key"],          // MERGE key — prevents duplicates on re-runs
    bigquery: {
        partitionBy: "order_date",
        clusterBy: ["customer_key", "country"]
    },
    tags: ["marts", "daily"]
}

SELECT
    ${generate_surrogate_key(["payment_id"])}  AS order_key,
    p.created_at::DATE                               AS order_date,
    c.customer_key,
    c.country_name,
    p.country,
    p.currency,
    p.amount_dollars                                 AS revenue
FROM ${ref("stg_stripe_payments")} p
LEFT JOIN ${ref("dim_customers")} c ON p.customer_id = c.customer_id

-- when(incremental()): only process rows newer than what's already in the table
${when(incremental(),
    `WHERE p.created_at > (SELECT MAX(order_date) FROM ${self()})`
)}

20.3 Assertions — Data Quality Tests

Assertions are SQL queries that must return zero rows. If any rows are returned, the assertion fails and Dataform stops the workflow (or marks it failed, depending on your failure policy). This is Dataform's built-in data quality testing layer — equivalent to dbt's tests. Assertions run after the model they reference is materialized.

definitions/assertions/ — Data quality testsSQLX
-- ── assert_positive_revenue.sqlx — no negative revenue ──────────────
config { type: "assertion", tags: ["quality"] }

SELECT * FROM ${ref("fct_orders")}
WHERE revenue < 0
-- Returns rows → assertion FAILS → Dataform marks workflow as failed

-- ── assert_no_null_keys.sqlx — no null primary keys ─────────────────
config { type: "assertion", tags: ["quality"] }

SELECT * FROM ${ref("fct_orders")}
WHERE order_key IS NULL OR customer_key IS NULL

-- ── assert_row_count.sqlx — table must have data for today ───────────
config { type: "assertion", tags: ["freshness"] }

SELECT 'missing_data' AS issue
FROM (SELECT COUNT(*) AS cnt FROM ${ref("fct_orders")}
      WHERE order_date = CURRENT_DATE())
WHERE cnt = 0

20.4 Dataform vs dbt on GCP

FactorDataformdbt (Cloud or Core)
IntegrationNative GCP console — no extra toolingThird-party — dbt Cloud (SaaS) or dbt Core (CLI)
LanguageSQLX — SQL + JavaScript config blocksSQL + Jinja templating

Section 20 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
SQLX ModelsSQL + config block = view, table, or incrementalUse type: "view" for staging, type: "incremental" for fact tables. Always set uniqueKey on incrementals.
ref()Creates dependency + correct dataset prefix automaticallyAlways use ${ref("table")} instead of hardcoded dataset.table names. Enables correct execution order.
Incremental patternProcess only new rows — use when(incremental())Filter by MAX(date) in the target table. Add uniqueKey to prevent duplicates on re-runs.
AssertionsSQL that must return 0 rows — automated quality checksWrite assertions for: not-null keys, positive metrics, referential integrity, freshness (today's data exists).
Dataform vs dbtDataform = GCP-native free; dbt = ecosystem + multi-warehouseNew GCP-only project → Dataform. Existing dbt expertise or multi-cloud → dbt Core.
