Part VII — Section 21

Cloud Composer — Managed Airflow

GKE-based managed Apache Airflow — DAG design patterns, BigQuery ELT orchestration, Composer 2/3 architecture, and production best practices.

Section 21 — Cloud Composer — Managed Airflow: DAG Design, Composer 2/3

21.1 Cloud Composer 2/3 Architecture

Cloud Composer is Google's fully managed Apache Airflow service. Composer 2 (and 3) runs Airflow on a GKE Autopilot cluster in Google's managed project — dramatically reducing operational overhead compared to Composer 1 (which ran on a user-managed GKE cluster). DAGs are stored in a GCS bucket; the Airflow scheduler watches that bucket and parses DAG files continuously. Workers use the Kubernetes Executor (Composer 2/3) — each task runs in its own isolated pod, eliminating worker slot contention and enabling per-task resource allocation.

Key Composer 2/3 improvements over Composer 1: workload-based autoscaling (scheduler, worker, and triggerer scale independently), smaller baseline cost (GKE Autopilot charges only for running pods), deferrable operators (tasks waiting on external events yield their worker slot to a lightweight Triggerer component — critical for pipelines with many sensors), and environment versioning (upgrade Airflow version without recreating the environment).

Figure 21.1 — Cloud Composer 2 runs Airflow on GKE Autopilot: each task is an isolated pod, deferrable operators free worker slots, DAGs sync from GCS

21.2 DAG Example — Daily BigQuery ELT Pipeline

The following DAG implements a production-grade daily ELT pipeline: load raw data from GCS into BigQuery, run Dataform transformations, and send a Slack notification on completion (or failure). It demonstrates key Airflow patterns: template variables ({{ ds }}, {{ ds_nodash }}), task chaining (>>), and idempotent design (WRITE_TRUNCATE on the raw load prevents double-loading on retry).

dags/daily_elt_pipeline.py — Production ELT DAGPython
from airflow import DAG
from airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.bigquery import BigQueryInsertJobOperator
from airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.dataform import (
    DataformCreateCompilationResultOperator,
    DataformCreateWorkflowInvocationOperator
)
from airflow.operators.empty import EmptyOperator
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

default_args = {
    'owner':            'data-engineering',
    'retries':          2,
    'retry_delay':      timedelta(minutes=5),
    'on_failure_callback': send_slack_alert,   # defined elsewhere
}

with DAG(
    dag_id='daily_elt_pipeline',
    schedule_interval='@daily',
    start_date=datetime(2024, 1, 1),
    catchup=False,       # don't backfill historical runs on first deploy
    default_args=default_args,
    tags=['elt', 'production']
) as dag:

    start = EmptyOperator(task_id='start')

    # ── Step 1: Load raw data from GCS to BigQuery (idempotent) ──────────
    load_raw = BigQueryInsertJobOperator(
        task_id='load_raw_data',
        configuration={
            'load': {
                'sourceUris': ['gs://bucket/raw/{{ ds }}/*.parquet'],
                'destinationTable': {
                    'projectId': 'my-project',
                    'datasetId': 'raw',
                    'tableId':   'events${{ ds_nodash }}'   # partition decorator
                },
                'sourceFormat':      'PARQUET',
                'writeDisposition':  'WRITE_TRUNCATE'   # idempotent — safe to retry
            }
        }
    )

    # ── Step 2: Compile Dataform workflow (pinned to Git main branch) ────
    compile_df = DataformCreateCompilationResultOperator(
        task_id='compile_dataform',
        project_id='my-project',
        region='us-central1',
        repository_id='my-dataform-repo',
        compilation_result={'git_commitish': 'main'}
    )

    # ── Step 3: Run Dataform transformations (staging → marts) ───────────
    run_df = DataformCreateWorkflowInvocationOperator(
        task_id='run_dataform',
        project_id='my-project',
        region='us-central1',
        repository_id='my-dataform-repo',
        workflow_invocation={
            'compilation_result': '{{ task_instance.xcom_pull("compile_dataform") }}',
            'invocation_config': {
                'included_tags': ['daily']   # only run models tagged 'daily'
            }
        }
    )

    end = EmptyOperator(task_id='end')

    # ── Dependency chain ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
    start >> load_raw >> compile_df >> run_df >> end

21.3 Cloud Composer Best Practices

PracticeWhyHow
Always use Composer 2/3GKE Autopilot — per-pod billing, better autoscaling, deferrable operatorsMigrate Composer 1 environments. Use --environment-size=small initially.
Deferrable operatorsFree worker slots while waiting on external jobs (BQ, Dataflow, GCS)Use BigQueryInsertJobOperator(deferrable=True). Requires Triggerer component.
Idempotent DAGsSafe retries — re-running a DAG for the same date produces the same resultUse WRITE_TRUNCATE for LOAD jobs. Use CREATE OR REPLACE TABLE for BQ transforms.
catchup=FalsePrevents thousands of backfill runs on first deployment of a scheduled DAGSet catchup=False on all production DAGs. Use separate backfill DAGs when needed.
CI/CD for DAGsVersion-controlled, peer-reviewed, tested DAG deploymentsStore DAGs in Git → Cloud Build trigger on merge → gsutil rsync to Composer's GCS bucket.
KubernetesPodOperator for heavy tasksRun arbitrary Docker containers — custom Python environments, Spark local, ML libsUse KubernetesPodOperator when task needs libraries not in the Composer environment.
Environment sizingUndersized scheduler → DAG parsing backlog; oversized → wasted costStart small. Monitor scheduler.tasks_pending metric. Scale up only when queue builds.

Section 21 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Composer 2/3GKE Autopilot — per-task pods, deferrable ops, lower costAlways use Composer 2/3. One pod per task = isolation + per-task resource allocation.
TriggererAsync component for sensor tasks — frees worker podsUse deferrable=True on BQ, Dataflow, GCS operators. Reduces worker node count.
DAG DesignIdempotent, minimal Python, catchup=FalseNo business logic in DAGs. DAGs = orchestration only. Business logic goes in Dataform/Dataflow.
CI/CDDAGs in Git → build → sync to GCSUse Cloud Build + gsutil rsync. Never manually upload DAG files in production.
Composer vs WorkflowsComposer for complex multi-step DAGs; Cloud Workflows for simple step-based automationUse Cloud Workflows for simple HTTP/API chains (<10 steps). Use Composer for DAGs with retry logic, sensors, and complex dependencies.
Part VII — Section 22

Data Fusion — Visual ETL/ELT

No-code ETL for teams without Beam/Spark expertise — CDAP-based pipelines, pre-built connectors, when to use and when to avoid.

Section 22 — Data Fusion — Visual ETL: CDAP, Wrangler, When to Use

22.1 What is Data Fusion?

Cloud Data Fusion is a fully managed, code-free ETL/ELT service built on CDAP (Cask Data Application Platform — open-source). Pipelines are built visually by dragging and dropping sources, transformations, and sinks onto a canvas. Under the hood, Data Fusion compiles visual pipelines into Apache Spark or MapReduce jobs that run on a Dataproc ephemeral cluster — so it's Spark, just abstracted behind a GUI.

When to use Data Fusion: teams with ETL requirements but no Beam/Spark/SQL engineering capacity, migrations from on-prem ETL tools (Informatica, Talend, SSIS), or workloads requiring pre-built connectors to enterprise systems (SAP, Salesforce, Oracle EBS, ServiceNow) that would take weeks to build manually. Data Fusion has 150+ pre-built connectors and a Wrangler UI for interactive data transformation without coding.

When NOT to use Data Fusion: high-throughput streaming (use Dataflow), SQL-expressible transforms (use Dataform/dbt — free and simpler), Spark ML pipelines (use Dataproc directly), or any workload where an engineering team can write the code — Data Fusion adds cost and complexity that code-based solutions avoid.

FactorData FusionDataform / dbtDataflow
InterfaceVisual drag-and-drop canvasSQL / SQLX code editorJava/Python/Go code
Execution engineSpark (via Dataproc ephemeral cluster)BigQuery native SQL engineApache Beam on GCE workers
StreamingLimited (Spark Streaming)❌ Batch only✅ First-class streaming
Pre-built connectors✅ 150+ (SAP, Salesforce, Oracle)Custom SQL / external tablesBeam I/O connectors
Idle cost$2–5/hour per instance (always on)$0 (serverless)$0 when no jobs running
Versioning / CI/CDPipeline JSON export to GitNative Git integrationFlex Templates in Docker
Best forNon-engineering teams, enterprise connectorsSQL-first data teamsStreaming + complex ETL
Avoid whenSQL suffices, high throughput neededNon-SQL logic, streamingSimple SQL transforms
💰 Cost Tip — Data Fusion Instance Always-On Cost Data Fusion instances have a baseline cost regardless of whether pipelines are running: Developer edition ~$2/hr, Basic ~$3/hr, Enterprise ~$5/hr. This adds up to $1,400–$3,600/month for an idle environment. Strategies to control cost: (1) use a shared Developer instance across multiple teams, (2) schedule instance start/stop via the Admin API if usage is limited to business hours, (3) prefer Dataform/dbt (free) for SQL transformations and only use Data Fusion for workloads that genuinely require its connectors or visual interface.

Section 22 — Key Takeaways

ConceptCore IdeaPractical Action
Data Fusion = Visual SparkGUI compiles to Spark on Dataproc under the hoodFor teams that need ETL without code. Engineers should prefer Dataform/Dataflow directly.
Pre-built connectors150+ connectors for SAP, Salesforce, Oracle, etc.Use Data Fusion when connector development cost > Data Fusion instance cost.
Always-on cost$2–5/hr regardless of pipeline activityUse shared instances. Don't create separate instances per team. Consider scheduling instance downtime.
Not for streamingLimited streaming — use Dataflow insteadAny real-time requirement → Dataflow. Data Fusion = batch ETL only.
Not for SQL transformsIf it can be done in BigQuery SQL, do it thereDataform is free, faster, and versioned. Don't use Data Fusion for simple SQL-expressible transforms.
Part VIII — Section 23

Cloud SQL and AlloyDB

Managed OLTP databases on GCP — Cloud SQL for standard relational workloads, AlloyDB for high-performance PostgreSQL with built-in columnar engine and Vertex AI integration.

Section 23 — Cloud SQL & AlloyDB — Managed OLTP, HA, Columnar Engine

23.1 Cloud SQL — Fully Managed Relational Database

Cloud SQL is Google's fully managed relational database service, supporting PostgreSQL, MySQL, and SQL Server. It handles routine database tasks: provisioning, patch management, backups, and replication. Cloud SQL is the standard choice for web application backends, microservice databases, and operational databases that need standard PostgreSQL/MySQL compatibility without the operational overhead of running your own database.

Key capabilities: High Availability (HA) — synchronous replication to a standby in the same region with automatic failover (typically <60 seconds); Read Replicas — asynchronous replicas for read scaling, available cross-region for disaster recovery; Point-in-time recovery (PITR) — restore to any second within the retention window (up to 35 days); Private IP via VPC — no public internet exposure. Limitations: maximum 96 vCPUs and 624 GB RAM — not suitable for very large OLTP workloads (use AlloyDB or Spanner for those).

Cloud SQL — create HA PostgreSQL instance with read replicaBASH
# ── Create HA PostgreSQL instance (primary + standby in same region) ──
gcloud sql instances create prod-postgres \
    --database-version=POSTGRES_16 \
    --tier=db-n1-standard-8 \           # 8 vCPU, 30 GB RAM
    --region=us-central1 \
    --availability-type=REGIONAL \      # REGIONAL = HA with automatic failover
    --storage-type=SSD \
    --storage-size=500GB \
    --storage-auto-increase \
    --no-assign-ip \                    # Private IP only — no public internet
    --network=projects/my-project/global/networks/my-vpc \
    --backup-start-time=02:00 \
    --enable-point-in-time-recovery \
    --retained-backups-count=14 \
    --retained-transaction-log-days=7   # PITR window: 7 days

# ── Create a cross-region read replica ───────────────────────────────
gcloud sql instances create prod-postgres-replica-eu \
    --master-instance-name=prod-postgres \
    --region=europe-west1 \
    --availability-type=ZONAL \
    --tier=db-n1-standard-4

# ── Connect via Cloud SQL Auth Proxy (recommended for secure access) ──
# cloud-sql-proxy my-project:us-central1:prod-postgres --port 5432
# Then connect: psql -h 127.0.0.1 -U postgres -d mydb

23.2 AlloyDB — High-Performance PostgreSQL with Columnar Engine

AlloyDB is Google's fully managed, PostgreSQL-compatible database engineered for demanding workloads. Under 100% PostgreSQL wire-compatibility, AlloyDB replaces PostgreSQL's storage layer with a Google-designed distributed storage system that decouples read and write paths, achieving 4× higher write throughput and 2× better read throughput than standard Cloud SQL PostgreSQL.

AlloyDB's killer feature for data engineering is the columnar engine: an in-memory columnar cache (separate from the row buffer pool) that automatically identifies hot analytical columns and caches them in columnar format. This gives AlloyDB 100× faster analytical query performance on OLTP data — enabling real-time dashboards and reports directly on the operational database without ETL to BigQuery. The columnar engine is transparent — no schema changes, no query rewrites; the query planner automatically routes analytical queries to the columnar engine.

AlloyDB also integrates with Vertex AI via the alloydb_ai_ml.predict() function — call any Vertex AI model directly from SQL for real-time ML inference on OLTP data.

AlloyDB — columnar engine + Vertex AI inference in SQLSQL
-- ── Enable the columnar engine ────────────────────────────────────────
-- (set at instance level in AlloyDB cluster config)
-- google_columnar_engine.enabled = 'on'
-- google_columnar_engine.memory_size_in_mb = 8192  -- 8 GB columnar cache

-- ── Analytical query — automatically served from columnar engine ──────
-- No query changes needed — planner detects it's analytical and routes to columnar cache
SELECT
    product_category,
    SUM(revenue)                     AS total_revenue,
    AVG(order_value)                  AS avg_order,
    COUNT(DISTINCT customer_id)       AS unique_customers
FROM orders
WHERE created_at >= NOW() - INTERVAL '30 days'
GROUP BY product_category
ORDER BY total_revenue DESC;
-- On standard PostgreSQL: ~10 seconds on 100M row table
-- On AlloyDB with columnar engine: ~100ms (100x faster)

-- ── Vertex AI integration — real-time ML inference in SQL ────────────
-- Call a Vertex AI model to predict churn probability for each customer
SELECT
    customer_id,
    days_since_last_order,
    total_spend,
    (alloydb_ai_ml.predict(
        model_id => 'projects/my-project/locations/us-central1/models/churn-model',
        instance => json_build_object(
            'days_inactive',     days_since_last_order,
            'lifetime_value',    total_spend,
            'order_count',       order_count
        )
    ) ->> 'churn_probability')::FLOAT   AS churn_probability
FROM customer_features
WHERE last_order_date < NOW() - INTERVAL '14 days';
-- Result: real-time churn scores for all at-risk customers — no batch ETL needed

23.3 Cloud SQL vs AlloyDB — When to Choose What

FactorCloud SQL (PostgreSQL)AlloyDB
Analytical queriesStandard PostgreSQL performance✅ Up to 100× faster (columnar engine cache)
Write throughputStandard OLTP✅ 4× higher (distributed write log)
Read throughputStandard + read replicas✅ 2× higher + read pool instances
HA / DRRegional HA (same-region standby)✅ Regional HA + cross-region replication (GA)
PostgreSQL compatibility100% PostgreSQL100% PostgreSQL (wire-compatible)
AI/ML integrationpgvector (vectors), basic✅ alloydb_ai_ml.predict() — Vertex AI in SQL
Max size96 vCPU, 624 GB RAM128 vCPU, 864 GB RAM (larger instances)
CostLower (~$100–500/mo for standard)Higher (~2× Cloud SQL for equivalent config)
RecommendationStandard OLTP, cost-sensitive, simple workloadsHigh-performance OLTP + analytics hybrid, ML inference
🎯 Interview Tip — Cloud SQL vs AlloyDB vs Spanner "What managed database would you use for a high-traffic e-commerce backend on GCP?" — Cloud SQL PostgreSQL for standard OLTP with <100K QPS. AlloyDB when you need the same PostgreSQL API but with 4× better write throughput, real-time analytics on OLTP data (columnar engine), or in-database ML inference. Spanner when you need global distribution, unlimited horizontal scale, or strong consistency across regions (e.g., financial ledgers, global inventory). AlloyDB is often the "Cloud SQL but faster" upgrade — same code, same drivers, 2–4× the performance at ~2× the cost.

Section 23 — Key Takeaways
